“Every man can write at least one book,”every
man with brains lias at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word ; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better Don’t write a.
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don't write half-a-columri where a mere paragraph
is enough. '* Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly-re-
ceived b'- The Bulletin.

Literary Notes.
A NEW MAGAZINE.
Afc its second or third number The Yellow
Book came to the parting'of the ways between
modified Bohemian ism and Boheiniaiiism utter.
The publisher voted against realism, Baudelair-
ism, and “ degeneracy,” end his magazine became
'Comparatively Philistinish and respectable. Now,
the English apostles of the Very Unconventional
who may be typified with more or less accuracy
as Aubrey Beardsley di art, and Bernard Shaw in
literature—have evolved a quarterly magazine of
their own—The Savoy—which professes in a not
too modest foreword to ask and offer nothing but
“good work.”
For us all art is good which is good art. We hope to
appeal to the task a of the intelligent by not being
original for originality’s sake, or audacious for the sake
of advertisement, or timid for the convenience of the
elderly-minded.
And so on.
Unluckily the prefatory promise is hardly re-
deemed by performances. The first number of
the Savoy is smart and shallow, and, on the
whole, weak and disappointing. The posirg, and
the mannerisms, and the self-consciousness, irri-
tate more often than they amuse The illustra-
tions are mainly Beardsley’s—that is, they are
mainly quaint gambadoes or irrelevant uglinesses.
Beardsley, too, contributes the first chapters of
a “ romantic story ” —whereof a passage to taste :
Helen slipped away the dressing-gown and rose before
the mirror in a flutter of frilled things. She was ador-
ably (all and slender. Her neck and shoulders were
wonderfully drawn, and the littl- malicious breasts were
full of the irritation of loveliness that can never he
entirely comprehended, or ever enjoyed to the utmost.
Her arms and hands were loosely but delicately articu-
lated, and her legs were divinely long. From the hip to
the knee, 22 inches ; from the knee to the heel, 22 inches,
as befitted a goddess. Those who have spen Helen only
in the Vatican, in the Louvre, in the Utfizi, or in the
British Museum, can have no idea how very beautiful
and sweet she looked. Not at all like the lady in
Lemprifere.
A little of this goes far.
Perhaps the most readable of other items is
that in which Bernard Shaw advocates Church
as a stimulant. Havelock Ellis’s notice of Zola
is heavy, but with some suggestion. A good deal
of the remainder is tedious. And Arthur Symons,
who flatters and was flattered by Paul Verlaine,
translates one of the dead decadent’s poems
characteristically, in the style of a craftsman :
-■MANDOLINE.
The singers of serenades
Whisper their faded vows
Unto fair listening maids
Under the singing Loughs.
Tirris, Aminte, are there,
Clitandre is over-long,
And D unis for many a fair
Tyrant, makes many a song.
Th»ir short vests, silk' n and bright.
Their long, p il», siiki n trains,
Their ilfgance of (b light.
Twin, soft, blue, shadowy chains.
And the mandounes and they,
Faintlier breathing, swoon
Into the rose and grey
Ecstasy of the moon.
LOVE’S MATERIAL BASIS.
Writes Frederick Rockell in Feb. Fbee.Review
(a magazine which isn’t dull, or ponderous, or
antiquated, or half as well read ns it deserves to
be) :
That the altruistic spirit as expounded in Tolstoi’s
‘Life’ is entirely absent in sexual love is conclusively
shown by the fact that the perfect altruist would sacrifice
his lover for humanity, while ihe perfect lover for his
heart’s desire would sacrifice the world. Altruism and
sex love are entirely antagonistic concepts, and in any
single consciousness each must vary inversely with the
other.
We must not overlook the fact that even this broad
love for humanity is based on egotism, the following of
the line of least resistance; hut there is a sufficient dis-
tinction between the egotism that receives its fullest
satisfaction in making one particular woman happy, and
that egotism whose happiness consists of indiscriminately
bestowing good upon others, to warrant the use of the
terms altruism and egotism to differentiate these two
phases of egotistic Consciousness.
Perhaps a still commoner error is that which posits a
spiritual basis for sex attraction. Men and women, im-
bued with the Christian idea of the degradation of phy-
sical sex union, may deceive themselves into believing
that the basic element of their relations with the opposite
sex is spiritual and not physical ; that the subtle
streams of feeling which bind them together flow re-
sponsive to spiritual demands; that the harmonious
blending of being with being is due to the harmony of
their egos in their essential nature, apart from the sex of
their bodies ; hut the helief is based on purely empirical
assumption, which, when put to a practical test, melts
into its native moonshine. If sex love he spiritual, it
must be capable of existing apart from sex. For spirit,
or mentality, has no sex. But where is the lover whose
lot e would survive the metamorphosis of the ego of the
loved person into a body of the same sex as his own, or
into the body of an aged woman or decrepit man? Where
is the spiritual lover who would, with unaltered feelings,
regard the beloved being as the sexual possession of an-
other ?
Alfred Lee, in S.M. Herald, raises the interest-
ing question whether the word “Australia”
(which he thinks was first given to this country
by Le Maire) appeared, so spelt, in print before
162-'. In that year an account of Le Maire’s
voyage to the South Seas in 1615 was published at
Amsterdam, and on page 32 of this account “ Aus-
tralia” appears. The de Quir Memorial (1610)
refers to “ Australia,” but the point is: Who
fixed the current spelling ?
The Queenslander’s portrait of Henry Lawson
is so trimmed and barbered that the most fas-
hionable of minor songsters might own its soft
impeachments.” And Lawson is the author of
“ When Your Pants Begin to Go ” !
Some recent phrases :
The mind and the temper which enable woman to front
life as a rational combatant, instead of .wget iting as a
more or less destructive parasite.
S ie tasted in that moment something of the weakness
of womanhood—its pitiful groping artificiality, its keen
passionate realness.
Life may be said to be composed of things that come off
and tilings that don’t come off.
The long nights when we dropped down the Irrawaddy,
the whistle of the wind in the white sails, and the singing
of the boatmen, and the sick suck of the alligators among
the reeds.
A poet proceeds not by argument, but by vision. He
does not clothe a soul with flesh, but informs a bodv with
life.
The et rnal rehabilitative process, in which every
woman shows herself such an adept in relation to the
man she loves.

A new vo uihe of Waterloo literature, E. L. S.
Horsburgli’s “Waterloo, a narrative and a
criticism,” is a student’s summation of documen-
tary evidence and comparison of contrary judg-
ments. The author does not warm to the
emotional aspects of his subject, but hib cool
e kit-ism is all the more luminous for the lack ;
•Hid there are dithyrambics enough already.
POETRY AND POLITICS.
One wonders whether William Watson is a poet
because he is a Liberal, or a Liberal because he is
a poet, or a little of each because he is a little of
both. Anyhow, the Liberal newspapers praise
him extravagantly; the Tory newspapers praise
him coolly; he sonnets the Liberal view of the
position in Armenia, while the Tory laureate
sonnets the Tory view ; and—where exactly is the
Muse while political devils are thus paid with such
a superabundance of piping-hot poetical pitch?
Superabundance—for the Armenian situation is
simply this : Britain has had definite diplomatic
warning that the continental Great Powers will
have none of her interference in Armenia,
massacres or no massacres. Salisbury has there-
fore simply had to ask himself whether he is
disposed to defy Europe for the sake of calming
the Nonconformist Conscience, and has answered
himself that he isn’t. Which affirmation of the
very sensible thesis that a great European war
would mean more bloodshed and misery than
many Armenian massacres has decided poet-
Watson, or politician-Watson, or poet-politician-
Watson that he does well to be very angry
indeed in seventeen neat little poems of 14 lines
each.
These sonnets are good enough (and Austin’s
counterblasts are bad enough) as sonnets go, but
they are worthlessed for want of factual and even
ethical basis. For, as the ingenious “0.5.”
(another Tory, by the way : the connection
between British politics and poetry must be close)
told W.W. :
It further seemed a work uncommon light
For one like you, a casual civilian,
To order half a hemisphere to fight,
And slaughter one another by the million,
While you yourself, a paper Galahad,
Spilt ink for blood upon a blotting-pad.
And then there is a suspicion of staginess in Mr.
Watson’s newest work—a hint of one eye in a fine
frenzy and another on the gallery—which detracts
still further from his “illusion.’-’ And he is quite
too poet-sure that “God”—who doesn’t crook a
little finger to blast “Abdul the Damned, on his
infernal throne ’—is angry at England’s inaction.
And—mother things.
POSTSCRIPT.
This enviable paper ! Oh, to think
That it will go, will really, really go
To her, my mistress. Had it soul to know,
What enviable paper ! Oh, to think
The sweet light of her eyes, her sweet clear eyes,
Shall shine on it; her sweet cool hands caress it,
And bear it to her sweet warm lips ; and press it
The sweet pale roses of her cheek. First, eyes,
Hands, lips, and cheek, and then, at night, all
nigh%
In the sweet darkness of her room (ah, so 1),
In the sweet stillness of her room (speak low !),
I guess where it will lie, at night, all night.
—Ernest Wentworth, in “ The Yellow Book."
Kenneth Graham’s “Golden Age ” has risen to
a deserved second edition. Its gleams of boyhood
are delightful enough to long preserve it from the
dust-heap of fugacious literature. Yet in one or
two later essays come signs .... well, happy is
the writer who is never in at his hobby’s death !
In the new Yellow Book, Richard le Gallienne,
who has done little notable lately, rebounds to a
“ prose fancy” on a level with his best. “As I
write, lie closes, “the moon looks down at me
like a Madonna from the great canvas of the sky.
She seems beautiful with the beauty of all the
eyes that have looked up at her, sad with the tears
of all those eyes; like a silver bowl brimming
with the tears of dead lovers she seems.”
Sydney School of Arts has a “ Suggestion
Book ” for the use of members. The other day
somebody proposed that the new magazine, Cos-
MOPOLis, should be obtained. The committee’s
report on this was “ eight numbers in the library.”
The next entry is by a subscriber who makes the
same suggestion, and in letters an inch long points
out that what is required is Cosmopolis, not Cos-
mopolitan. To a request for Watson’s “ Father
of the Forest,” the answer was “wait for a
cheaper edition.” No wonder that, with intelli-
gent readers, the popularity of this up-to-date
institution every year increases.
When Mrs. Kemble published her “Memoirs”
in the Atlantic Monthly, she referred to
“Omar” Fitzgerald as “an eccentric man of
genius, who took as much trouble to avoid fame
as others to obtain it.” Fitzgerald asked her to
withdraw the passage when the Memoirs should
appear in book form ; and meanwhile had the
separate magazine sheets bound for his private
use. These lie lent to a friend, who noticed that
me page was pasted over. It was the page with
the deinurred-to reference. Thus was the critic
justified of her author.
Anthony Hope’s “Comedies of Courtship”
reprints several magazine stories generally lacking
his early inspiration. “ The Curate of Boltons ”
most nearly suggests the light that failed—blown
out by flattery and a aucceWe-publishers.
Macmillan and Co. achieve “a big scoop ” by
;he addition of Kipling’s prose, Saintsbury’s new
translation of Balzac, and Wells’s “ Wonder-
ful Visit” to their cheap Col. Library—whose
owered merit-standard now goes up to the top of
;he class at a bound.
A new story by George Gissmg, “Sleeping
Fires” (Fisher Unwin), does not do that clever
observer credit. He analyses emotions admirably
from a fact-basis ; but his fancy cannot fly alone,
and when here, at page two-score or so, the plot is
laid bare, its development becomes tedious.
Canon Farrar’s “Gathering Clouds” —his se-
cond religio-historical romance in the style of
Kingsley s “ Hypatia ’’—runs to two volumes in
Longmans, Green’s Col. Library. Few large
underdone pies could weigh on the silent watches
of the night more than those two volumes.

LOOK!
BEFORE YOU
T. E! A FI
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use vthen the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PARTED.
Maoriland.
It was not in the morning
Or evening that we met,
No land the world adorning
Was round about us set —
But we remember yet.
Wild roses were the border
That girdled all the land,
Dear Love in sweet disorder
Had dropped them from his hand —
Like Time’s deceiving sand.
Were dryads tryst a-keeping?
Were fauns a-foot with Pan ?
Were pain and sorrow sleeping
As when the world began ?
As Love himself began.
The swans have flown asunder
On Love’s secluded lake,
His star is muffled under
Clouds that will not break—
Oh ! sweet, for old love’s sake!
H. C.
SONG OF THE OLD SUN-DIAL.
I sing no nitric lays of truth,
But film the cancer-eaten past
With eerie fay-lore, verve of youth,
Romance and burgeonry of caste :
I strive that Glory’s charnel-room
No gentle nostril overpowers ;
Tho’ grief a million days may gloom,
“ I only count the sunny hours.”
The sky may warn, in cirrus scroll,
Of cataclysmic change ahead ;
Insistent stratus layer with dole
Horizons spacious Hope had spread ;
Weird wrongs may mass their cumuli,
Or, lurid, belch from nimbus towers :
These weary Joy. I pass them by,
And “only count the sunny hours.”
The dark is for “ the common herd ”
By whom God’s dirty work is done ;
By whom life’s sweetmeats are prepared
For those who can enjoy the sun.
Y ou’d have me pen their shoddy strife,
And deem their fungus virtues flowers ?
Deny that glad repose is life,
And cease to “count the sunny hours ” ?
Tho’ woman soiled blaspheme the night,
And veins of men are leeched for gold,
Tho’ stark religions nations blight
And vulture Hunger waxes bold ;
There are a few who know not this,
A lily few, in rosy bowers,
To spare their dainty hearts it is,
“ I only count the sunny hours.”
Melbourne.
DANTON.
THE DREAM OF THE FREE.
I had a happy dream last night:
Methought I was a slave ;
Weak wills ruled mine ; their touch was light;
Soft eyes commandments gave.
To wife and child, no more apart,
Love’s chains were binding me.
I woke, and sadness filled my heart
I found that I was free.
Oh ! did I deem my lot was hard,
To toil and never cease ?
Were wife and child a poor reward?
Death brought me soon release ;
But in that world where loss and pain
Shall ne’er remembered be,
They’ll let me wear those chains again,
And never more be free.
Maoriland.
C. CLYDE.
INNOCENCE.
She smiled ; dim love-lights, long concealed,
Life beckoned, slow were born :
And sequent passion swiftly sealed
Her present peace forsworn.
She smiled again, and stooped to drink
Where mortals may not quaff ;
Unheeding on the vibrant brink
A demon’s dreadful laugh.
In wide wastes of white shifting sand,
Half conquered, all beguiled,
A grey ghost led her by the hand ;
And yet again she smiled.
N.S. Wales.
HESKETH.
Disclosed at the trial of the Reform Com-
mittee at Pretoria that Col. Rhodes communicated
with Dr. Jameson by means of a cyclist named
Celliess, the despatch being carried in the saddle.
This is the lir.it time the cycle has been used in
actual warfare. Celliers was taken prisoner when
returning 'with the doctors letter to the Colonel,
and the despatch fell into the hands of the Boers.
“ What strikes a stranger in Brisbane,”
writes a much-travelled Bulletin correspondent,
“ is the ignorance and uncommunicativeness of the
local police. If you ask one to direct you any-
where the almost invariable reply is : ‘ Well, sor,
raley I don’t know where it is. I’m a stranger
here meself.’ They must move them around a
good'deal up there, so that they won’t form local
ties. Consequently, if a Brisbane man wants to
know the time, he has to look at his own watch.

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, MARCH 28, 1896.
Syndicates and Boodle.
The Sydney Daily Telegraph, which holds
a perpetual brief on behalf of ‘ ‘ private en-
terprise,” raised its voice the other day, for
the five hundredth time or more, in the cause
of the boodler and the syndicate, and in op-
position to the principles of State Socialism.
The following is Fat Man’s case, as set forth
in the D.T. :
Of what use is it (Freetrade) if, the moment anybody
proposes to take advantage of it, Parliament raises an ob-
jection, as it has recently been in the habit of doing when-
ever private enterprise has asked for permission to go to
work at its own risk and utilise the opportunities for pro-
fitable employment which the country offers? At the
present time there are vast amounts of stagnant capital
that would be invested in providing conveniences for the
people and facilitating public industry. The State could
get a number of needful works of this kind done . . .
without increasing the liabilities of the taxpayer by a
single penny, or taking any responsibility for their finan-
cial success. There is the connection of the two halves
of the metropolis by bridge or tunnel. . . . Then
there is the supplying of Sydney with electricity for
power and lighting purposes. ... If any one offers
to bring the city up to date in this way, it is treated as a
plot to loot the public, and indignantly stamped upon . .
as if there could be any possibility of loot in connection
with such matters unless Parliament deliberately author-
ised it. . . . The money which would come here for
legitimate investment therefore goes into wild-cat mines
in Western Australia. . . . Experience proves that,
except under certain special circumstances what is done
by private enterprise ... is generally done more
effectively and at less cost to the public than when under-
taken by the State. The State is such a bad business
manager . . . that in the world’s competitive arena
. . . it has no chance against private enterprise,
&c., &c.
* * * »
This outbreak raises the whole question of
private versus State enterprise, and the vast-
ness of its mendacity, as well as a good many
other things, makes it worthy of a passing
notice. In the first place, it assumes that
the people of N.S. Wales are poor and per-
manently short of employment because the
State won’t hand over the electric-lighting of
Sydney and the North Shore railway-bridge
to “private enterprise,” which is a large
assumption and an eminently foolish one to
boot. These two works would find employ-
ment for perhaps 2000—or more probably
1000—out of the 30,000 or 40,000 of
the unemployed, and they would employ
these few workers for a year or two at most.
The great balance of the workless multi-
tude would be left just where they were ; the
benefit would be almost imperceptible and
what there was of it would be.- only tem-
porary. The capital—it is distinctly foreign
capital that is alluded to—that found invest-
ment in the North Shore bridge and the
electric-lighting service would be counter-
balanced to some extent by a large amount
of local capital wrecked in the superseded
gas and ferry services. Meanwhile, the
inference that relief can only be obtained in
this fashion is wholly ridiculous. The
refusal of the State to hand over these two
splendid monopolies to private enterprise for
ever, merely for the sako of affording an
imperceptible amount of temporary relief, is
no reason whatever why the capital which is
alleged to be yearning to employ itself and
the people should not find employment
forthwith. There are innumerable avenues
open which the State has left exclusively to
private enterprise, and where no Parlia-
mentary sanction is required. New South
Wales wants weaving, spinning, worsted,
tweed, and miscellaneous clothing factories,
iron, brass, and steel works, foundries,
shipbuilding-yards, carpet-factories, tea-
plantations, beet-farms and sugar-mills and
refineries to treat the product thereof,
tobacco - plantations, cement - works, wire-
works, glassworks, sawmills, and a thousand
other industries, to supply locally the wants
which are now supplied by the importer.
There are £15,000,000 or £20,000,000 worth
of goods per annum wanted to be made in
New South Wales, to fill the urgent require-
ments of the inhabitants of the jirovince,
and, at the same time, to furnish work for
its people and profits for its capitalists.
And, with all this immense field of enter-
prise open, the spectacle of the aforesaid
capitalists—who want to employ their money
so badly —weeping on the Government door-
step because the State won’t assist and coddle
them by handing over rich monopolies per Act
of Parliament is abject and pitiable. The
enterprise which won’t move unless the
State endows it with a valuable concession,
and supplies it with half-a-million compul-
sory customers is not private enterprise, or
anything distantly resembling it.
* * * *
Private enterprise, in great public works
like the North Shore bridge and the Sydney
electric-lighting scheme and many other in-
dustrial and financial undertakings, is dis-
tinctly dearer, less efficient, more clumsy,
more dishonest, and more unreliable than
the enterprise of the State, the Telegraph’s
dictum to the contrary notwithstanding. In
almost every line of business where the State
and the private capitalist have competed, the
State has proved itself the more efficient of
the two. The State has conducted banking
operations in Australia for many years, and
every State savings bank has kept solvent
through good times and bad. Private enter-
prise has strewn this new country already
with wrecks innumerable. The Bank of
Australia, Bank of Auckland, Bank of
Queensland, Bank of Port Phillip, Town
and Country Bank, the Bank of S.A., the
two Mercantiles, the Federal, the awful City
of Melbourne, the Commercial of S.A., the
twelve (or thereabouts) land banks of
Matthew Davies, the three or four cracked
Munro institutions, the Australian Banking
Co., the two or three dozen other miscel-
laneous banks which broke in different parts
of Australia since 1890, the innumerable
collapsed building societies, the 8.N.Z.,
which was only saved by being taken,
by the State, out of the hands of private
owners—these are a few out of the
great multitude of examples of the stupidity,
expensiveness, dishonesty and unreliabity of
private enterprise. So are the Queensland
National, kept alive by State support after
private management had run it on the rocks;
the Colonial of Maoriland, rescued in the
hour of danger by the new State-managed
institution ; and the New South Wales banks
which didn’t burst because they were saved
by the State guarantee of their note issues.
The Bank of England, commonly regarded
as the brightest example of everything that
a bank should be, only exists to-day because
the State came to its assistance over and
over again ; otherwise it would have gone
the way of private enterprise in general. On
the other side of the record the Maoriland
State Life Department has shown that the
State can conduct insurance business very
much more cheaply than any privately-
managed institution of the same size under
the same conditions, and the State office is
the only one belonging to this country on
whose honesty and solvency there rests no
shadow of suspicion whatsoever. On a rough
estimate two-thirds of the life assurance
offices which existed in Britain and
America have died - pf dishonesty or mis-
management, or both, but this one State
office has, so far, kept its record untarnished.
The Maoriland Public Trust Office also
stands immeasurably above the private
trustee companies (at least half of which are
admittedly in great difficulties) for honesty
and efficiency. As for the private trustee,
who is (or should be) mostly in gaol for being
a Gent One, he is too great a failure to be
worth mentioning.
* * * *
State railway management has, so far,
shown distinctly better results than the
“ private enterprise ” article. The difference
between the two is that the State, as a rule,
honestly meets its obligations ; the private co.
files its schedule when things go badly, and
leaves its creditors in the lurch. If the Vic-
torian system had been in the hands of private
companies, the Outer Circle line, the Lance-
field line, most of the little Gippsland
branches, and a whole lot of the miscellaneous
cockspurs would have gone broke separately
on the American plan, written down their
capital, and compounded with their deben-
ture-holders. Then they might have shown
3 per cent, profit on what capital was left.
The more profitable lines, which yield 5 or 0
per cent., would have meanwhile gone on as
usual, and the whole system might have
shown a paper dividend of 4 per cent, all
round, instead of 24as at present. The
State could produce similar results by the
same process ; if it liked to repudiate
£20,000,000 of the public debt, or to allege
by a book-keeping entry that its railway sys-
tem was liable for only £20,000,000 capital
instead of £37,000,000, it would arrive at
exactly the same results as the private syndi-
cate and by exactly the same method. But
there would be no real saving ; the profits
would not be one penny greater ; it would be
a mere juggle with pen and ink and nothing
more. The Maoriland Midland Co. lias
cheerfully abandoned its unfinished line \
sold its available land-grants and “ blewed
the proceeds, and left its luckless debenture-
holders to scramble for their principal and
interest. The State, even if its management
had been equally awful —-and the most
iniquitous Government ever known in these
provinces never made such an unspeakable
muddle of any enterprise would have
debited the line with its entire cost and, pro-
bably, when it was finished and in working
order, have shown a dividend of 2s. 6d. per
cent, per annum on the original outlay. The
private co. avoids this by bidding the original
outlay, as represented by the people who lent
the money, to go to perdition, and this rough-
and-ready process of writing off capital
enables private capital to show much better
results on paper if it feels so inclined. But
the difference doesn’t represent any saving
whatever. If the private railways of Europe
and America were debited, as the Australian
State lines are, with all the money spent
upon them, the annual interest returns
would be something appalling in their ex-
treme smallness. The worst kind of State
management has built the Australian rail-
ways more cheaply than the private lines of
Canada, the United States, and every Euro-
pean country save Scandinavia and Denmark
—which is at least something for the despised
system to boast of.
*■ * * *
In Great Britain public ownership is
rapidly superseding privates enterprise in the
matter of gas supply. Over one-third of the
British gas supply is now in the hands of
the people, with the result that the private
companies charge on an average 3s. 10gd.
per 1000 cubic feet, while the corporations
furnish an equally good illuminant for
3s. 4|d. The successful State tobacco mono-
polies of many European countries, and the
State postal and telegraph systems are
further evidences of the possibilities of
national control. The State, it is true, has
temporary booms and deliriums, but private
enterprise gets them in a much worse form.
The Victorian railway system, which is at
present under a very bad cloud, is often held
up as an awful example of the evils of
State management because it represents
£37,000,000 sunk—much of it in a sudden
boom madness—in an enterprise which only
yields a little over 2f per cent. Private en-
terprise, on the other side, is represented
by the £40,000,000 of nominal capital sunk
in Westralian gold-mines, which yield so far,
all round, about one shilling per cent. ; so
that, judged by the actual returns, private
management is about fifty-five times the more
incompetent and expensive of the two. Pri-
vate enterprise—its cheapness, intelligence,
and other advantages—is further represented
by all the millions buried in the mud at
Panama : about as many as are represented
by the whole railway system of South Aus-
tralia or Maoriland. It is represented, on a
smaller scale in Australia, by the insolvent
Mildura irrigation settlement, the defunct
A.S.N. Co., the Midland railway already
mentioned, the broken banks with their
awful “revelations,” by between ten and
twenty gone-broke fire-insurance companies,
by Fink’s |d. in the £, and the like. The
difference between State control and private
enterprise, as shown in this country, may be
summed up in a few sentences. The State
never writes off its capital ; the money it has
spent in needless railways, ill-planned harbor,
irrigation and other schemes, costly and dis-
honest land-resumptions and the like, always
stands up in judgment against it in the
figures of the public debt. Private enter-
prise buries the story of its failures in the
Insolvency Court to the extent of about
£4,000,000 or £5,000,000 per annum, and
says no more about it. £f the State simply
put the Illawari'a railway, or the famous
Outer Circle line, aqt its various irrigation
failures, or its loans to municipalities that
can t pay up, into a schedule, and paid 6d. or
so in the £ to the British bond-holders whose
money was invested in these particular enter-
prises, and got its certificate in the usual
course, it could probably show that it was
making 10 per cent, on its remaining under-
takings and thereby demonstrate the bless-
ings of State management to all comers. Or
if it could reconstruct its creditors, or drop
its lost capital out of sight, ~ and leave its
shareholders—the investors in the Public
Debt—in the lurch, or settle its ' good
assets on its wife and then assign its
estate, or compound privately for one
Fink m the £, it would arrive at the
same result. And if, on the other hand,
the aggregate private enterprise of the coun-
try was debited with all the capital written
off, and the hundreds of millions wiped out
m the insolvency court, and with the liquida-
tions, and the millions of unaccounted-for bad
debts, and the secret compositions, it would
probably be shown that it was earning some-
thing like os. per cent, per annum all round
or possibly less, and the general verdict of
the unthinking multitude would be that
private enterprise was only another name
for idiocy.
* * * *
To return to the original text, the Daily
Telegraph’s plea for the handing over to
the private boodler of the North Shore rail-
way and the Sydney electric-lighting mono-
poly is a fine example of confused argument
and wilful suppression of fact. These ruinous
proceedings are advocated, first, on the plea
that they will absorb the stagnant capital in
the country. Secondly, they are supported on
the plea that they will bring into the country
“ for legitimate investment ” some of the
money now flowing to Coolgardie, therefore
it seems they are really to be executed with
British money, and the stagnant local capital
is to be left in its present unprofitable con-
dition, so this end of the plea is a self-
evident fraud. They are to absorb the
surplus labor of the country, W'hereas these
two small temporary jobs would hardly make
any visible impression on the great mass of
the unemployed. If they are carried out by
the State they will probably be done with
borrowed money for which the community
will pay about 3h per cent. ; if they are done
by private enterprise they will be built with
money that will want 5 or 6 or 8 per cent.,
or as much more per cent, as it can get,
and in each case the per centage must
be paid by the community in some shape or
another. Yet, by putting the matter in
private hands and doubling, or nearly
doubling, or more than doubling the amount
to be extracted from the taxpayer, the work
is to be done according to this reasoning,
without increasing that person’s liabilities
“ by a single penny.” And, curiously enough,
the desire of the Fat Man paper seems to be
solely directed towards the handing over to
private enterprise of these two undertakings,
each a magnificent monopoly with an assured
profit attached, and no others. There is no
suggestion that stagnant private capital
should occupy itself by building a railway
system west of the Darling, -where no railways
haveyetbeenprovided, oroutbeyond Hayand
Cobar, or in the gruesome desolations around
Hungerford. These profitless, but necessary,
jobs are cheerfully left to the State and the
taxpayer. The building of railways in the
wilderness “Out Back,” the locking of
western rivers, the construction of irrigation-
works in the thirsty backblocks, and all such
undertakings, are distinctly matters for the
State only. But similar works in the
densely-peopled city, where dividends are
certain, should, in the Telegraph’s opinion,
be reserved strictly for private enterprise,
and any interference with it is a sinful
example of grandmotherly legislation. And
private enterprise, according to the Tele-
graph’s definition, is a thing that howls
piteously to be subsidised by valuable State
concessions, and then works them with bor-
rowed English money, and which won’t
move unless it is thus subsidised, and won't
risk its own money by any chance, but
always shrieks for J. Bull to come to its
assistance. An affair like that is hardly
worth calling “ enterprise.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Freetrade Prices.
A few days ago a (presumably genuine) lady
correspondent broke out in the columns of the
S.M. Herald to the effect that Freetrade was,
after all, a snare and a disappointment. The
price of necessaries for a workman’s family, she
alleged, hadn’t diminished in the least; the
alleged benefits hadn’t come along; and the
load that was to be lifted off the backs of the
toiling masses hadn’t lifted worth a straw.
Other correspondents promptly followed in the
same strain, and the trouble grew so bad that
at last the serious old paper was moved to
demonstrate (in its issue of March 18) that
there really was a reduction in prices, and that
the weary toilers and moilers were experiencing
the blessings of Freetrade though they might
not be aware of the fact. It published a long
list of prices as they were before and after the
beginning of the imported shoddy regime, and
the list has brought consolation up on the
Rocks, and carried joy to Waterloo and Botany
and Surry Hills. Tinned turkey, tinned tongue,
Strasburg meat and anchovy paste have
dropped from Is. to lid., and anyone
who knows the vast quantities of these
things consumed by the working classes
will appreciate this boon at its full
value. American tinned fruits (largely con-
sumed by the local pick-and-shovel man) have
fallen from 21s. to 19s. per doz. Imported
English biscuits by the best makers have also
declined ; citron-peel is down ; likewise pre-
served cherries, 22 kinds of farinaceous foods,
Mellin’s food, Allen and Hanbury’s food,
and Neaves’ food. (The wharf-laborer is
much addicted to Neaves’ food, and the rush
for Mellin’s food among coal-lumpers is some-
thing unprecedented.) The costly varieties of
boots, ranging from 325. 6d. a pair (the kind
naturally used by people who repair the streets)
to 12s. 9d., have all been reduced, though the
coarse, unpoetic blucher apparently remains
where it was—at least, no figures are given to
show the contrary. Four-button gloves are
much cheaper; so are silk velvets, surah silk,
best Brussels carpet (the kind that the artisan
and laborer and corporation mud-pusher all use
in their drawing-rooms), Kensington art carpet
squares (grea.tly rushed after by itinerant pea-
nut vendors and the men who heave the ropes
01 i • kj l6 ferry-boats), and tapestry carpets
(chiefly patronised by small selectors). Also,
dagdag (whatever that may be) is now
only 295., whereas it was formerly 355., and
gentlemen s white shirts have been reduced
considerably. The present writer also adds,
from personal experience, that tapestry cut-


tains are selling at lower prices, and that
marb.e clocks are a good deal cheaper. All
these things go to prove that the frequent
boast of the CoD-fearing Ministry that it has
taken a load amounting to .£500,000 (sometimes
the figure is stated at £800,000) a year off the
shoulders of the working classes rests on a good,
solid foundation. Bread, it is true, went up
about December last and hasn’t gone down
since ; meat remains the same ; and Premier
Reid declared recently at Hillgrove that grain
and flour were dearer than they had been for
many years. But the reduction in best Brussels
carpet, art squares, four-button gloves, silk
velvet, surah silk, tapestry, boots at 325. 6d. a
pair, tinned turkey, tinned fruits, bottled
cherries, citron peel, shrimp paste, and Skin-
ner’s real turtle-soup (now only 2s. lid. per
tin as against 3s. under a semi-Protectionist
r4gime) has brought gladness to the homes of
the very poorest and filled the pick-and-shovel
person with a joy that is too great for words.

The Humorous Aspect of a Bank.
The City of Melbourne Bank inquiry is full of
wild humor to those people who did not risk
their money in that famous wild-cat institution,
and who can therefore afford to take an
unbiassed view of the concern. A good deal of
the humor has been supplied by Thomas
Loader, chairman of directors. Loader was
apparently the principal boss of the institution.
He was paid by the shareholders to guard their
interests, to know what the bank was doing,
and to tell the truth to the shareholders about
the dreadful affair, and if he didn’t do these
things his fees were money obtained practically
under false pretences. How he attended to his
duties is best shown by his own evidence. Mr.
Loader “didn’t think he ever saw” a book
which showed that £138,000 had gone in bad
debts ; he didn’t know whether the bank ob-
jected to an enormously large overdrafter giving
id. in the £ in full of all claims, or what the
securities were for a £190,000 loan ; he had
never heard of a certain £57,000 overdraft,
and he really couldn’t say how the
bank came to guarantee £l2OO a year
to one of its debtors who owed it
£200,000 on rather shaky security. He couldn’t
give any information about a security said to be
worth £83,000, and really worth, perhaps, a
tenth of that sum. In regard to a cable mes-
sage to the London directors stating that the
largest advance was £170,000, while there were
debtors who owed the bank nearly twice that
amount, this same remarkable chairman “really
knew nothing of it.” He couldn’t tell how one
large overdraft was never charged interest, and
when he heard that the bank bought its own
shares, and registered clerks and others as the
nominal holders of them, he merely thought
that it was “ very likely.” Mr. Loader
vaguely understood that the deceased manager
had debited the loss on some of his (the mana-
ger’s) private share transactions to a customer,
who repudiated the debt with scorn; but
nothing was done in the matter. He “sup-
posed ” that something had been done about
a bogus draft drawn by the same manager for
£2300, but was evidently hazy on the point;
and he didn’t even know, after 25 years of bank-
directing, how an appropriation for losses was
made. He also couldn’t explain how an alleged
“inner reserve” of over £lOO,OOO was re-
presented as being in existence after it
had all been used up, and he didn’t know
anything about the balance-sheet of an awful
firm which owed the bank £300,000 or more
These are a few taken at random out of many
dozen things that Loader “ didn’t know ” or
“couldn’t remember” or “had never heard
of.” The rest of the directors were all more or
less hazy—one of them really didn’t know that
he owed the bank a very large overdraft until
his attention was drawn to it ; they mostly
knew hardly anything that they should have
known, and they mostly owed the bank an im-
mense lot of money on dreadfully bad security ;
but Loader’s haziness about everything was
more hazy than the haziness of all the rest of
the board put together. Taking him on his
own valuation, Loader’s defence was that he
was ignorant, stupid, reckless and incompetent;
that he didn’t do the work he was paid to do,
and let the bank muddle itself away through
his callous indifference to the interests of the
people who trusted him; and that after 25
years’ banking experience he was such an un-
speakable “ duffer ” that he didn’t know some of
the simplest details of the business. And as the
law stands, Loader can’t be made responsible
for his ghastly neglect. If he had driven a
locomotive which blew up and killed a dozen
passengers, and he defended himself by saying
that, though he called himself an engineer, he
really knew nothing about locomotives, and had
merely gone fooling down an incline at random,
the law would probably give him seven years
for manslaughter. If he was a captain and
wrecked his ship from sheer ignorance and
neglect, the law would take his certificate from
him. If he were anything but a bank-director,
he would be held personally responsible for the
consequences of his reckless muddlement—but
a bank-director is practically responsible for
nothing at all. And he can’t be made respon-
sible, for, as old James Service pathetically
remarked, if directors (like Loader) were held
personally liable for their doings, it would be
impossible to get decent men (like Loader) to
accept the position. And if men like Loader
wouldn’t accept the position, the consequences
would obviously be something awful.
A Presbyterian on Drink.
The N.S.W. Presbyterian Assembly recently
got on the subject of drink, and made a number
of wholly unexpected remarks thereon. In the
matter of whisky the Presbyterian Assembly—
whose name mostly begins with Mac —is pro-
bably better qualified to speak with authority
than any other religious body in Christendom.
It may not know more about beer than does
anybody else, and its ideas about cocktails and
gin-sling and brandy-smash and dog’s-no3e may
be limited, but on whisky its information is
solid and reliable, and extends down to the
deepest bowels of the subject. And the
Presbyterian Assembly, in the vastness of its
knowledge about whisky, was mostly unfavor-
able to a certain enthusiastic brother who
wanted to rush things in the Cold Tea interest.
Said one reverend gentleman :
It was not whisky that was ruining the people, but
Kow s embrocation and painkiller, sold diluted with water,
shearers and bush workers, overcome with the loneliness
and miseries of their existence, came into town when the
feeling of depression became so strong that they could
not bear it, and were driven to drown their miseries by
knocking down their cheques. It was not the drink that
u a a , oln S them the harm, but the hocussed liquor. He
had known men knocked down by a couple of glasses of
liquor, and who knew nothing after. They said that
when they had drunk those two glasses they felt their feet
go, and their limbs became helpless. Two glasses of
honest liquor would not do this for any man. . . But
they must do something as well to counteract the loneli-
ness which caused the spree. Young men had nowhere
to go to, and no means of amusement, and they went to
the hotels to seek it. . . Drunkenness was as often
caused by poverty as poverty by drunkenness. It was
not poverty that killed a man, but hopeless poverty. The
feeling that he was down and couldn’t get up brought a
man to the condition in which he would drink anything
just to drown his misery. . . Teetotallers could do a
great deal more for their cause if they were not so unrea-
sonable. He had often tried to go with teetotallers,
though he had never been one ; but when he went to
them he found them so terribly crabbed, cranky, and un-
sociable, that he had to seek other company. If you went
into a teetotaller’s house, you could not get up without
antimacassars sticking in your coat-tails. Their houses
seemed made to look at, not to live in. You couldn’t
smoke, you couldn’t wink, you couldn’t do anything.
Ordinary people found it hard enough to keep ten Com-
mandments, but instead of ten the teetotallers had ten
thousand.
Several other reverends tackled the subject in
the same strain. There was apparently nobody
in the Assembly who approved of heavy drink-
ing) but the more jovial of the rev. Macs ob-
jected strongly to the grimness and gloom of
the Cold Tea movement, and earnestly urged
the Cold Tea faction to try and be more cheer-
ful. Wherein they undoubtedly struck the
right nail on the head. The cadaverous aspect
of the teetotal party, and its desire to sit
straight up on a hard chair and to be in bed by
ten, are things that do a great deal to keep the
whisky interest going. When the bushman
comes into a strange town after a long spell of
solitude among the gum-trees and the plains,
his first craving is to find a live human being,
or a number of them, to foregather with. In
many cases he doesn’t want drink so much as
he wants to become rapidly acquainted with a
lot of people and get rid of his loneliness as
much as possible. He can’t do it in a bun-shop,
for even a bushman can’t eat buns all day, and
the terrified female behind the counter wouldn’t
meet him in a hilarious spirit, anyhow. He
can’t go straight to the house of the principal
teetotaller and become a temporary brother
to him, and sing rowdy songs in his
drawing-room, and he can’t go and riot
in the boot-shop. What he wants is as much
noise and gaiety and good, or bad, fellowship
as he can crowd into the time, as an antidote to
the silence and grey desolation that have eaten
into his soul; he wants noise of any kind—
music, profanity, the smash of dishes, free
fights, anything at all that is unlike the old
solitude. And the pub. is the only place where
a complete stranger can go and begin to riot
with all and sundry without previous acquain-
tance. If the leading teetotallers are willing
to get together and make friends and yell with
any complete stranger from the bush at a
moment’s notice, and sing comic songs with
him, and introduce him to the gold-washed
girl in the tart and ginger-ale shop, then they
also may supply a long-felt want jin his soul.
But they are sad and silent like the gum-tree
and the native bear and the staring bullock,
and Clancy of the Overflow finds no joy or
variety in them.
The Bench v. the Law.
Sydney stipendiary magistrate Giles is
a decent magistrate—as Sydney stipendiary
magistrates go. (The Bulletin leaves it to its
Sydney readers toappraise the value of this com-
pliment). No doubt the jabbering Mr. Giles’s
decision to refuse costs and make fines nominal,
in diseased-cattle-selling cases, is reached hon-
estly. Yet this decision, and the action based
on it, are matters of very serious offence to the
public. The law of N.S. Wales declares that
anyone who sells diseased cattle is guilty of a
crime against the public health, and may be
heavily fined therefor. The law is sensible and
right. In many cases it is impossible to tell"
that a beast is diseased before it is killed.
Whoever buys a diseased beast buys it as sound,
and pays the price for it as sound. Conse-
quently it is unjust that, when the carcase is
condemned as unfit for food, he should lose his
money. Any risk which he can guard against
betakes willingly. Here is a risk which he
cannot guard against, and should not be com-
pelled to take. No man has a right to pass off
on another damaged goods as sound, says the
law. Therefore, let the seller suffer, said
Parliament. “We compel the stock-buyer to
vend nothing but good meat to his customers ;
we will similarly compel the stock-seller to vend
nothing but good meat to his customer, the
stock-buyer.” And so, a few months ago,
when certain diseased cattle were sold for
the Sydney market, the buyers refused to
purchase them as sound, and prosecuted the
salesmen for breach of the law. The
salesmen were fined, in moderation. Then
a great outcry arose. The salesmen button-
holed their club-friends, and they and their
club - friends buttonholed members of the
Government and their club-friends, and—so it
was allegecl—a circular was issued from the
Department of Justice, desiring magistrates to
deal gently with the erring salesmen. The
magistrates were said to declare that they
would not be dictated to, and the fining pro-
ceeded —but, significantly, in much more mode-
ration. Now comes Daniel Giles to judgment,
and declares that he will not give effect
to the spirit of the law at all, and
doubtfully to its letter; and embodies his
declaration in a fine of Is., without costs. Mr.
Giles arrogates too much to himself. He is
the interpreter of the law, not its judge. His
private opinion seems to be that the seller of a
diseased beast, a worthless article, should be
allowed to cheat the buyer out of its price ;
but the bench is no place for the utterance of
Mr. Giles’s private opinions, when they are
held against equity and against law. Relying,
it may be, on the wink of his official superiors,
he goes much too far. The Bulletin trusts
that, if possible, an appeal to the Supreme
Court will at once follow. Should that prove
fruitless, Parliament may be safely trusted to
deal with Mr. Giles.
Sydney Daily Telegraph sets forth some of
the strange anomalies of the N.S.W. Land and
Income Tax :
If you have £3OO worth of land, and make £3OO a year
oy growing potatoes, your annual tax is 00 pennies, or ss.
rJut if you write a book or patent an invention, or keep
a store, or drive a railway-engine, and make £3OO, and
land at all, your annual tax is £2 10s.
This may be an anomaly, but it’s the law.
Aflorist who earns £2O a week from the cultivation of
£241 of land pays no income-tax. His total tax is a penny
a year. 1 J
If, however, he pays a manager a salary of £3OO a year,
the manager is taxed to the extent of £2 10s.
As a contrast to the florist, the professional or business
*?who earns £2O a week, and hasn’t any land, will pay
£2O a year in taxes.
Income obtained directly from the land is exempt from
income taxation, because the land pays land-tax.
If, therefore, you derive rents from houses on land of
an unimproved value of less than £240, you include the
amount in your income-return.
But if your rents are from houses erected on land whose
unimproved value is over £240 you do not pay income-
What you pay is land-tax.
If you receive a rent of £4O on a house built on land of
an unimproved value of £240 you pay 20s. tax.
While if you receive £4O or any other amount of rent
from a house built on land of the unimproved value of
£260 you pay only Is. Bd. tax.
In the first instance the amount of the tax depends on
the amount of the rent.
In the second it depends on the value of the land.
Consequently, if a man lias a salary of ,£2OO a
year, and also a piece of land worth (unim-
proved) ,£240, and a house on it which yields
£BO rent, he comes under the income-tax and
pays £2 a year taxes. But if a man has a
salary of £2OO a year, and also a piece
of land worth (unimproved) £720, on
which is erected a terrace of houses
bringing in £4OO a year, he only comes
under the land-tax, and pays just ,£2 a
year on more than twice the income and five or
six times the property ! This cheerful state of
things is probably called discouraging the big
monopolist, and shifting the burden of taxation
on to the shoulders of the rich, and other things
of the same kind. In other words, if the Act
has been correctly interpreted, the big landlord
of the Sydney Burdekin description (whose
land is worth over £240) pays neither land nor
income-tax on his house rents, while the small
one whose land is worth less than £240 pays
6d. in the £ on his house-rents. Either the
Telegraph or the Act has been drinking.
There is one (and only one) hopelessly illogical
item in the Queensland Labor programme—
that clause which demands the establishment of
a “ State department of Labor to which men
can apply for work at a minimum wage as a
right.” No one province and no one country
or even continent can run such a department,
unless it can and does effectually shut out all
immigrants except people of solid wealth.
If Queensland established a department
which was bound to find work at a decent
wage for all comers, there are probably
50,000 Cornstalks, 40,000 Victorians, and
40,000 people from Maoriland, Fiji, South
Australia, Tasmania, and VYestralia, to say
nothing of the countless thousands of British,
Germans, and others, who would want to
“ trek ” for the land of promise at once, and
Queensland can’t supply work and a minimum
wage to all creation. A country where employ-
ment and decent pay were a dead certainty
would attract a stream of workless inhabitants
from all the four ends of the earth. And, by
the way, why is the department to find work
for all men only ; where, in the name of brother-
hood, do the women come in ? Has the seam-
stress, who makes shirts for 16 hours a day and
7s. 6d. a week, got no rights in the matter?
The Bulletin would like to see N.S.W. Public
Service Board entrusted with the task of
retrenching the N.S.W. Public Works Com-
mittee, which conducts the most barefaced
public grab extant. Worse still, the steal is
one with which no Government is likely to
interfere, inasmuch as the P.W. Committee
system controls both the votes of those in
possession and of those expectant —the former
being more than sufficiently numerous and the
latter simply legion. The committee consists
of thirteen Ms. P.—the Devil’s number-
selected from the two Houses of Parliament;
the members get three guineas a sitting and
sit as often as ever they can, four times a week
(12 guineas) being a pretty regular thing, and
five times (15 guineas) not uncommon ; when
members are engaged on sectional committees
of the main body and go anywhere to inspect
proposed works they draw two guineas a day
regularly with travelling expenses added. The
regularity of their attendance is simply para-
lysing. A political wreck may be too ricketty
to crawl a yard under ordinary circumstances,
but when a committee-sitting is on he spends
money in an ambulance to fetch him to town
and dump him down at the committee-table in
order that his name may be inscribed on the
attendance-book as entitled to a fee ; and when
a strong blast of eucalyptus or iodoform is
wafted into the nose of the permanent Works
officials and loud crutch-clumps and coughs are
heard upon the stairs, the professional staff know
that poor, patriotic old Jobbleton is coming for
his “stuff.”
It would be desirable, in the interests of the
economics of horse-feed, wear-and-tear and
interest-on-capital, to know the whole particu-
lars of the Collingwood (Vic.) municipal con-
tracts for supply of man, horse and dray. In
one case it is said that the contract-price is
38s. 6d., and as the minimum wage of 6s. per
day is paid to the man, according to a docu-
ment signed both by the contractor and the
driver, this philanthropic dray-owner manages
to serve his country, feed his horse, shoe him,
risk his death, run a stable, repair his dray,
and receive his recompense as entrepreneur, on
2s. 6d. per week. Here surely lurks the philo-
sopher’s stone of economics. By the way, why
was objection taken in this council to requiring
a statutory declaration, instead of the present
unconvincing document, in such cases ?
Seduction, pure and simple, is not a wrong
known to the law. Whether it should be, is,
of course, arguable. But in some cases, by a
fiction, "heavy damages can be got against the
seducer. If a girl’s people are in sufficiently
comfortable circumstances to keep her at home,
she can be seduced with the certainty of getting
some compensation for the seduction. For her
father or mother can sue the man for the loss
of her services, although said services may
consist in bringing a cup of tea to her mother’s
room once a month or so. Once the alleged loss
is established, there is practically no limit to
the amount of damages obtainable—this being
one of the few cases in which vindictive dam-
ages are allowable. But, if she is a poor girl
sent out to work for a stranger in order that
her services may be really helpful to her home,
and the stranger’s son or visitor, or the stranger
himself, seduces her, she has no remedy at all,
because her parent cannot allege loss of services
in such circumstances. Here is a real woman’s
grievance, and it is pleasing to note that the
S.A. women’s-franchise crowd are questioning
M’sP. there re their willingness to have the
law altered.
The Kanakaland Elections.
Kanakaland Government is an adept in all dis-
honest artifices. Its Premier is head of a Political
Association formed to disfranchise democratic
voters —a conjunction which, as ,T. G. Drake says,
would be tolerated in no other community
governed on the lines of the British constitution.
A good deal of its energy is devoted to propping
with public money broken financial institutions in
which its supporters are deeply interested. After
careful enquiry into the politics of 3500 new voters
enrolled in January, who would be formally
entitled to vote in April, it decided that a
majority were probably opponents of its policy,
and advanced the current elections by a month
in order to rob these duly-qualified elec-
tors of their votes. At the same time,
it scattered the elections over the same month,
in accordance with time-dishonored principles of
political corruption, in order that its plural votes
might accumulate unrepresentative majorities, and
its de r e ited candidates might slink up many con-
venient backstairs. Already it is reaping the
harvest of its cunning. Thomas Joseph Byrnes,
its Attorney-General, routed at North Brisbane,
has ample time to succeed at Cairns—Burns,
Philp, and Co’s spittoon constituency, trebly-
mortgaged to the Govt.' bank, whose “mean
whites ’’ are spurned even by the Chinese who earn
their livings for them. Byrnes sat for Cairns in
the last Parliament, and explicitly said “ I will
never desert Cairns till Cairns deserts me,” and
Cairns humbly thanked him for the honor. Then
he thought he saw a chance of succeeding at North
Brisbane, and threw Cairns over without a word
of apology, and Cairns humbly thanked him for
the honor. Then the Govt, party at Cairns
chose Ernest Milford, a local solicitor, to repre-
sent it, and Byrnes sent up the parasitic little
Lissner as his nominee, so the party kicked out
Milford, crawled to lick Lissner’s boots, and
humbly thanked Byrnes for the honor. Now
Byrnes is beaten at North Brisbane, and, on the
whole, he thinks he will sit for Cairns again, and
Cairns will humbly thank him for the honor. As
for the independent manhood of the Cairns Govt,
party, one of the local banana-Chinamen could
easily put that in his opium-pipe and smoke it.
* * * •
The election which has reduced Thomas Joseph
Byrnes’s head in one act from the size of a prize-
pumpkin to that of a split pea has given the gods
more laughter than they have had for many a day.
There has been nothing like it since Parkes was
thrown out of East Sydney in ’B3 with a howl that
split the welkin. Yellow Pup fought Green Pup,
Paddy Mick struggled with Sandy Mac, and
Courier white-elephant was gloriously burst by
Telegraph white-elephant. Byrnes was deter-
mined to foist himself on North Brisbane —a very
conservative constituency of aged Tories—and
succeeded in getting the formal approval of his
party-chief. He forgot that, though one man
could lead the Political Ass to the green water,
twenty could not make its orange section drink.
And his rival and colleague, Horace Tozer—the
John Davies of Q. underground engineering, who
gained entrance to Parliament in ’BB by rousing
the Wide Bay Prodhestants to beat Catholic
John Elood—would indignantly deny that he was
hauling on the tail of the Yellow Animal
behind the scenes. So two pure Presbyterians—
Macdonald-Paterson and Bob Fraser—were forced
into the running. They claimed to be every bit
as good Ministerialists as Byrnes and Kingsbury;
and the Labor-party, which is learning tactics,
put up no candidate and gave them all its support.
The Courier shrieked wildly for Byrnes; the
Telegraph, at whose parson-ridden office the
Yellow Pup has a favorite kennel, shrieked still
more wildly against him. He was hampered
by alliance with Kingsbury a clever politi-
cal “bounder” whom M‘llwraith carried in
with him three years ago, but who has
never become popular, even with his own
side, and who lives always in the odor of
insincerity. Then there was a wide feeling that
“ Tommy ” was getting altogether too bumptious,
and that a snub would do him good ; while Pater-
son and Eraser, though absolutely “safe” men
from a Tory point of view, are not smirched
with the dirt of Boodlewraith—which even Q.
Tories occasionally eat with grimaces. Result—
Paterson, 1342 ; Fraser, 1252 (elected); Byrnes,
1157 ; Kingsbury, 1009 (gnashing teeth in outer
darkness). Defeat of the accredited Govt, candi-
dates ; comparative triumph for the Labor-party ;
and a very nasty pill for Bulcock and the Courier.
» * * *
So far, the Labor-party has done as well as it
expected. The first batch of elections—as per
another time-dishonored artifice—was arranged to
put in a good body of safe Government candi-
dates, and so encourage the others. As a nett
result, including half-a-dozen unopposed candi-
dates, the Labor-party puts five men in and
Government 26 ; while there are three in-
dependents. Labor-leader Glassey got in for
Bundaberg, beating Ministerialist Duffy. Bunda-
berg is a Kanaka centre, and the Govt, expected
a victory. But there has been k little too much
Kanaka lately—and here again the Yellow Pup
barked. Labor divided the seats at South Bris-
bane and Fortitude Valley, lost one at Ipswich,
and was beaten by four votes at Too-
wong, where one of its most valuable members,
Mat Reid, was defeated by an Independent new
man, Furney. Ogden was thrown out at Towns-
ville, and Powers’ retirement at Maryborough
gained the Ministerialists a seat. A good man
was narrowly beaten at Mackay—H. B. Black—
and Ministerialists Dalrymple and Chataway were
returned. “A peculiar feature” noted is that
“amongst the rejected candidates there are five
ex-members, all Govt, supporters, and all R. C’s,
including the Attorney-General. Among 26 Govt,
supporters elected there is not one R.C., and there
are only three R.C’s out of the 34 members
elected.” Good old Freemasonry !
