MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s B. and B. Farewells.
Royal “ Silence of Dean Mait-
land.”
Alexandra “ His Natural Life.”
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
The opera co. is doing a sunset blaze of
glory at Sydney Lyceum with “ In Town,” which,
taking it all round, is a good deal better a render-
ing than that given by the alleged Gaieties some
little time ago. The new Duke of Muffshire
in particular, and the new Captain Codding-
ton, and Muffshire’s son Clanside (Flo.
Young) are especially commendable. Miss Mari-
etta Nash makes a lovely and fiendishly agile call-
boy ; also May Pollard as the heroine is as beauti-
ful as a dream of Elysium. By the time these
remarks appear in print, however, “ In Town ”
will be a thing of the past, and after a particularly
special outbreak on Thursday night, which will
definitely close the season, the opera co. once more
vanishes over the horizon. The great and
wondrous “ Djin-Dj in ” commences on Saturday,
and if it is anything like the production it was in
Melbourne a few months ago it should be the most
dazzling thing of the Easter season. The Japanese
landscape has already arrived, and the earthquake
is on the way. Two other Sydney theatres are
preparing the biggest spectacle they know of for
this festive Easter-tide, and W. and M., by turn-
ing on “ Djin-Djin ” show that they have realised
the greatness of the emergency and have resolved
to keep ahead. Meanwhile the odds are some-
thing to one that Sydney can’t support four big
theatres and three small ones all at once next
month, and that one management, if not more,
must go down with a dull, sickening flop.
# * * *
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) does one conse-
cutive night of “ Monte Cristo ” on Saturday by
way of farewell to Dampier, who, with Miss
Dampier, Mrs. Marshall, and various others, are
leaving the big theatre for a Victorian tour.
Thereafter the bereaved co. breaks out into the
great military spectacle, “ Called to Arms.” On
this occasion the Ber-ritish flag will once more
wave triumphantly over heaps of dead, and the
routed enemy, after fully revealing his mean,
underhand character, will fly out of sight, and the
leading characters will be happily re-united, or
their innocence will be proved, or they will die
nobly in the face of immense odds, or recover
their lost child, or be restored to their ancestral
estates, or find the missing will, or something to
the same effect. The British flag is now waving
itself violently in the back part of the theatre to
get into condition for the effort.
* * * *
Bland Holt opens Sydney Royal on 4th
April, with a very large drama entitled “ One of
the Best.” Said drama has been a triumphant
success in Bull-land, and B. Holt backs it to prove
the biggest thing he has yet accomplished. There
is a gorgeous Highland regiment in it, and rumor
asserts that Bland Holt, Baker and other leading
characters appear in kilts and struggle with a fur-
tive, breezy petticoat through four long acts.
Miss Elizabeth Watson, the leading lady, sister of
the swan-like Henrietta, doesn’t appear in kilts,
■ which is much to be regretted. There is no actual
battle in this drama, though the British Flag
waves a good deal, but it is understood that if
there was a battle the low, degraded enemy would
be routed with great slaughter, as usual.
* * * *
A dramatic co., which insisted, contrary to
all evidence, on taking itself seriously, broke out
last week at Sydney Music Hall, and was still
there at time of writing. Its first production was
entitled “ Sydney Under the Limelight,” and
proved to be the “Two Orphans” localised and
brought up to date regardless of consequences.
The consequences were too awful for description.
Jacques, the wicked brother, was changed to John
Donald Haggard ; his principal accomplice was
Wilson Jones Boaker, and Mother Frochard
appeared on the bills as “Old Mother Hag Hag-
gard.” Another character was described as “the
man of to-day M.P.,” whatever that might be. The
two orphans arrived at Redfern Station, where the
one who wasn’t blind was abducted by John Donald
Haggard, assisted by Wilson Jones Boaker. The
cripple, who cleaned boots instead of running the
familiar knife-grinding machine (which possibly
wasn’t available), was a good deal larger and more
massive than his weedy big brother Jacques, and
Jacques in a belltopper and new suit didn’t cor-
respond at all with his beery she-parent and the
family attic. Jacques also did duty as the prin-
cipal nobleman in the gilded haunt of vice where
the abducted girl was taken, and when he loftily
offered to send “ ten of his people if necessary ” to
look for the blind girl it didn’t seem to fit in with
the 2s. 4d. he had just embezzled from his crippled
brother, and the threepenny beer he had had with
that walking disease, his parent. The gilded haunt
of vice was a solemn place where the dissipated
noblemen didn’t know what to do with their
hands and were much embarrassed by their feet.
The abducted girl was openly chloroformed by
John Donald Haggard and Wilson Jones Boaker
—at least, the writer believes it was Boaker—in
George-street, outside the railway station, and the
blind girl was left sitting on a carpet-bag in the
middle of the road, where she ought to have been
promptly run over by a tram-car, but wasn’t.
Later on, the blind girl was found singing, in what
seemed intended for a snowstorm outside St.
Mary’s Cathedral. The wild, mediaeval lawless-
ness of John Donald Haggard and Wilson Jones
Boaker broke out all over the drama, but ulti-
mately the former was knocked out, and what be-
came of Boaker this paper doesn’t remember.
The co. in general was too much agitated about
getting safely off the stage without falling over
itself to call for serious criticism.
* * * *
Later on John Donald Haggard and Wilson
J ones Boaker and the rest attacked ‘ ‘ East Lynne ”
and routed it with great slaughter, leaving much
dead grammar and decomposed pronunciation on
the field of battle. The version produced was a
new one apparently written with a hay-fork, and
it was played in a way quite different from any-
thing The Bulletin ever saw before. “ Oliver
Twist ” is to be staged next Saturday, and later on
the co. proposes to attack “ Hamlet,” which is
too old to defend itself and will probably collapse
in the first round. The Prince is to be repre-
sented by some one described in the bill as “ the
Young Rocni’s,” whoever he may be. Said bill,
however, is an irresponsible kind of document,
and probably it had bolted at the time this state-
ment appeared.
* * # *
The only notable change at the Tivoli is
that John Coleman is doing his last five nights,
and that the popular Miss Florrie Forde re-appears
for six nights only, and the Thornton Sisters nave
been added to the programme. The Opera House
has put up a new first part, called “ Moonlight
Revels,” which, considering that everybody who
went out in the moonlight of late has had to revel
under an umbrella, seems hardly so well-timed as
it might be.
* * * *
A choice Gabbettian edition of “ His
Natural Life ” was published at Melb. Alexandra
on Saturday, and received with awful delight by a
gasping multitude. Manager Dan. Barry’s talent
in “ catering for his special class of patrons” is
proverbial. He reads their innermost souls, and
knows exactly what they want. When in a posi-
tion to treat them to a full meal of mutilated
corpse D.B. serves it up with plenty of pepper and
blood sauce. The bill of fare abounds in appe-
tising details: “A floating hell,” “ Gabbett, the
man-eater,” “150 lashes for chewing tobacco,”
“the famished man-eater,” “the blood-reeking
triangles—the lash—flogged almost to death,” “a
hideous instrument of torture—nine hours agony, ”
in addition to sweets and cheese.
* * • »
A super., purporting to be the real original
Gabbett, brought over from Tasmania at vast ex-
pense, figures among the chain-gang and chews
the cud of bogus bygone-banquet reflection. No
horror has been left unturned in the production
of this masterpiece of cold meat. The working
Gabbett (not the amusing scarecrow) is a brawny
tragedian, steeped in chalk and red ochre, who
seems always on the point of smattering some-
body’s brains with an uplifted axe. He makes
fifteen attempts to cleave Dora Mostyn (the Crow)
in twain when he gets her alone in the bush, but
Dora pleads for fair play on the ground that she
has little or no flesh on her bones, whereas he has
been fattening on the defunct “ Moocher ” (whose
sole remaining leg lies festering in the sun behind
the scenes), and at last Gabbett consents to decide
the question at issue by drawing straws. Dora
wins this draw, nevertheless the dishonorable
beast of prey flourishes his chopper as before.
Then he rampages off to find the Moocher’s leg,
leaving his young friend to soliloquise upon her
approaching doom. Clever Miss Mostyn certainly
holds possession of the house just here. In her
mind’s eye she sees Gabbett devouring the Moo-
cher’s lungs, liver, and pleurisy, and lapping up
the crimson gore as it drips from the Moocher’s
heart. An actress who can talk of such private
matters to a deadly silent audience, and make
them whistle for more after she has crawled out of
sight, is a valid excuse for prolonging “ His
Natural Life ” into next week, or even further. It
seems probable that Miss Mostyn will hear Gab-
bett’s back teeth crunching through the Moocher’s
collar-bone for several nights to come.
* * * *
At the Princess’s a round of popular revi-
vals is bringing the B. and B. season to an end.
Arrangements as follows : to-night (Thursday)
“Rebellious Susan,” on Friday “The Idler,” on
Monday and Tuesday next week “Mrs. Tan-
queray,” followed by one night each of “Dandy
Dick” and “The Magistrate.” Saturday next
at the Princess’s has been reserved for the
farewell benefit of Mr. Boucicault, now about
to dissolve his partnership with Mr. Brough
and leave a grateful country to mourn his
departure. The Bulletin would like to
dilate upon the claims of the junior B. to all the
benefit which Melbourne can afford, but a lump
in the throat of this soft-hearted journal forbids
further utterance on the subject. Nor is there
any need to remind Melbourne of its old friend’s
theatrical services. Boucicault is very dear to all
admirers of artistic work, and, on the occasion of
his complimentary benefit, nobody worth mention-
ing will fail to be present in the flesh, except those
people who, being unable to procure standing-
room, will merely get there in the spirit. Fortu-
nately, the space available for spirit accommoda-
tion is unlimited.
* * * *•
“The Silence of Dean Maitland” took
some pains to make a poor start at Melb. Royal.
Tuesday, instead of Saturday, was chosen for the
first performance, whereat the last act of this
grief-stricken drama was considerably weakened
by the omission of a front scene. The leading
man pitched his remorse in too low a key, and the
newspapers next morning committed fewer com-
plimentary perversions of the truth than might
have been expeoted. Circumstances were un-
favorable to the show at the outset, therefore its
present efforts to redeem the past and build tip a
decent reputation deserve all the friendly
oouragement they are likely to get. j
of Dean Maitland ” is a fairly intelligent and
quite intelligible adaptation of a clever novd. any-
way. Also, it is sufficiently well acted in some
parts to be interesting.
* * * *
A brisk, crisp, vigorous stage story was
not constructible out of the book sm& j
Everybody who appears in the proiogue is doomed
to die, disappear, or grow old before the finish.
The survivors are turned grey by grief, and a fine
chance for the introduction of somebody s patent
hair restorer is lost for ever. Eighteen yearsof
human suffering happen between the acts, and
the flight of time is unduly emphasised by the in-
troduction of a small young man who was unborn
when the curtain rose. The Bulletin must pro
test against this late arrival. He is ta,me and un-
necessary. A letter to the effect that he was
waiting round the corner, ready to burst into
notice unless his illegitimate father gave him
money to stay away with, would be preferable to
his presence. Probably the audience would sub-
scribe for him to remain absent if they were
warned of what they might otherwise expect.
* '* * *
King Hedley—better to say Mr. King
Hedley, since he isn’t a reigning monarch—
interprets the difficult character of Dean Maitland
with a little force and much discretion. Consider-
ing that he is an amorous, cowardly hypocrite in
the prologue, and a weak-minded apostle during
the next two acts, Hedley may be congratulated
on his rather likeable performance. He wears the
cloth of piety with an air of penitence and stands
fully 6ft. high. His final confession of sins in
front of the pasteboard altar is well spoken.
One listens to him all the time without smil-
ing, and feels almost sorry when he drops
dead. Miss Maud Williamson comes out strong
as the Dean’s depressed sister, Cyril Keightley is
an impressive victim of mistaken identity, barring ,
a slight disposition to “ twang,” and Edith Kelly
makes a good, garrulous old villager. Also, Miss
Polly Emery, in a child character, is a ray of sun-
shine. Some other people help to carry the matter
through, and the scenery goes on its merits, which
are considerable. “The Silence of Dean^ Mait-
land,” although announced to close on Friday,
will run awhile into next week, if the public give
the word.
** * *
The cold criticism dealt out by the Mel-
bourne morning papers to “The Silence of Dean
Maitland ” was, in a relative sense, very unfair.
The Theatre Royal show, at popular prices, is
much more praiseworthy than a hundred other
ventures which the same unreliable journals have
given encouraging notices to. But because it
happens to have been adapted from a powerful
novel it is judged by that novel (which nine-tenths
of the audiences know nothing about) instead of
being compared with the ordinary blood-and-
thunder melodrama, and judged by the golden
rule of “ so much advt., so much marmalade.” No
Melbourne daily newspaper can conscientiously
disparage any theatrical production nowadays,
seeing that none can be inferior to the belauded
shows presented at the Royal, and other houses,
within the past five years.
** * *
The cathedral scene in “The Silence of
Dean Maitland,” as played at Melb. Royal, can’t
possibly impress anyone acquainted with cathedral
pomps and vanities. A surpliced choir, consisting
of six men, sits facing the pulpit instead of way
back in the chancel, whilst the Dean’s blind son
figures, in a black velvet suit and a front pew, as
the sole solo choir-boy, and starts the service by
singing, “ Come, all ye faithful.” After which the
Dean strikes up a personal yam to the congrega-
tion, just as though he were a little Bethel shep-
herd who had forgotten to open the proceedings
with prayer. The scene is unnecessarily absurd.
For a couple of sovereigns they might have got a
verger to come in and stage-manage it to much
better advantage.
At Poverty Point .
The lately-deceased Marius caused a lady to be twice
divorced.
At boom-time plans were drawn for new Melb. theatres,
in Bourke-street and Collins-street.
Haresfoot: “I am not surprised at your anecdote of
G. V. Brooke preaching. Like most vinous Thespians he
was profoundly religious.”
Paradis : “ What an unsurpassable drama, in its way.lis
‘ Fedora ’! It is all action. Give the players the scenario
and they could fill in words, after the style of Richardson’s
Show. Not an ounce of superfluous cackle. No purple
patches. Nothing so absurd as Romeo’s description of
the Apothecary’s Shop to interrupt the development.”
Clifton Alderson, acting, it is supposed, for Geo. Rig-
nold, wanted badly to buy the Australian and Indian
rights of “ The Strange Adventures of Mrs. Brown,” but
he was too late, as the piece had been secured by Frank
Thornton (writes Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent),
whom you will see anon down under in this remarkably
funny piece, which as a laughter-maker is only excelled
by “ Charley’s Aunt.” Fred. Kerr, who owns the piece,
refused many offers, being determined that no one should
have it for Australia but the gay Frank, who undoubtedly
will make the most of it, though it is possible that he will
not play Kerr’s part, but that of the detective, which is
extremely funny.
The Hopetoun-visaged James Wood, a well-known Mel-
bourne tenor vocalist, is now at Glasgow—resting. Aus-
tralians anxious to write their “ name on the scroll of
fame, ’ and get well paid for doing so, should remember
that “ London is the market for real ability—but a starva-
tion workhouse for mediocrity.” Though this advice in
no way applies to Wood.
The claque , of which Coleman complains in Melbourne,
is an established accessory of some of the London music-
halls. At the Empire, especially, performers complain
bitterly of the blackmail thus extorted from them
amounting in some cases to £2 per week.
A leading bass in the Carl Rosa Co. is another valuable
export from Australasia to London’s fogs. Harry Pringle
is a Tasmanian, over 6ft., and a manly, handsome man.
He has been in England eight years, and is married to a
pretty little prima-donna.
The yarn about Teddy Lonnen’s capture and imprison-
ment by the Boers was all flam, being merely a specimen
of Mr. Lonnen’s funny ways.
Re Phil. Greenwood’s testimonial. Clifford Ferley as
hon. sec., got in for Phil, over 70 guineas* and J. C Wil-
liamson paid his passage to England.
Frank Cates’s health has given way, and to give his
lungs a chance he must have a sea-voyage, for which
purpose a testimonial movement is afoot. His voice is
almost entirely gone. Cates has long been out of luck.
Bland Holt’s tour through Maoriland has added materi-
ally to his cash in hand, and the company—especially
Walter Baker—seems to have “put on flesh.’ 5
Marie Elster has accepted a year’s engagement with the
Neilson Opera Co., now touring the English provincial
towns. “ Maritana,” “Faust," “Bohemian Girl,” “ H
Trovatore,” and “Daughter of the Regiment” are the
operas she has starred in up to date, and the press says
sweet things of the new lady from Australia.
Plain Bill Holloway and Charles Arnold were running
opposition shows at Johannesburg when the Jamieson
“invasion” happened. Two members of Arnold’s co.,
fearing that the Boers would sweep into the town and
shoot everybody down “ like rabbits,” made a bolt of it,
and were proclaimed traitors by the more valiant mum-
mers who stuck to the Flag, dontcherknow.
The divorce, in London, of Harry Atkinson (Fitts),
“ The Australian Orpheus,” by his wife, daughter of W.
K. Thomson, of Melbourne, is just announced. Another
love-partnership ends in bankruptcy and .dissolution.
Nine or ten years ago Harry was a slim, gentle-mannered
youth, with a long, silky moustache, and sang in the
church choir. Now he is a stout, coarse, clean-shaved
“ bounder,” with the manners, style, and language of the
music-halls, where his “turns” are almost as good and
as well received as ever.
Charles Clark, first and best of the lecture brigade, has
sailed for Melbourne, where he will begin his farewell
Australian tour about the middle of April. He has
written two new lectures for his last run round.
Kissing goes by favor. “It was long ago,” says a New
York paper, “ that Apple Mag of the Herald was under
orders to ‘ write up ’ Mme. Melba on every possible occa-
sion, and it was plain, from a look at Tuesday’s issue, that
these orders are still in force. The true story of their
earliest propagation has been familiar to the inner circle
for some time past. It appears that, two or three years
back, Mme. Melba met Mr. Bennett on a yacht owned by
a mutual friend. The newspaper proprietor made the
acquaintance of the prima-donna, and promised her that,
should she ever visit the States, the Herald should be at
her disposal. When Mme. Melba sailed for New York she
thought it unnecessary to remind Mr. Bennett of his
kindly offer, and the result was that her early perform-
ances were treated with extreme brutality by Apple Mag.
Mme. Melba bore with this condition of things for some
days, but she finally yielded to the advice of friends and
cabled to Mr. Bennett. The outcome of this move was a
clear change of tack on the part of the Herald, and, ever
since, Apple Mag has raved with the same heartiness over
Mme. Melba’s happiest, as over her feeblest, achieve-
ments.”
Madame Melba’s recent concert-tour in America was
such an extraordinary financial success that she could, it
is said, retire on the proceeds, a rich woman. She sings
in Paris during May, and goes to England in June, the
chief prima - donna of the world’s greatest operatic
organisation.
Since Fuller Maitland, of the Times, alluded to Ada
Crossley as the first contralto in England all the minor
critics have been busy placing her in front of Clara Butt,
the only singer in England who can come near the Aus-
tralian.
Melba’s secretary and companion, Miss Louie Bennett,
was married at New York on 14th Jan. to Mr. Kenyon
Mason, a young member of London Stock Exchange.
Besides being the devoted prot6g6e of the great singer,
the bride has other claims on Australian interest, for she
was born in Melbourne —Mr. Henry Bennett being her
father, Chief Justice Madden her cousin, and
Dr. O’Hara, her uncle. Melba invited young Mason
to America to see his sweetheart, and when the time
for parting came the hostess vowed she would make
them both happy, and gave them a wedding. Dr. Corri-
gan, the R.C. Archbishop of New York, conducted the
ceremony, which was privately celebrated in his draw-
ing-room, owing to the bridegroom not professing
the R.C. faith of his bride. A breakfast followed in
the smartest suite of rooms at the Savoy Hotel.
The place was a dream of flowers, crystal, and silver, and
the menus lovely little bells bearing medallion portraits
of the bride and bridegroom. All the members of the
Italian Opera Co., including Madame Frances Saville,
were at the breakfast, for which Melba smilingly paid
£5OO, and in addition she gave the bride a cheque for
£2OO and a grand piano. Mr. and Mrs. Henry Abbey’s
present was a suite of rooms on the promenade deck of
the steamer Paris, en route to Europe. Melba’s gown at
the ceremony was a most remarkable but lovely confection
of peach satin with turquoise velvet. With this she wore
a very large black Gainsborough hat, having waving
plumes, and her famous turquoises and diamonds. The
White Hungarian Band made its first appearance in New
York at the wedding, which Melba had carried out strictly
en princestse.
One of the most popular of Australia’s few clever
actresses is playing speaks with hubby again after being
“ out ” for years. Hubby has been “ resting ” assiduously
of late.
The most palpable absurdity of “The Silence of Dean
Maitland,” as put up at Melbourne Royal, is the distribu-
tion of gifts in the second act. A tired audience sits in
deep dejection for about a quarter of an hour, whilst the
Dean is fishing paper parcels out of a handbag, and deal-
ing them round to a pack of youngsters whos? enthusiasm
is suggestive of the vivacity of a dead frog. This is Art.
Oscar Wilde’s “ A Woman of No Importance,” and
Pinero’s latest, “ The Benefit of the Doubt,” are being
kept for the B. & B. season at Sydney Cri.
Marshall Hall, the Melb. musical professor, was fined
2s. fid. for biking along a footpath at Mornington (Vic.)
last week. He has been running classical-music concerts
for years past, and has so far met with no punishment,
yet the day he turns his jigger from a lumpy, sandy
alleged road on to a smooth but deserted path he is visiter!
with fine or gaol. Inscrutable are the ways of civilised
man.
Beryl Faber, of the B. & B. Co., returns to London per
same steamer as Boucicault. She has a brother and sister
in the “perfesh”—C. A. Smith, whilom-cricketer, with
John Hare in the U.S., and Miss Faber, who played Lady
Montague recently in “ Romeo and Juliet,” with Forbes
Robertson and “Mrs. Pat.” Beryl Faber has only been
playing four years. She goes back to George Alexander,
at London St. James’s.
George Rignold opens a season of six months at Melb.
Royal Aug. 1. He has a fine list of new' plays.
Absolutely the best-played character in B. and B.’s
“ Fedora” was that of the coachman, who spoke one word
—“ Ipanoff ! ” —in a quite convincing style. A lot of
execution can be done with one word, carefully fired off.
For instance, Miss Oliffe's nervous “Yes!” in the Mas-
queraders,” when the astronomer-person asked her if she
was willing to let her husband stake her on a game ol
cards.

Merrail’s Hydraulic Quartz Mill,
a very recent American invention, is being intro-
duced into Australia by A. G. Mitchell & Co., 118
The Strand, Sydney. The Mill has had many
trials, and has proved every time that it will
revolutionise the mining industry. It reduces the
hardest rock to pulp with phenomenal rapidity ; a
sft. Mill requires from 5 to 8 h.p., and crushes
from 15 to 25 tons every 24 hours; a 6ft. Mill
requires from 8 to 12 h.p., and crushes from 25 to
60 tons —the variation in the respective quantities
is due solely to the fineness of the screen. No. 10
stamp battery with its 10 h.p. can do more than
half the work of a 6-foot Mill. Wear and tear is
very small. The same firm are agents for THE
GOLD KING AMALGAMATOR, another
American invention which is fast displacing the
plates ; if used after the plates it saves all the fine
gold which otherwise would pass away in the tail-
ings. It pays for itself in a few weeks ; New
Zealanders attest—“lt saves every speck of gold
and mercury.’’ It will enable the beach miner
to make twice his present catch. Trials will take
place shortly; meanwhile, send for pamphlets and
particulars.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The examination into the affairs of the City of
Melbourne Bank stands adjourned sine die. It was a
lively examination whilst it lasted, and the evidence
which is now in course of transit to England will doubt-
less be received with howls by a special meeting 1 of
depositors. And when those distant victims have vented
some more of their feelings in another burst of noise, and
the chairman has duly commented upon Judge Moles-
worth’s “ scathing remarks,” there will be an end of the
matter, The Bulletin supposes. The Federal Bank
directors were examined some months, or years, a°-o.
They made a number of awful disclosures, and the Judge
passed a lot of scathing remarks, also the lawyers charged
terribly long bills for working up the job, and the
Court adjourned sine die. The rest was silence. If
there had ever been anywhere an intention to pro-
secute the Federal directors that intention was pre-
sumably, withered by a cold blast of Smellbourne legal
advice. If the Hinglish depositors propose to seri-
ously harry hon. Thomas Loader & Co. (who have the
advantage of a dead manager to dump responsibility
upon) legal advice may be expected to strike the sugges-
tion dumb. In short, this examination, like the former
examination, was a ghastly farce. It started with the
alleged purpose of discovering whether “something ought
to be done,” and haying elicited the fact that the afore-
said “something” is desirable, it will arrive at the
conclusion that nothing can be done.
* * » *
Among the directors of the exploded City of
Melbourne Bank there seems to be a beautiful unanimity
on at least one point. Everybody seems to be agreed that
almost all the very wrong things were done by Colin
Longmuir, the man that’s dead. The wickedness of the
man that’s dead is a truly painful thing to contemplate.
The Mutual Life Assurance Society of Victoria
holds its annual meeting on the 30th March, when Thomas
Loader (of the famous City of Melbourne Bank) offers him-
self for re-election as a director. Considering the awful
Story of ignorance, neglect, crass stupidity, incompetence
and sinful muddlement that Mr. Loader recently told about
himself at the City Bank inquiry, his gall in asking any-
body to re-elect him a director of anything is sublime.
And if the policy-holders re-elect Loader after reading his
own account of himself, they deserve anything that can
happen to them. The spectacle of Loader on the direc-
torate should be sufficient to scare any intelligent insurer
away from the office.
* * * * *
The old, solid Union Bank reports that the half-
year’s profits are £36,062, against £37,172 for the last half-
year, and £44,676 and £44,132 for the two previous halves.
This is the smallest profit which the Union has reported
for many years past. The 5 per cent, dividend absorbs
£37,500, so that even this humble percentage, like
the three preceding dividends, hasn’t been fairly earned,
and for the fourth time in succession the div. is made up
by taking a few hundreds from the previous balance. The
Union has between £4,000,000 and £5,000,000 cash on
hand, and is apparently paralysed by its enormous dead
weight of gold. A bank which keeps all its capital and
reserve fund, the full amount of its note circulation, and
over £1,000,000 of its deposits lying idle in the cellar, may
be a good concern for an invading army to plunder, but
as a dividend-producing machine it is bound to be below
par.
* * * *
The N.S.W. land and income-tax has proved a
blessing to the great tribe of seedy brokers, financial
agents, commission agents, and other people who
live by lurking mysteriously in an office in com-
pany with a battered table and two chairs. They
are all busy advertising their willingness to fill
up your schedule for ss. or more—in cases of very
complicated land-tax schedules the charge is anywhere
from ss. to £lO, or even £2O. Many people are badgered
and harried into the impression that these more or Ipss
mouldy persons are licensed by Government, and that
unless one of them fills up the tax-paper it will inevitably
be all wrong, and the result will be three months’ gaol, so
the sharks, many of whom know no more about the Act
than their clients, are prospering greatly. There is
hardly any kind of incident or event or calamity that the
financial agent won’t advise you about for 10s. 6d. or
eighteen-pence. If the Day of Judgment were at hand
the cheap and threadbare agent would charge 7s. 6d. for
explaining it to you.
* # * *
The Sydney Land Bank and Financial Agency
00., a concern which has for some time past led a pre-
carious existence in the wilds of Castlereagh-street with
no visible means of support worth mentioning, decided to
liquidate the other day. Said “bank” in its later days
was only a wreck on a rock, and the uselessness of trying
to look as if it was afloat became at last too apparent to
be disguised any longer.
* * * *
The Standard Fire and Marine Insurance Co.,
the smallest of the four big Maoriland offices, has gathered
in £73,858 of premiums during the year, and interest on
investments (£4339) and other items makes a total income
of £78,222. The nett profit, however, is exactly £5134,
and as £4339 of this is interest on the co’s own funds,
and £25 consists of share transfer fees, it follows that this
big insurance business has only yielded £770 of profit.
As the board hadn’t the moral courage to cut down its 7J
per cent. div. to 5 per cent., it pays £5625 to the share-
holders ; and, as this amount won’t come out of £5134,
the difference is taken from last year’s balance. Consider-
ing the precarious nature of fire and marine business,
this is a very solemn thing to do, especially as the income
from investments has dropped by £6OO in two years, the
nett premiums have fallen £4OOO since ’92, and the actual
profit of the insurance business has gone down steadily
from £13,000 in ’92 to £6BOO in ’93, £3500 in ’94, and now,
in ’95, to £77o—which is a hair-raising series of drops.
The Standard struck an awful snag in 1880-82, when its
dividends stopped and its reserve-fund mostly vanished,
and the co. itself seemed likely to fade off the face of the
earth. Since then it has been having a good time on the
whole and has rebuilt its reserve; but another streak of
bad luck appears to be coming along, therefore the desire
to pay 7i per cent, dividends, when the year’s earnings
are only 6ij per cent., should be suppressed till the clouds
roll by.
* • * *
Dear Bulletin, —Re City Mutual on 29th Sept.,
1594, vou wrote thus“ In reference to the remarks
made in last week’s Bulletin, as to the relative expenses
of various Australian life-insurance societies, there is one
aspect of the case which was, perhaps, not made suffi-
ciently clear. A large part of an insurance office’s ex-
penses consists of commissions and medical charges con-
nected with new policies. Business is expensive to acquire,
but costs, or should cost, comparatively little to retain
when once secured. Therefore, the expenses of a new
office where a very large proportion of the business is in
this initiatory stage, are generally greater than those of
an old one, and an institution which does a large new
business usually shows a larger expense account than one
which is less progressive. Last year the City Mutual had
by far the largest expense account among Australasian
insurance offices; but in proportion to its size its new
business was three times as large as that of the Mutual of
victoria, and considerably larger than that of the A.M.P.
or Colonial Mutual, or even the National Mutual, which
comes second on the list in this respect. Also, the City
Mutual reports the largest percentage of interest on its
nvested funds—about £6 Bs. per cent., against the other
offices average of £5 175.” Those remarks apply with equal
orce to-day. The City Mutual’s new business each year
snows a steady increase, and with one solitary exception
it is the only office which during the past few years records
a steady yearly increase in its renewal income. The City
Alutual has one of the soundest connections in the pro-
vince, and it may be of interest to learn that it is the only
Australian life office which periodically submits its figures
..I'.® scrutiny of an actuarial authority unconnected
with its staff.—Geo. Crowley.
The statements quoted by Mr. Crowley are as
true to-day as when they were written. The City Mutual
has the largest expense account, and also the largest
proportion of new business ; and, as new business
is vastly more expensive than renewal business,
the one partly accounts for the other. But, in
instituting a comparison between the City Mutual
and the A.M.P., let it be assumed that all the expense is
attached to the new business and that the renewals cost
absolutely nothing—an absurd assumption, of course, but
one which gives all the advantage to the City. In that
case the A.M.P. pays £166,000 expenses to earn £104,000
of income, and the City Mutual pays £10,197 to earn
£4577, which shows the latter’s expenses, even on new
business, to be a great deal the heavier of the two.
Therefore, The Bulletin adheres to its belief that
the expense rate of the City Mutual is a great deal too
heavy, and that every other office doing business in
Australasia, except the A.M.P. and the Maoriland
Government Department, is in the same condition.
Also, the question of expense doesn’t touch the main con-
tentions -that the City Mutual’s ordinary life rates are so
small that they may one day prove too low ; that the offer
°[ ,r> psr cent, interest in certain cases is taking a consider-
able risk ; and that there is a curious discrepancy in the
tontine estimates. Also, it makes no difference in the great
fact that there are far more life assurance offices in Aus-
tralia than the country wants, and that excessive compe-
tition is keeping the expense-rate dangerously high.

your issue of 7th instant we would state : -Ist. The num-
ber of shares taken up in the firm of W. F. Coxon and
Co., Ltd., bv an applicant depends entirely on his qualifi-
cations. We pay from 20s. to 30s. per [sic.] and 15
per cent, commission (we can show where good men earn
£5 to £8 per week). 2nd and 3rd. We have not had one
instance where a man has been discharged, had his salary
cut down, or been discharged to make room for another
man. We do our best to encourage and assist our em-
ployes. 4th. A shareholder in this co. has the same
privileges as a shareholder in any other concern has in
his co. sth. The capital of the co. is 112,000 in shares of
£1 each (payable as per prospectus). These shares can
be disposed of at their face value. The co. is managed by
a board of directors, who meet monthly. Mr. W. F.
Coxon is the managing director, and any information
concerning the company will be gladly given on applica-
tion to the head office, 145 Phillip-street, Sydney—Yours
truly, W. F. Coxon and Co., Ltd.. Geo. A. Wallace,
secretary.

[The Bulletin has also received a copy of the
articles of association, but as these begin at page 17 and
71 rules are missing the document is not very explicit. So
far as can be made out, however, it seems that “ no mem-
ber holding less than 10 shares has a vote,” so apparently
the small shareholder has no say whatever in the manage-
ment of the co.—which is a remarkable commentary on
the statement that “a shareholder in this co. has the
same privilege as a shareholder in any other concern.”
Also, it seems that the previous head of the firm (the man
who sold the business to the co.) is fixed as managing-
director for life and can’t be removed by the shareholders.
There are three directors besides this permanent person,
and all the four seem to have been appointed by them-
selves, or by the managing boss, or by somebody quite
apart from the mass of shareholders, and as they are only
to retire at the rate of one a year it will be three years
before the shareholders can control a majority of the
board—if the provision shutting out small shareholders
from voting allows them ever to do so. Also, the
directors retain power to invest the co’s money in land
and other things, and to convey any property of the com-
pany to trustees of their own appointment, and to do
various other interesting things.]
* * * *
The S.A. 3 per cent, loan recently floated in
London sold nominally at £97 4s. lid. From this must be
deducted 15s. Id., accrued interest and the usual
expenses, so that the amount actually realised is not
much over £95. At this rate it costs the State about £3
Bs. per cent, allowing for the loss at redemption ; and if
allowance is made for the cost of remitting the interest
for 20 years and the cost of humping the money back
when the loan is repaid, wjiich it presumably will be some
day, there is) very little to choose between a nominal 3
per cent, loan in London and a 3£ per cent, loan floated
locally. Last year S.A. offered a 31 per cent, loan in
Adelaide, and was rushed with far more money than it
required. Interest was then higher and money scarcer
than it is now, and Adelaide is not nearly so
full of surplus coin as Melbourne and Sydney; and
considering these circumstances it doesn’t seem as if the
Kingston Government had gained anything at all by sell-
ing its securities in England instead of in Australia, and
making the load of foreign indebtedness a little bigger,
and keeping up the old dreary ruinous fiction that Aus-
tralia can’t get on without leaning on British capital. The
one intelligent feature in the business is that the Govern-
ment retains the right to redeem the loan any time be-
tween 1916 and 1926, so it can’t be compelled to convert
on ruinous terms in a falling market. Otherwise the
Kingston finance is about the usual kind that Fat Man
Governments have been supplying for many years past.

The balance-sheet of the Cheque Bank (of
London) shows a net profit for 1895 of £14,172, against
£5323 in £1894. The dividend and bonus are at the rate
of per cent, on ordinary shares, and 12.) per cent, on
founders’ shares : £5OOO is carried to a reserve-fund, and
£2461 remains to credit of profit and loss. The Cheque
Bank has a paid-up capital of £84,702, and an uncalled
capital of £132,648.
The Civil Service Co-operative Society of N.S.W.
finished its 49th half-year on 31st Jan. The cash receipts
were £41,372, an increase of £5322 on the previous half-
year ; and the net profits, after providing for depreciation,
working expenses, &c., were £3273 equal to over 90 per
cent, per annum on the modest paid-up capital. Share-
holders get a dividend of 10 per cent, per annum on their
shares, 2s. in the £ on their cash purchases, and 9d. in the
£ on monthly accounts, and £5Ol is carried forward. The
C.S. Co-operative Society has had a long career of suc-
cess, as the result of good management and consistent
laying to its book. It has now a capitai of £6993, a re-
serve fund of £11,500, £1490 accumulated out of un-
claimed dividends, and, against £27,490 worth of assets,
its total liabilities to the public are only £3845.

Hot coals from Coolgardie Review :—“ Men
who are now rioting in luxury in London on money made
out of Coolgardie frauds, ought to be rotting in gaol with
white-fingered rascals like Jabez Balfour. Men who are
piling up wealth in holy Adelaide, ought to be piling up
stones in Yatala Stockade, and some whom we know in
Perth, fattening on oysters and champagne, ought, if they
had their deserts, to be picking oakum in Fremantle.”
* « * *
Judging by present appearances, it is quite pos-
sible that the VVestralian gold-returns for 1896 will show
little improvement even on the miserable returns
of 1895. Bayley’s gave 5000 oz. in the first half of ’95 and
only 2000 oz. in the second, and it seems now wholly
dead as a gold-producer. The Star of the East died, to all
intents and purposes, last August. All the mines that
contributed large amounts to the aggregate gold-yield are
flickering out, or have already flickered, save the Great
Boulder alone, and who will guarantee the Great Boulder?
It is not for want of crushing-power—in the Coolgardie
district alone 10 batteries out of 12 are lying idle—it is
simply want of gold. Moreover, much of the gold that
was procured cost more than its value to obtain, and when
the British shareholders get tired of paying the difference,
quite a lot of mines must needs go bung. There are some
£40,000,000 of foreign capital to pay dividends on, and all
the gold won in all Westralia last year, if every penny of
it went to foreign companies, and there were no workmen
to pay, no crushing expenses, no charges whatever, is
equal to just about 2 per cent, dividend per annum. After
allowing for expenses, it is less than 0 per cent. City
of Melbourne Bank deposits promise better than that.
* * * *
Will the Coolgardie papers reprint the articles
on W.A. goldfields that recently appeared in Sydney
Daily Telegraph? They were written by a very straight
man who knows what he talks about, combines practical
mining knowledge with knowledge of journalism, and has
been out there as a mining manager for the last 18 months.
What have they to say of this? “There are at leasts
dozen crushing plants at convenient points; the aggregate
is not less than 120 stampers ; there is abundance of water
to keep going continuously. But out of the 12 batteries
only two are now at work —Bayley’s South 10 stampers
and Londonderry 5 stampers. The rest, like the party in
the parlor, are ‘ all silent and all damned.’ These plants
cost an enormous sum of money, and it is a pity that they
should be idle. There are tens of thousands of tons of quartz
available, but divested of their patches as they have been
the lodes are not payable under present conditions.”

The holy city will have to grow considerably before it can

The very latest excuse is that Bayley’s Reward
collapsed, not from want of gold in the mine, but for want
of funds to get it out. Not enough capital, in fact. If
the gold in the great show-mine of Westralia won’t pay
for getting itself out, what kind of a goldfield is that pro-
vince, anyhow?
* * * *
The Coolgardie Mining Review : —“ The Bul-
letin denies the existence of good mines in and around
the town of Coolgardie, but the Australasian, owned and
worked by the brainy French investor, Dr. Simon, can
give the lie direct to the Sydney newspaper.” We are
told that 20 tons have gone 6oz. to the ton, and the future
is based on that average. And “ it lies about two miles
from the Coolgardie post-office.” Alas, how many alleged
mines have lied within two miles of Coolgardie? After
reading Coolgardie papers, one would even think that the
post-office itself would have to be pulled down for the sake
of the richness of the mine it covered. Wanted, a list of
the other rich mines, say within 10 miles of the post-office
—rich in respect to current crushings and divs., and not
merely in regard to what they are going to do, in the
opinion of the newspaper-men who have to earn their oats
(and thistles) on the spot. The Bulletin sincerely hopes
the Australasian will turn out well, for Dr. Simon and Co.
deserve it. They gave £40,000 cash for the New Vic-
toria, and—what do the late owners think of it ?
The reef seems to have turned dog ; and the Hill End
mine, for which £25,000 was given, and which, so far, has
returned so well, is not looking like a long-lived mine.

At last a crushing is promised from the Mari-
tana, W.A.—lOO tons are to be sent to the Leviathan
battery, which has now plenty of water, and is ready to
crush any mine’s ore, having none of its own.
* * * *
“An extraordinary shoot of gold” has been
struck in the Maori mine, Menzies, W.A. Nothing is
called a patch in W.A., but bitter experience has proved
that nearly everything extraordinarily rich yet found
there has been a small pocket or a short-lived patch.
* « » *
S.A. Govt. Geologist Brown says of the im-
mediate vicinity of Coolgardie : “ There is no likelihood
of artesian water being found by boring in uptilted and
faulted rocks such as these.” He believes that in the
deeper portions of the tertiary formations, occupying
wide areas of the flat country and plains, there will be
found beds of drift sand and gravel with probably lignite
and pyrites, containing abundance of water.
* * * *
Block 10’s big concentrating plant is, after very
great delays, got in order, and was satisfactorily tested
last week.
* * * *
Junction is disposing of a little of its second-
grade sulphides, in crude form, to Block 14 ; its first-grade
ore is still being shipped. The mine is now connected
with the railway line to Port Pirie and Port Adelaide ;
and Junction North is now having a branch put in.
Junction’s exports of ore still average over 1000 tons per
week. No change in the look of the stopes. The second-
grade sulphide contract with Block 14 is for 10,000 tons.
Block 14 has been looking better lately, especi-
ally at the 300 ft. level. Here the ore is more compact
than the bulk of what has been developed hitherto. Still
the mine could not conveniently keep its mill and works
running on its own ore ; its consumption of crude ore
now exceeds 2000 tons a week, independent of the small
quantity of carbonates sent to the furnaces at Pt. Adelaide
and used for blending. Sulphide concentrates are in such
active demand for shipment to Europe that for some
classes the co. finds it more profitable to sell straight out
to shippers than to roast and smelt on its own account.
Koch’s Pioneer, Bendigo, 250 loads and plates,
2490 z. 2dwt. ; 9d. div. (£1875), the second since the new
year. Principally breaking a 16ft. body of spurry country
at 1450 ft., that looks good for more divs.
Hustler’s Reef No. I—the1 —the Sunday tipster’s sweet-
pea, that slumped ss. in one pop—had a lean time last
half, calling up £945 13s. to meet ex.’s. Milled 988 tons
for 2050 z. lOdwt. Co. driving a level north at 1000 ft. to
intersect the Comet run of spurs, and, if picked up,
holders stand a chance of getting back the money they
dropped when advised to buy in at top rates, because the
“ stock was going to £l.” Has a long road to go yet ere
reaching 20s.
Catherine Reef just more than paid its way on
4dwt. stone the last six months. Milling 4230 tons for
10240 z. Bdwt. From the appearances, the mine should see
the div. list again this term.

thumped out £4611 ss. divs. last half, and on Saturday

At Beenak (Vic.) several alleged payabX
deposits of stream tin, assaying up to 78 per cent., are
being opened. But the crowd running the field is more
anxious to sell than to develop.

Great Southern’s six months’ stamping* 336(5
loads, lOlOoz. 9dwt. No div., but the co. has £l3OO in
hand to meet contracts preparing- the mine for another
div. spell. Negotiations for a London float are off.
* * * *
Reefer Lansell expects to strike a “bonanza ” in
his 180 mine. Now down 3350 feet, and so hot that the
men reckon they are within hailing distance of Sheol.
At 3140 ft. a thermal spring was cut, and, as a similar
strike in the deep ground of the Consolidated, Virginia
(Nevada, U.S.) preceded mining £27,000,000 worth of gold
and silver, Bendigonia,ns hope that Lansell may have a
like level. Anyway, his pluck deserves it.
* * * *
Harris’s Reward (Tas.) is .£llOO in debt. To
put the co. on its legs two 6d. calls are necessary. But
the mine on its crushings promises to be a better call-
bleeder than div.-paver. Whether the co. is to go on or
burst will be decided next week.
* * * *
The prospectus of the Newcastle and Stockton
Gold-mining Co. (no liability) appears in this issue. The
co s ground consists of 50 acres on the famous Turon field,
near Wattle Flat (N.S.W.). The capital is £30,000 in
3U,000 £l shares, and the former holders of the property
show their confidence in the undertaking by accepting
payment wholly in shares. With so small a capital to pay
dividends on even a very moderate mine should have a
show. Pleitner, M.E., who gives a good report of the
venture, used to be Mayor of Baker’s Creek in its best
days. The syndicate (mostly experienced working miners)
which formerly held the ground is taking up, in its indi-
vidual capacity, a number of contributing shares in addi-
tion to those allotted in payment for the property.
Dr. Fiaschi, the Italian medico so well-
known in Sydney, is leaving for six months’ nigger-
shooting in Abyssinia—in other words, the clever
doctor who, as an all-round practitioner, has few
rivals in Australia, is going out, from patriotic
motives, as a volunteer in Baldissera’s dishevelled
army. Pity that a man embodying so vast and
rare a store of medical instinct and experience
should be set up as an Aunt Sally for casual black-
fellow’s bullets.
Mr. Hugh Dixson tells The Bulletin that
the Victoria Regia plant itself has not cost him
£5 to raise, apart from his own personal labor; and
for the same amount of the latter he could have
raised twenty specimens. Indeed, he did raise
two, one of which he sent to N.Q. for the purpose
of establishing it in Australia, but the plant, un-
fortunately, did not carry. House and all did not
cost £2OO, and the building was erected largely
with the object of forming as complete a collection
as possible of Australian and New Guinea epiphy-
tal orchids. Strangely enough, such a collection
does not at present exist in any of the Australias.
Apropos of a press statement that Mr.
Hugh Watt (once Home Rule M.P. for Camlachie,
Scotland, who ratted and as a Unionist only
polled 179 votes in ’92) was abusing him in Lon-
don, Premier Kingston has written to the S.A.
papers saying that Mr. Watt offered, when in
Australia, to “make it right” with him (King-
ston) with an offer of £20,000 stock in Watt’s pro-
posed Northern Territory Co. The Premier goes
on to say that he at once showed Mr. Watt the
door, and now publishes a statement of the occur-
rence which he at once committed to writing, and
brought under the notice of several persons, in-
cluding his colleagues, ex-Govemor Kintore, the
leader of the Opposition and the Agent-General.
Sangster, M.L.A. (Vic.), has succeeded,
after much labor, in getting a very unpromising
board created to inquire into the coffin-ships of
Melbourne. And now strange and awful evidence
is_ pouring in about vessels in the coastal trade
with iron plates worn so thin that they can be
crumpled up by hand, rivets that are mere masses
of rust, angle-irons decayed till they are falling in
pieces with their own weight, patched boilers with
rust-holes in them large enough to admit two
fingers, floating cemeteries that are liable to double
up in a heavy sea and drop their cargoes out
through the bottom, and similar horrors.
Up to now, the trouble has been mostly over
a certain well - known collier averred by
the complainants to be chiefly a mass of
rotted plates held together by paint and
force of habit; but one witness alleged that there
are many even worse vessels on the coast. If his
statements prove reliable, some of these other
vessels should be worth seeing. The Vic. Marine
Board, which licenses the alleged floating grave-
yards to go to sea, is mainly a body of persons
interested in the shipping trade. “Noman is a
competent judge in his own cause.”
Poor old Parkes sold oft' his library and
art curiosities the other day, reserving only a few
precious things. The old man was in low water
eight or nine years ago, and his books were pri-
vately sold then ; but afterwards he had a bit of
financial luck, and bought them back again. It is
a queer commentary on men, and books, and
Parkes, that in last week’s sale scores of volumes
came to the hammer uncut and unread, which had
been so sold and bought back, and were once more
sold—still uncut and unread. On the whole,
Parkes got good value for the collection—as
second-hand books go. He hadn’t bought with
much discrimination as to price, and had
generally paid top figures lots of volumes,
for which he gave 12s. and 16s. new, went
for Is. Od. and 2s. 6d. On the other hand, a
few books that he had acquired for half-guineas
fetched guineas —not very many. “ Henry
Parkes ” on the fly-leaf wasn’t worth anything
extra—it was too common. Taking the books all
round, the collection was evidently that of a man
with a taste for reading, who loved books for their
own sake, but was neither student nor collector.
When Parkes had the cash, he plainly enjoyed
spending it lavishly on nicely-bound volumes of
some literary or artistic interest; but he doesn’t
seem to have worried them much. Probably they
gave him a sense of pleasant companionship as he
gazed round the walls, and struck him as a suitable
setting for the Grand Old Make-Believe of Aus-
tralia.

The most important point for the consideration
of all persons who desire to possess photographs
of those nearest and dearest to them is with regard
to permanence rather than price. No photograph
that will turn yellow, break out in spots, or vanish
into nothingness within a few weeks can have its
value expressed in figures. When delivered, it
may shine like the enamelled mystery on a soap-
box, but the seeds of disease are there, and must
come to the surface. This may be regarded as
one of the certainties of cheap photography at uer

SOCIETY
CAMEO
In Mongrelia. On Sunday afternoon a
white woman came on to the pub. verandah with
three piccaninnies, evidently her own. One, half
Chinky, angle-eyed and creamy ; one, half nig.,
splay-footed and snuff-and-butter; one, white en-
tire, snub-nosed and freckled. “One touch of
nature makes the whole world kin.” The coming
Bananalander will have an aunt in Pekin, an
uncle in Fiji, and a cousin in Houndsditch.

* * * *
The Cairns storekeepers always say “ Sir”
to Kanakas.
* * * *
It would surprise most Australians, and a
good many Britishers, to learn who were the two
exalted personages implicated in a recent memor-
able smuggling case. Though a charge of con-
spiracy to defraud the Customs —a more serious one
than would have held against the actual de-
fendant himself—might have been formulated
against these parties, action was not taken, nor
were their names published in any of the papers.
* » * *
The humor of the New Australian fiasco is
gorgeous—and pitiful. One of the pilgrims has,
at latest, run short of clothes for his family—
consequently he is compelled to lock up his
eldest daughter by day m the paternal mansion
of wattle-and-dab, and only allow her out when
the dusk of evening kindly conceals the damsel’s
deficiencies in the matter of raiment. . . The
wife of a prominent Paraguayan agitator left both
her hubby and the settlement the other day, and
ran away with a so-called “fancy man.” Deserters
are only allowed back by the voice of the majority;
consequently, when the erring pair applied for
re-admission into the settlement, the matter was
put to the vote. Paraguayan communistic opinion
views these little lapses very leniently, wherefore
Helen and Paris were received back joyfully into
the bosom of the social family—the husband voting
in favor thereof. Surely magnanimity could go
no further.
Rev. Rentoul, recently discoursing before
Melb. Presbyterian Assembly, viciously denounced
“Trilby” as the record of the “adventures of
three drunken artists with a naked wumman,” and
advised everybody in search of light and whole-
some literature to purchase Prof. Harper’s inter-
esting worruk on Deuteronomy! By the way,
readers of “Trilby” have always been of the
opinion that only one of the three artists was
drunk at all, and then only on one occasion.
There’s nothing in it with the rev. fibster when he
gets going on a high-gear.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey, at the Melb. Oddfellows’
banquet, spoke of “ the powers and possibilities of
well-directed self-help.” Judge Molesworth had
something to say upon the same subject during the
City of Melbourne Bank enquiry. ‘ ‘ Help your-
selves, ” says Brassey. “You have helped your-
selves,” says Molesworth, J., to the overdrafting
City Bank directors.
Melb. Anti-Sweating League has discovered
a woman who earned at the rate of 6s. 9d. for 66
hours’ work, and has been cut by the sweater, so
that her earnings for the same time would be 4s.
“Let ’em sweat,” says the Fat ring in the Leg.
Council.
The “official Catholic” is
becoming a wondrous spectacle of
untraditional grovel. Having
evidently no knowledge of what
is the right thing to be done
in the august presence of a
“prince of the church,” he
falls into the most inglorious
attitudes in the presence of
Cardinal Moran. At a
recent meeting at th 6
Australia Hotel, Sydney,
in connection with the
formation of an Irish rifle
corps, a full display of
the peculiar tactics of
these gentry was given.
The Cardinal stood in
the centre of the room,
attired in a red robe and
skull cap, and as each of
the big “ lay ” guns en-
tered he approached the
princely figure, took the
august hand, and, kneel-
ing on one knee, bent
over the hand as if mak-
ing a minute examina-
tion of the apostolic
finger-nails. In the real
Court ceremonials the big-wigs of
ceremony confer honor in tliei r trained genuflections,
the result of mutual pride in both the groveller and
the grovellee; but the wild, untrained, Brumby-
like attempts at the manners of an ecclesiastical
court by the Australian “ official” Catholic make
ocoders squirm in agony. One notable excep-
tion on this occasion was a well-known maker of
long and dismal speeches (“ It is my sacred and
solemn jooty ”), who entered the room, manfully
sailed up to the Cardinal, shook him warmly by
the hand, and passed on without deflecting one
inch from the perpendicular.
* * * *
The Australasian, after publishing photos,
of sundry Melbourne city barmaids, is going to the
suburbs for Hebe subjects. The efforts of what
used to be “ high-toned ” journalism to maintain
its circulation are rather pathetic. In about an-
other six months’ time, the Australasian will
have arrived at Kitty, the damsel to whom Bird
J. P., used to offer advice gratis. She was formerly
in the business.
A sensational divorce-case is threatening
Wellington (M.L.) The peculiarity about it is
that there are said to be a round dozen ot co-
respondents. But only one or two are to be
“made an example of.”
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — lie a recent case at
Sydney Central, isn’t it a bit rough that a man
should be charged as a vagrant because he is an
opium-smoker ? Boozers are not lugged out of
pubs, and charged, quoad boozers, with ‘ in-
sufficient lawful.” Official hypocrisy over this
opium business is sickening. If the whole com-
munity used the drug, it wouldn’t kill one person
for fifty that are slaughtered by the compounds
which, with only the feeblest pretence at restric-
tion, are vended all over Sydney as decent alco-
holic Iiquor.—MACASSAR.
* * * *
Mrs. Mackin, the Sydney lady who fired
six shots at a burglar and then hurled the empty
revolver after his retreating form, is remembered
by many Melbourne friends as a game young
sportswoman. Her father, the original Mark
Foy, had a place down at Mordialloc, where Alice
Foy (now Mrs. M.) used to shoot, swim, ride, row
and fish equal to any two ordinary amazons rolled
into one. There were no flies on Miss Alice. She
came forth, in trousers, and hauled the boat out
to sea when she wanted to go fishing. Lucky
thing for the burglar that the lady of the house
wasn’t armed with an axe. He’d have been be-
headed on the spot.
* * • *
The people of Nauru, in the Marshall
Islands —according to Dr. Irmer, the German
Captain-General—are mentally and physically the
finest he has met with in the Pacific. “ The mis-
sionaries, however, have never been able to do
anything with them,” and—is this cause and effect?
—“crime is unknown in the island.” But no
doubt, in the long run, the “ gawspel ” will get
there and change all that.
* * * *
Residents of Manly, Australia’s most charm-
ing watering-place, don’t take kindly to the present
ridiculously low steamboat-fares. What they want
is a fair thing only. Under the present regime,
“Our Village” seems to an outsider to consist
principally of scantily-clad youngsters, “ spoon-
ing ” couples, waste-paper, larrikins, and banana-
peel.
• • * *
Holdar M. : About the reputed “casual-
ness ”of the native inhabitant. The other day,
in Sydney, a “bloke"and his “bit” boldh
preached a matrimonial agency. Arrived at t.. 0
portal of this soul-uniting sanctum, whereon is
invitingly inscribed, “ Enter without knocking ”
young Lochmvar s grit seemed to fail him some-
what. However, confidence being whispered
into his ear by his undaunted charmer, he
ascended the balcony steps, adjusting a gor-
geous neck-tie by way of screwing up his
determination, and vanished, leaving fair Ellen
on the footpath meditatively consuming a
bag of black grapes and nonchalantly swinging on
her heel. The blissful preliminaries arranged, the
bridegroom from Surry Hills re-appeared on the
balcony and emitted a curt, triumphant whistle,
signalising to his choice to accompany him inside.
This she proceeded to do, after stooping uncon-
cernedly to recover a few grapes that had escaped
from a bunch she was at the moment working
upon. Whom the agent hath joined, let no Win-
deyer put asunder.
* * * *
D.F. : “ Interesting problem for a Demo-
cratic community. By what right do students
from the medical schools ‘ walk the hospitals ? ’ &c.
Could a patient sue a student who felt his pulse or
thumped his or her chest, say, while in an uncon-
scious state, for assault, criminally or civilly ? Are
hospital institutions supported by the rich to pro-
vide a poor ‘corpus vile’ on which their own
future medical attendants may try their prentice
hands ? If the exhibition of patients to pawing
students is within the law, is not the submission
to it a ‘ consideration ’ for any services rendered
by the hospital, and accordingly the word ‘ charity ’
a cruel misnomer ? ”
Dear Bulletin, —Do you know that an
Irish curse, however forcible in timbre, will not
work unless “bare flesh touches the ground”?
Recently I saw a young woman, wife of a Gipps-
land selector, not long from the “ Ould Sod,” cast
a long, black curse on a bailiff put in by a store-
keeper. She rushed out in a frenzy, tore down
her stockings, and, flopping on her knees, sent up
one of the most vivid and picturesque anathemas
it has been my happy lot to listen to. lam fol-
lowing the career of that bailiff with much
interest. —S.S.
* * * *
The bad times have seriously affected the
clergy of at least one church. Forty per cent, of
the Wesleyan ministers have, during the past year,
received only from <£l2o to <£l6o. The minimum
salary is supposed to be <£22o, and until lately any
deficiency was voted out of the connectional fund ;
but, owing to the heavy drain, this has now been
stopped.
* * * *
The Salvation Army leaders, terrified
apparently by the rebellion of Ballington Booth,
have lately decided not to leave their officials too
long in one province. Consequently, a large
“ swap ” of blood-and-glory barrackers is just
taking place between Maoriland and Queensland.

Dear Bulletin, —
“ Dr. Brigham Brown’s ”
assailants display a
lamentable lack of per-
spicacity in assuming
that a writer of such
comprehensive in te 11 i -
gence would advocate
excessive sexuality as a
manly and wholesome
thing. He uses the word
“ lust,” I take it, in the (literal)
sense which is better exemplified in its
derivative “ lusty ” robustly apprecia-
tive of robust enjoyments; prone to
satisfy normal appetites in the normal
manner which in no way involves the
“ diseased sexual brain-centre ” of
“H. H. Champion,” who, by the
way, writes as if satyriasis—an ex-
tremely rare acute affection—were
quite an ordinary result of chronic
excess, and who, before he talked
about “scientific smatterings,”
should have been aware that, in
the words of the greatest Eng-
lish nerve-specialist, “ One man
x will sustain with little or no in-
jury an amount of sexual in-
dulgence which would infallibly
land another in the lunatic asylum.
Excess for one man is so far from
being excess for another that it is
impossible to lay down any satis-
factory general rule.” — Pagan.
Gov. Buxton’s two daughters
lately received bicycles from England,
but they have not ridden so far.
What enormous profits the Chinese must
make in selling opium to blacks and others ! A
Winton (Q.) Chinaman has paid over £7O in fines
within the last six months for the aforesaid
offence. And still he continues to flourish.

procession on St. Patrick’s Day were borrowed
from a local Irish undertaker. Here and there a
spectator would involuntarily raise his hat as the
long-tailed steeds walked slowly by, with a lorry
d banner in tow.

NO ADMITTANCE
On a day that would an iguana roast,
A Westralian boomer gave up the ghost.
Which flew Heav’nward, bold and hardy.
St. Peter gazed at the ghost, irate.
And sternly said, as he slammed the gate—
“ You can’t come here—you should know tnar
well !
We’ve lost our prejudice against Helly-^
But we draw the line at Coolgardie.
THE WIDOWER.
“ There comes a sound of marriage bells.”—-Tennyson
“ Back from the party, hot and tired !
Come here, I want to play with you;
Sit on my knee, you little rogue,
And tell how passed the day with you.
“ The pretty lady that I know
Said you’d a winning way with you !
Shall papa go and ask her, then,
To come and always stay with you ? ”
“ That will be nice ; well have such games !
And when I’m tired she’ll carry me ;
And I can ask her if she’ll wait
Till I grow big, and marry me.
“ And yet I’m not so very sure :
One funny way she’s got with her
Is that she seems to love me more
Whenever you are not with her.”
“ I think you’d better go to bed ;
But, if you really care for her,
Come, Curly-locks, and kneel by me,
And say a pretty prayer for her. ”
Melb.
R. K. DEE.

Ladies Benevolent Societies in Melbourne
suburbs seem in no small degree to exist just now
tor the purpose of enabling poor women to hang
on as the slaves of the sweater. Legree now-a-
days doesn t have to feed his nigger—Charity does
that for him.
Miss Olive de
Hugard, winner of the
recent exhibition in
pianoforte - playing at
Melb. Conservatoriurn of
Music, deserves more
encouragement from the
so-called influential pa-
trons of native talent
than she has received
hitherto. Born at Ben-
digo some 18 years ago,
this pretty young artist
scored her earliest
triumph at the Melb.
Juvenile Exhibition
(1880), which served also
to bring Lalla Miranda
and George Majeroni
into notice. Little Olive
easily captured first award
in the “ under 14 ” piano-
forte competition, and
several subsequent suc-
cesses in juvenile con-
tests at Ballarat, Bendigo
and elsewhere endorsed
the good opinion of her
first judges. A more
weighty testimonial to
her talent came from
Charles Halle, who, in
a private letter to a
friend, recommended the
immediate despatch of
Miss de Hugard to
Europe. Also Gov. Pre-
datoros Robinson, with
his cultivated taste in
music and keen eye for a
charming face, tried to
advance the damsel’s
cause by speaking to
Lady Hopetoun and Pre-
mier Patterson about
her. He might as well
have poured his enthusi-
asm into the ears of two
embalmed Pharaohs.
Miss de Hugard was
still on the right side of 16 when she
won a Melb. scholarship entitling her to a
term of tuition from Herr Pabst, and under the
care of that cold, classical professor she advanced
rapidly. Pabst brought her out in public as
his “ star ” girl-pupil about the same time that
he launched Percy Grainger as a boy pheno-
menon. Percy was taken up by sassiety and sent to
Europe. The gifted Miss Olive got left, and little
more was heard of her, after Pabst’s departure,
until she came along to the Conservatoriurn, the
other week, and won the exhibition aforesaid.
Thus far, the subject of this notice h is not been
a patient plodder at the piano. Herr Pabst,
whilst warmly admiring her genius for the instru-
ment, was apt to complain that his brilliant pupil
showed a wayward spirit and lacked boy Grainger’s
pertinacity. But Miss de Hugard, at 18, seems
very much in earnest. For some time past she
has been a State-school teacher at Mentone
(Melb.) with little time to spare for music;
and now that she has obtained a year’s
leave of absence from the school in order
to attend the Conservatoriurn the damsel intends
to love her piano as a—well, say, brother. Miss
de Hugard’s face mirrors an artistic soul. Her
talent and skill as a pianiste are proved. She
may win renown if she will. The Bulletin hopes
that this fair young daughter of the soil will not
blight her promising career by an early attachment
for any commonplace creature of the opposite sex.
How many bright girls have sacrificed a first-class
future at the altar of Augustus, and permitted a
waxy moustache to brush away an admirable
ambition ?
* * * *
Some of the banners of the Irish friendly
societies need repainting badly. There was one
large patriotic banner in the Melb. St. Patrick’s
Day procession which bore a victorious emblem
representing portraits of Parnell and Glad-
stone linked together by a scroll, whereon
were painted in large letters the words “ Home
Rule.” A banner like that is about comparable
to a banner inscribed “ Sedan ” in a Parisian pro-
session. For Parnell is dead, Gladstone is retired,
and Home Rule is—well, where is it ?
* * * *
The blessings of free education are highly
prized in Bananaland. The other day subscrip-
tions were solicited for improvements to the
Central schools, Brisbane. Scores and scores of
well-to-do parents contributed nothing, ,or next to
nothing ; but a poor washerwoman shelled out 10s.,
and a Chinaman dumped down a pound like a
—well, “ white man.”
* * * «
Last week at Quarter Sessions, N.S.W.,
Acting-Judge Rogers sentenced a youth to a year’s
imprisonment under separate treatment, saying he
did not wish him to' mix with criminals. The
prisoner begged the Judge to give him two years.
“ Why,? ” asked his Honor. “ I’ll be half-starved,
your Honor,” was the reply. “ Every second
week for the whole time, I’ll get nothing to eat
but bread and water.” The Judge enquired, and
found the lad was right, but he did not alter the
sentence. The law and the gaol regulations only
gave him the choice between herding the youth
up with old criminals and half-starving him
(starvation in youth is one of the most powerful
factors of insanity), and he chose the latter.
Perhaps he was right; but The Bulletin would
like to know who is responsible for such insanely
brutal regulations—regulations at utter variance
with the commonest facts known to medical
science.

The annual Assembly of N.S. Wales
Presbyterians has earned such a reputation for
prayerful belligerence that Sydney dailies always
send their funny paragraphists along, in place of
the cut-and-dried reporter, to write up the pro-
ceedings.

can’t keep this pace up. Sooner or later they will

paper with a St. Petersburg correspondent,
appears to have recently retrenched in that

A Victorian cleric
of the “age of consent”
species says : “No
young woman who re-
spects herself would be
alone with a member
of the opposite sex.”
Only a young woman
who was always occu-
pied with dirty thoughts
about herself and the
opposite sex would act
on such a rule. If a
young woman must
never be alone with a
man because it might
give opportunity for
what the prurient old
maid and the unclean
parson are always thi nk-
mg about, then no bish-
op who respected him-
self would ever be alone
with anybody else’s
money lest he should
steal it, or with a plug
of tobacco lest he might
chew it, or with another
bishop lest it might ’e id
to something too hor-
rible to mention. After
reading some recent re-
velations the thought of
what might happen if
one bishop was left
alone with another
bishop is simply awful.

mate that the public is tired of the age of

the 160th time it creates a sick feeling like
that which arises when one meets a combined

A doleful article on the future prospects of
the Melb. junior bar appeared in a recent law-
paper. The “ amalgam ”is alleged to be driving
out all except those who have long purses to
draw from. The poor man has little chance under
the new system. It makes articles compulsory,
and <£42o is not an unusual figure for this luxury
—so the law-paper says.
* * * *
One of Auctioneer Kinsman’s marriages
came to a curious conclusion the other day. It
was a case of four years ago-where the husband, a
youth of 20, was arrested and convicted of abduct-
mg his wife, a maiden of 16—for marrying whom
Kinsman was fined £5O. The husband was re-
leased to come up for judgment when or if called
upon. Deeming discretion the better part of
valour, or repenting his bargain, he cleared out
without again seeing his wife, and has remained
away ever since. Here is a maid, wife, and
divorcee at 20.

Melbourne Argus says of the money-lend-
ing business : “ The simpleton who is refused a
loan is fortunate compared with his brother
simpleton who gets one. The latter victim’s ex-
periences resemble nothing so much as those of an
animal in one of the famous pig-killing factories of
Chicago, which goes in squealing at one end of the
process and emerges in the shape of pork-sausages,
at the other. ” But is this original ? Does a Tory
newspaper ever point a moral with a happy simile
“on its own ” ? And porUsa usages out of a
pawnshop !
* * * *
The wife of a Sydney brewery employe
has, in three confinements, launched into this
golden land seven children ; twins on the first and
second occasions, triplets on the third. And all
are “doing well ” —except possibly the old man.
* * * *
New idea for annihilating criminals evolved
out of the inner consciousness of a sugar-boiler :
Gently place the patient in a vacuum pan, six
strokes of the pump, and whe-e-ew ! Chemically
examine the exhaust for traces of the immortal
soul.
* * * *
Another use for the bicycle. On a part-
Sydney-owned sheep-station in the far North-West
(N.S.W.) the resident partner and manager found
himself travelling alongside back-fences while he
was learning to ride his imported bike. Thushetook
to doing odd boundary-riding jobs on that station,
and then passed onto boundary-riding as a regular
thing. Present intention is to import three or
four machines, including one for the station
manager’s wife, which lady is expected to combine-
pleasure and business. Punctures may be numer-
ous ; but that will be a small matter beside the
cost of imported chaff in that arid region—neces-
sary during part of practically every year for the
working horses, and very rarely running into less
than £2O per ton delivered. On several Western
stations the use of telephones—fencing-wire tele-
phones—has for a considerable time past reduced
the number of both horses and men.
* * * *
Most of the big Australian coasting
steamers now carry a compositor (at £5 a month)
and a printing plant. It is the type-snatcher’s
duty to print the menu for each meal. “ Between
whiles ” he is supposed to help the stewards.
* * * *
A Maoriland publican was served the other
day with a prohibition order against himself.
And the downtrodden grogist had to solemnly
paste up in his own bar the intimation that if he
was found supplying himself with liquor during
the space of so many months, he would be fined
according to law.

Mrs. HARRY MARSHALL.
There is evidently nothing in the point
that an alien sat on the jury which convicted four
of the Daily Post defendants ; but several high-
class lawyers think the imprisoned men must get
out on the ground that Judge Rogers was wrong
in ruling that an accused person could either make
a statement or give evidence, but that he could
not do both.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.
