Sydney, March 24, 1896.
Mr Dear Moorabinda—
Wallace Brownlow’s benefit matinee, at Sydney
Lyceum, was a S.R.O. proceeding, the general
effect being enhanced by the drapery of flags,
laurel-wreaths and life-belts which adorned the
gallery in honor of the shipping-companies’
theatre-party the same evening. But I felt much
safer with those life-belts hanging round. You
see it was Brownlow’s farewell matinee, and if the
tears of the matinee girl had flooded the play-
house those belts might have saved many lives.
The programme proved a great draw, beginning
with the piping-hot act of “Rosette,” with Mrs.
Courtice Pounds making her Australian debut as
Corisandre. Mrs. C.P. wore three feathers in her
hair, and they made her look far less modern than
Flo. Young does in the same dress.
The draw of the bill was the temptation scene
from “Ivanhoe,” with Mr. Brownlow and Miss
Young as the Templar and Rebecca, and two
minor characters to square the party. The battle-
ments were correctly staged, and Leon Caron’s
orchestral scoring of the difficult music was no
small factor in the success of the item. Miss
Young, in white over a rather coquettish pink
lining and ropes of barbaric ornaments, played
Rebecca in her own dark hair, and made a pic-
turesque ensemble poised on the battlements.
Here and there her conscientious singing of the
very arduous soprano music rather suggested a
tour de force, but those who remembered Miss
Young’s success as Michaela in “Carmen,” with
Annis Montague in the title - rdle, were not
surprised at the ease with which she assumed the
serious grand-opera manner necessary in “Ivan-
hoe.” Mr. Brownlow played the Templar in a
rather tangled black wig, and attacked his music
furiously as befitted a doubly-dyed villain who
woos his love “as the lion woos his tawny mate”
—and generally gave that lawless knight his head,
familiarly speaking. Applause and bouquets fell
at the end of the act, and, after the curtain had
been hauled up three times, Mr. Brownlow made
the customary speech, in which, as he didn’t
equivocally assert that he was “too full for words”
after the manner of beneficiaries, I only objected
to his statement that “ grand-opera doesn’t make
you laugh.” Now, I think it all depends on how
deep the lace-frill is on the short fat tenor’s velvet
knickers and whether his hair has a borrowed
appearance. I have always thought that the
short step from the sublime to the ridiculous was
much shorter than usual when you got to grand-
opera.
Judging by press notices in British newspapers
which reach Australia, Miss Ada Crossley is reap-
ing golden opinions on her “rare sweet con-
tralto,” and warm welcomes are promised her in
advance from every place she has ever visited,
which looks a very encouraging sign. Australians
will be pleased to hear of Miss Crossley’s success
in climbing the artistic tree, and—as she intends
to stick to concert work and abjures opera—with-
out showing her stockings, either. But then the
typical contralto is a lot too fat to be shapely.
The concert on St. Patrick’s night in Sydney
Centennial Hall had Gov. Hampden, the inde-
fatigable, in the front row, and a glittering array
of talent on the stage —a debutante contralto, who
was too nervous to do herself justice, supplying an
artistic touch of green to the programme. The
feature of the audience was a festive youth in the
gallery pelting rolled-up bits of paper at a lady
underneath. A great many people lost a lot of the
national music in their anxiety to see how many
times he hit her on an average.
Gov. Hampden was represented by a broker at
the sale of bridegroom Parkes’s library, the other
day, and saved his pocket considerably thereby.
If anybody connected with Guvmentouse had
turned up to bid, the public would have expected
great munificence—there is a strong feeling that
the Governor always gives top prices. Poor Lady
Duff was often in despair over the figures she was
obliged to pay. “ How much do you give for a
fowl ? ” she once asked a Sydney medico’s wife,
and, when she was told, said, simply, “ How they
do rob us ! ” Yice-royalty may obviate this by
purchasing its chops incog., which brings me back
to my point. His Ex.’s purchases by proxy at the
Parkes sale look as if the Gov. means to become a
painfully accurate authority on Australian ancient
history.
People say that at least one of the Australian
Governors should get a reward for thrift, not only
on account of the strict watch he keeps on his own
expenditure, but also for the absolute genius he
displays in’seeing that the bleeding country is not
overcharged for tacks. The regilding of his par-
ticular residence brought out H.E.’s talent for
knowing to a nicety how far the State is liable for
furniture-polish.
St. Vincent’s Hospital ball is going to be a
dazzling one this year under the eyes of Gov. and
Lady Hampden, Gov. and Lady Brassey, and
other visiting Governors who cannot be definitely
alluded to at present (though Gov. Buxton is
known to be a starter). All the consuls and, of
course, a large naval contingent (St. Vincent’s is
popular with the navy) intend to be present, and
a committee of ladies have the poultry supper in
hand. Now that the School of Industry ball is a
thing of the past, St. Vincent’s is the oldest
charity ball of the lot. Miss F. Doyle and Miss
Armstrong are hon. secs.
Naval men certainly do adorn a ball-room, but
they are far from being the grown-up “ catchee-
catchee ” spoken of in “Pinafore.” Many a
Society mother grieves over her grass-widowed
daughter, occasional wife of a roving loo’tenant
whose infant child calls him “that man” on his
brief visits to his home on shore. And yet, though
the lot of Captain Corcoran’s bride is one of the
most trying fates a flesh-and-blood damsel can be
condemned to, you still find Sassiety twining
garlands in his hair, so to speak, and using all its
wiles to lure him to the altar.
A society marriage on the tapis in Sydney
is a case in point. The bridegroom’s barge of
battle has only three days in port, and into these
three days the hero must cram a wedding and a
honeymoon and sail away again on the third day—
yet the damsel is willing to bolt her “ hour ” in
this hurried fashion, though the misogynistic, un-
sympathetic admiral—who is openly alluded to as
a “ wretch ” by the very best society— comes in for
a good deal of polite objurgation for not giving
the bridal roses at least a week to wither in. But
the grass-widower Rear-Admiral has no sympathy
with Love’s young dream. On dit when he sus-
pects that one of his officers is taken that way be
immediately sets him hard tasks, as if it were
possible to work the complaint out of the system.
I was astonished the other day by the pyramids
of bulky MS. which over-ran The Bulletin
sanctum, and, on enquiring, found that these piles
of literature were the outpourings of a locust
swarm of correspondents desirous of publicly con-
tinuing the polygamy-cwm-polyandry discussion.
There were so many of them that it was made quite
plain that polygamy and polyandry are the sub-
jects nearest and dearest to an enormous number
of my fellow-citizens. But it’s all vanity. No
man or woman ever learns from any other man’s or
woman’s experience of the opposite sex. They
may think they do, but they don’t, and that, I
presume, is why in the office of this scurrilous
paper there seems a strong desire to branch off on
a more practical theme—such, for instance, as
“ Why do young ladies want to teach Chinamen?”
Divorce proceedings are occasionally picturesque
and, sometimes, even humorous. In one fresh
Sydney divorce-case, the respondent’s most brutal
act was averred to Be “trampling on petitioner’s
wedding-dress,” and yet, after jumping on his
wife’s wardrobe with hobnailed boots, he pretended
to drown himself, because “her extravagance was
ruining him.” The alleged drowning-attempt was
very funny. He kissed his little daughter, and,
remarking tragically, “Good-bye for ever!”
rushed down and heaved a huge stone into the
harbor, and then lay low on the grass. Then he
spoilt a fine tragedy and a robust evening-paper
sensation by going home meekly to tea.
There was more picturesqueness about the
divorce of a Sydney solicitor and his wife, in
which the chief alleged-cruelty exhibit was that
he had deliberately set fire to her nightdress while
she was on her knees, engaged in prayer. This is
indeed an up-to-date form of arson. If it gains
ground, it will probably become fashionable for
the fond papa to take out a fire-policy on each of
his married daughters.
Apropos kneeling, there was a notable incident
at the recent Irish sports, during the girls’ dancing-
contest. Father O’Flynn and a long row of his
brethren were in the front row of the ring, and,
anxiously desiring to give those behind them a
chance of seeing the fun, they all dropped on their
knees. The row of kneeling priests looked, for all
the world, like worshippers at the golden calf—the
appearance in the arena of a girl in long yellow
hose giving color to the—but, no, I am afraid that
fiction won’t do. I wanted to round off a neat
allusion to golden calves, but whoever would
seriously credit a girl in yellow stockings at an
emerald green Oirish fete ?

commonly reported, Shakespeare to the contrary,
that most of the friction arises from the pecking
and sparring of the only fat fledgling m the
clutch.
“History repeats itself. Once before a Vic.
Governor’s wife braved old Ocean all on account
of a susceptible cub, suspected of a weakness for a
fair subject of his vice-regal Drother-in-law s
roughly speaking, old enough to be his grand-
mother. In the present instance, matters have
not reached the acute stage, but in the ‘ inner
circle ’ there are whispers of parental rebukes and
curtain-lectures in high life, provoked by the
suspected penchant of a mild matrimonial vassal
for the mature charms of a local society leader.
“The latest society ‘Dreadful’ is the Trilby
picnic, located by bush or beach, as convenient,
the one thing needful being water in the vicinity.
At a certain stage of the revelry the hostess
usually announces to her guests, with the impres-
sive air of one breaking bad news, that she’s most
awfully sorry, but there’s a creek to cross, and the
only plan is to take off shoes and wade.
In view of the prevalence of these entertain-
ments, there would seem to be an opening in
Melbourne for a thoroughly competent pedicure.
The effects of pointed toes and high heels cannot
be undone in a day, and few modern maidens feel
as confident in their nethermost charms as that
16th century French damsel of high degree who
wagered that his frisky highness, Henri Quart,
would kiss her bare foot before sunset. Then she
tripped forth en paysdnnc, a milk-pail balanced
on her head, and her-- —

“ The unco yuid Anglican parson who fell foul of
dancing in a recent pulpit frenzy, is, as his
parishioners know to their sorrow, a typical
specimen of the lachrymose snuffiebuster. As
shepherd of a Melb. suburban fold, he went out of
his way to collide oratorically with the corns of
the congregation. All remonstrances met with
the same aggravating response, ‘ My friend ! your
frame of mind is far from Christian ; until you
have humbled your spirit with prayer, not forget-
ting strong crying and tears, I must refuse to
confer with you ! ’
“ The divine Sarah has confidentially informed
half-a-hundred interviewers that a favorite trick
of hers is to wear, when acting, the most magnifi-
cent clothes and jewels, and to do her best to rise
superior to them. And in this she generally suc-
ceeds. The gorgeous appearance on her entry
often ‘ hits her audience between the eyes, and
makes it gasp,’ but the superb millinery is soon
forgotten in the genius of the acting. In ‘ Fe-
dora, ’ Mrs. Brough evidently emulates the great
Frenchwoman—for more splendid gowns than the
Russian princess wears have seldom been seen
here. But does she rise superior to them ? Hardly,
I think. Even when, torn between love and re-
venge, she wrests the secret of Vladimir’s murder
from Ipanoff, one is almost too conscious of the
fact that she is looking extremely smart in ame-
thyst wall-paper brocade tres decollete, set off with
touches of Black. In Act I. a voluminous! y-
trained cream brocade skirt, with bodice of sable,
gives her quite the appearance of the Russian
princess of fiction—the only real, live Russian
princess I ever saw, by the way, wore per-
petual cashmere, prunella boots, and a mus-
tard - colored wig always slightly awry A
‘poem’ in shimmering yellow crepe de" Chine
trimmed slightly with iridescent ‘fish-scale’ em-
broidery is worn in the great third act, and the
unfortunate princess dies in black crepe flashing at
hem and wrists with gold and bronze ‘fish-
scales. Through the whole play Mrs. B. wears
her beautiful hair parted in the centre, much irisi
and drawn slightly over the ears, and in the two
first acts she sports a fine tiara of diamonds. Her
cloak is of steel-grey silk, brocaded with primroses
and lined with sable, and it has vast sleeves of
black velvet edged with fur. Countess Olga Faber
never looked prettier than she does inthebuttercup-
brocade gown finished with turquoise-blue velvet
and old lace, which she wears in the salon in the
second act. In the third act she accepts “Jon"
de Sealingwax,” as he calls himself, in brown
crepon striped with blue with bodice of blue
velvet, and beefeater hat of brown and blue.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Sydney Cup is, at time of writing, very
likely to be won by either of two horses from Victoria—•
the trusty Wallace or the roguish Music. Given the
latter at the post in good condition and a trying mood, he
would certainly shake the stake. The Music that came
with a rattle after Hova at the end of the Flemington
Stakes, that made a remarkable run, after lagging in the
extreme rear, for the Melbourne Cup, and subsequently
won the Williamstown Cup in greased-lightning style,
was a brilliant horse when so disposed. Music’s weight
in the Randwick event is 31b. less than he carried at Wil-
liamstown, and, if he goes for it, the “ books,” who have
good memories for sensational performances, will save
him for themselves as much as possible.
Wallace’s running into a place seems to be
merely a matter of health and freedom from “ interfer-
ence.” Interference is the dangerous animal which beats
so many first favorites on Australian racecourses, but if
Wallace can get away from him the St. Alban’s colt
should finish first or second—always supposing that the
weather gives the fastest horse a fair chance to do his
best. Wallace was not quite as hard as he might have
been at Flemington the other week, nor did James Gough
start to punish his mount quite soon enough. If the
horse improves 71b. during March, which he is likely to
do, he will be right among the front rank half-a-mile from
home, and then the others can say their prayers. Mean-
while, there is no price about Wallace. 5 to 1, with a
several days’ chance of a “going wrong” against the
backer, is a worse investment than 5 to 2 on the day of
the race.
* * * *
The ring is as chary about laying Wallace for
Sydney Cup as it was about his sire years back. Being
marketable for a very limited amount, his win wouldn’t
put much of a crimp in their bank-notes.
» * * •
The season’s record up to date shows that none
of the “ fashionable” Victorian jockeys have won oftener
than once in every five tries, or thereabouts. Bob Lewis’s
figures are 165 mounts for 32 wins. Herbert Holmes has
almost as good an average for 105 mounts, and W. H.
Pearson’s average (113 mounts) is fractionally better.
These three appear to have yielded their “followers” a
decent amount of fun for the money, however, inasmuch
as they have each run into a place about as often as they
finished “ nowhere.”
* * * *
Imported Van Cleve, sire of champion trotter
Fritz, stood the season at Hobartville before Mr. Buckland
got him, but he never got a trotter worthy the name, nor
has he sired anything out of the common beyond the
flyers handled by Mr. Buckland from their infancy; from
which it would appear that early instruction is as much a
factor as breeding in the making of champions. Mr.
Buckland has kindergarten tracks at Wonbobbia, and the
handling and instruction of the youngsters commences at
a very early age.
'* * » *
The big Indian event, the next Viceroy’s Cup,
promises to be of unusual interest to Australian horsemen.
Quiver has been bought with the special object of annex-
ing the Cup, and reports are going that Hova’s owners
intend taking him across the pond for the event.
* * * *
Young Bryan, who took to Maoriland the Syd-
ney trotters Pastime and a fine colt by Era, has ind'e d
had bid luck. Pastime, who showed half-miles in 1.15,
has never won a race ; and the colt, after breaking a bit
and having a miraculous escape from eternal smash, has
now died of colic.
* * » »
Ebor and Norton both ran in England last
month. The Australian horse with list. 121 b. up finished
seventh for a Kempton Park hurdle race, and the Maori-
lander carried list. 61b. nowhere in a small field for a
steeplechase at Sandown Park.
* * • •
The prime reason why the three-year-old Damien
is sneakingly regarded in Melb. as a good outsider for
Sydney Cup is that he has a racy appearance and is in the
dangerous stable of J. Wilson, jun., owner of Newhaven,
and, till lately, owner of Quiver. Kangulandai, a two-
year-old stable-mate, may pick up a crumb during the
meeting.
* * * *
Grandmaster, sire of so many good ’uns—most
of them, unfortunately, gelded—is now 28 years old, and
as blind as a bat.
* » » *
A related Doncaster -S. Cup double —Patrona
and Patroness.
» • » *
Eleven three-year-olds have annexed Sydney
Cup, the majority carrying light jocks. Wallace carries
8.12 ; his sire won with 9st.
» * * *
The promising Hotchkiss horse, True Blue, is
one of the sort likely to make his mark in the near future.
Having undoubted pace, he may prove in Hooke’s stable
a worthy successor to the retired Jeweller. His nomina-
tion against the cracks for Randwick w.f.a. events indi-
cates a strong belief in him.
* * * *
Unluckily, unreliable Response, in great fix just
now, is one of the best-treated mares in Doncaster and
Cup, but she has a knack of just missing races, no matter
whether the company is good or bad. She’d make ago of
it with a ’bus-horse up the straight, and then manage
to get beaten somehow.
* » » *
Stanbury left for England on Saturday per Aus-
tral to fulfil his engagement with the English champion,
Harding. Harding must be a good little ’un, but it is i
physical impossibility for a 9sst. man to extend such a
man as Stanbury— i.e., of course, the Stanbury we used tc
know. Hanlan may be urged as to what a little man can
do, but it must be remembered that even Hanlan weighed
the best part of list, in condition, and at the period of
his victories was the only man in the world who knew how
to utilise the sliding-seat.
* * * *
The quarrel between the Victorian and N.S.W.
rowing associations, as to whethei a man who is an amateui
under Victorian and a professional under N.S.W. rules
shall be allowed to row in the Victorian representative
boat, is on all-fours with the dispute about equalising the
franchise under which provincial representatives shall be
elected to the Federal Convention.
* * * *
The pigheadedness of the N.S.W. Rowing Asso-
ciation over the miserable Dawson squabble will meet
its just reward if Vic., Q., and Tas. ignore the older
province and race “ on their own ” upon the Yarra.
* * * *
It is 11 years since Tasmania competed in the
interprovincial-eight event, but it is thought the required
exes, will be forthcoming for representation on the Parra-
matta in May. Tas. has the makings of a stronger crew
than usual.
* * * *
Some trouble may be experienced in satisfac-
torily selecting the N.S.W. four-oar crew. There are a
number of first-class men—enough to form two good
crews, and as the regatta is held locally there’s no reason
why the association shouldn’t be doubly represented.
* » * *
Melb. bookies complain of the times, and of
being struck so often lately. The reason is that some are
backers one race and layers the next. The penciller of
to-day isn’t a man of risks. If a backer gets a leg-in in a
paltry £2O double the bookie haunts him until he lays
some of it back.
* * * *
The conservative notion re the press has been
partially abandoned by the V.R.C., who now supply more
information concerning inquiries than heretofore. Yet
Melb. dailies don’t give the names of bookie witnesses,
“ well-known backers,” or anybody. So that if reporters
were admitted to the star chamber they probably wouldn’t
say more than they do under the new order. The public
only want to know if the investigations are conducted
squarely.

Personal Items.
Noticeable that parents now shun naming
their male offspring “Oscar.” It’s now not
reckoned good enough for a dog.
One Bismarck Brown gave evidence in a
false-pretences case, at Melb., last week, and
Themistocles J ones was fined the same morning.
Said that Lord and Lady Drylaw (chrysalis
name, Loch) are threatening a visit to Australia
m order to see how successor Brassey and friend
Hampden are jogging along.
Premier Turner is a chronic sufferer from
rheumatic gout. This, coupled with the unceasing
strain of overwork, has completely knocked him
out, yet he persists in taking no holiday.
Gov. Brassey’s crushed secretary, Walling-
ton, has obtained an appointment in England,
and the shadow of his famous nose will not float
upon the floors of Melb. Guv’ment House much
longer.
F. C. B. Yosper, the “ bread-or-blood ”
orator who was clapped into gaol up North, has
got a ,£6OO a year appointment in the Westralian
geological-survey department. He has evidently
got the bread—buttered.
Maoriland appears to be the chief recruit-
in ff 'ground for Sydney dailies in want of reporters.
No less than five ex-Maorilanders figure on Sydney
Daily Telegraph staff, and the most recent addi-
tions to the S. M. Herald are also Maorilanders
acquired from the D.T.
Drs. Marie Castilla and Helen Sexton,
both of Melb. University, are going into partner-
ship and invading the hitherto wholly male
“ Doctors’ Commons ”of Collins-street. At pre-
sent Dr. Castilla is on the Women’s Hospital staff
and Dr. Sexton in practice at St. Kilda.
Melbourne Australasian published an
alleged criticism of Lawson’s book “In the Days
when the World was Wide.” In half-a-column
Lawson was utterly condemned, but the whole
thing would boil down into two lines : Lawson’s
politics are not the politics of the Australasian.
C. J. Ham, the Vic. M.L.C. who has
taken upon himself the position of the Father of
Australian Federation, is the genius who, not so
long ago, denounced a Democratic measure sent
up from the Lower House as “an implical pheno-
mena ; the product of an immaculate conception. ”
Whereupon, even the ranks of Fatmanity fcould
scarce forbear to laugh.
The late D. J. C. Donnelly, M.L.A. for
Cowra (N.S.W.), was an old-time W.A. prospec-
tor, he and his father having exploited the Mur-
chison as early as 1850. Lack of water drove
them back to Perth. Then Donnelly tracked to
Ballarat (arriving just after the Eureka episode),
where he was one of the first—if not the first-to
find gold under the basaltic rock.

“Johnny,” the driver of the Melb. Black Maria,
is about to retire from the “ foorce.” It is related
of one of Johnny’s regular passengers, of the
“Lady Munro ” type, that on one occasion, just
ere entering the van, she went to the side and, look-
ing up at Johnny with the air of a Toorakian
dame of the old (Boom) regime, said, “John,
home!” and then stepping quickly, but with
stately grace, into the dark interior of her
“ brougham, ” drove off to quod.
The recent death at Melb. of Frederick
Leopold Smythe, the clumsy, good-natured Irish
barrister, removed the last of the group of Yic-
torian counsel of the Aspinall, Ireland, and
Sewell era. In his student days Smythe was a
close and intimate friend of Daniel O’Connell. It
was Smythe who once blundered into an apology
to Chief-Justice Stawell, Redmond Barry and tne
elder Williams sitting in Banco for having
addressed their honors as “gentlemen.” “I made
a mistake, your anners,” he innocently said.
“Father” Chiniquy, it appears, is still
neglecting his obvious duty of making the grass
grow green, as he writes a letter to an Australian
friend (a parson, of course) under date of January
last. The firebrand says :
You are living in peace in your beautiful and rich land
of Australia for which your heroic ancestors shed their
blood and fought the battle of liberty.
Such cant comes well from Chiniquy, who, on the
first night of the Hobart riots which had been
caused by his lectures, left the committee-room by
a back way and disguised in a woman’s petticoat.
In the early days of Botany Bay clergymen
were often magistrates. The sainted Samuel
Marsden, in his character as magistrate, once
ordered a servant to be flogged. The servant took
to the bush afterwards, and, being captured and
charged with a capital offence, was hanged. Mr.
Marsden attended him as spiritual adviser on the
scaffold. Speaking of these magisterial clergy-
men, Dr Lang once said : “In other countries
the clergy have been accused of taking the fleece ;
but N.S. Wales is the only country I ever heard
of in which they are openly authorised, under a
Royal Commission, to take the hide also, or to flay
the flock alive.”
Leslie Stephen, lately appointed trustee of
London National Portrait Gallery, is a member of
the Australian Stephen family, and was cousin of
story-writer Harold W. H. Stephen, of Sydney.
Harold sent hatfuls of his literary work to Leslie,
who was at that time editing Cornhill and had
the ear of publishers generally, but very little
Moore, Photo.
Mr. REUBEN FOX, as Svengali, in Williamson and Mus-
grove’s coming production of “ Trilby.”
good came of it. John Hay, visiting England,
obtained for Harold an appointment as Sydney cor-
respondent to a London daily, which made Harold
upbraid Leslie for inefficient kinship ; but Leslie
continued to write kindly, appreciative, and en-
couraging letters, always requesting more and
more work to be sent to him. An Australian pub-
lisher is turning over Harold’s booklets with a
view to early republication.
Mr. E. Pariss Nesbit, Q.C., of Adelaide,
writes :
Dear Bulletin, —A few words in reply to your state-
ment that I am “ running politically amuck ” in Adelaide.
A man who runs amuck stabs at everybody he can reach.
I have done no such thing. I was compelled in self-
defence to tell the public the facts with regard to my
retirement from the late firm of Gordon, Nesbit and
Bright. When my dear friend, J. H. Gordon, stood for
the Leg. Council seven years ago he voluntarily told me
that when I wished to come forward in politics he would
stand back. For nearly seven years I effaced myself poli-
tically to give him a show. When, a month ago, I asked
him to fulfil his promise he demurred. I thereupon
retired from the firm, and a week afterwards he retired
from the Ministry. lam 43 years old, and for 30 years it
has been the dream of my life to represent my native dis-
trict, Barossa, in Parliament. You quote my advt. in
which I say that though Kingston has done very good
work he is very slow and very stupid and that he has
truckled to the crowing hens who run the Women’s
Christian Temperance Union and the Social Impurity
Society. You add —“ And Kingston had made Nes-
bit a Q.C. in spite of the opposition of at least
one of the Supreme Court Judges.” Nothing of
the sort. My appointment as Queen’s Counsel was
delayed for nearly two years because the Playford
Government would not act on Chief Justice Way’s nomina-
tion of Henry Downer as Q.C., and the Chief, not
unnaturally, declined to nominate me until his prior
nomination had been confirmed. Homburg', the then
Attorney-General, said that I was the only member of the
Bar entitled to the appointment, and he would not con-
sent to the appointment of anyone else. Even Sir John
Downer, the Premier of the next Government, did not
venture to act upon the then somewhat stale nomination
of his brother. But I have it on the word of his Attorney-
General, Mr. Homburg, that, subject to some doubts as
to the power of Australian Governments to appoint
Queen’s Counsel, Sir John Downer’s Government would
have appointed me if the Chief Justice had made the
nomination. Shortly after the Kingston Ministry came
into power, as both all the Judges and the Government
were agreed that I was entitled to the appointment, the
Chief Justice told me that he did not see why my
appointment should be longer delayed because the
Government had refused to appoint Mr. Henry Downer,
especially as Sir John Downer’s Government had not
appointed that gentleman. The C.J. sent for Mr. Henry
Downer, and informed him of his intention to
nominate me. Mr. Downer very generously said
that I was clearly entitled to the appointment,
and he would not play the dog in the manger.
As to my now politically opposing Kingston. There is no
man in South Australia to whom politically he owes more
than he does to me. While disagreeing with much of his
policy, especially with regard to the Malcolm land cases,
I have recognised his great force of character and his
general good intentions. The present Coalition Ministry
was formed at my suggestion. I gave Kingston fair warn-
ing a month ago. I said, “ I am with you on the whole.
I shall support you in the forthcoming elections. But if
you truckle to the Social Impurity Society I shall change
sides.” The very next week he licked their boots. I like
a man who is either a saint or a sinner. Kingston is a
sinner at night and a saint in the morning.
If The Bulletin wanted an excuse for printing
this somewhat discursive letter it would plead the
two last delightful sentences. But, all the same, it
begs to differ from Mr. Nesbit. It is very human
to be a sinner at night and a saint in the morn-
ing. The over-night sinner who wants also to
sin in the morning is a hog.
AN.S.W. minor Judge, who some years
ago joined the great majority, was noted for his
absent-mindedness and sleepiness on the bench.
Once, after listening to a case for three days, he
staggered the litigants by remarking : “ I really
don’t understand what this case is all about, so I
must give a verdict for defendant.” And he did.
On another occasion, when trying a case without
a jury, he patiently waited till counsel had finished
their addresses; then, turning to the jury-box,
which was filled with attorneys’ clerks waiting for
their cases to be called on, he began, “ Gentlemen
of the jury, this is a case ” Here there was a
burst of laughter, and it was explained that there
was no jury. “ Verdict for plaintiff,” said he.
But the Supreme Court rarely
upset the decisions—he never
was absent - minded enough to
“ give reasons.”

Sydney D.T.’s London
correspondent compares W. P.
Reeves, M.L. Agent-General,
with his predecessor, Westby
Perceval—to W.P.R.’s disad-
vantage. The truth is that
though Perceval is an able
young man, Reeves is a good
bit above him. Perceval and
Reeves were school-mates; they
entered M.L. Parliament at
the same time; and Reeved
at once became a recognised
force, while Perceval was
little more than his hench-
man. Three years afterwards Reeves was strong
enough to secure Perceval’s appointment as
Agent-General, on the ground that the post
wanted a young, up-to-date man. Reeves’ imme-
diate ambition then was a Ministerial career, but
all his friends knew that his ultimate aim was the
Agency, and that Perceval’s appointment was of
the warming-pan variety.
Melb. : “A word for ex-Speaker Bent.
After all, his career is one of the pet models of the
Y.M.C.A. Lectures to Young Men. Look at the
struggling market-gardener, getting up at 2 a.m.,
and hieing to the city with Moorabbin cabbages.
Then, by sheer industry, he becomes rate-collector.
Seventh Heaven Higinbotham was a great
prophet, but no local member ; and Bent, ousting
him, showed what looking after your constituents
meant, in piers, culverts, marble dung-coffers, or
whatever might be required in a succulent con-
stituency. Minister of Railways—Speaker.
The ex-Speaker was for some time the
occupant of, perhaps, the most desirable
estate in or around Melb.—that of Landcox,
Brighton. It is a remarkable-looking place,
quite a magnificent bit of artificial forest.
Squatter M‘Millan, who laid it out 20 years ago,
spared no money in an endeavor to outshine Joe
Clarke’s Toorak estate. Both of them had first-
class London artists to do the frescoes, which are
probably unequalled south of the line. However,
‘ The great ones of the earth build desolate places
for themselves.’ Both millionaires found their
palaces of art intolerable, under painful circum-
stances, in quite different ways. ”

YARRAN
After All.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The brooding- ghosts of Australian night have gone
from the bush and town ;
My spirit revives in the morning breeze, though it
died when the sun went down ;
The river is high and the stream is strong, and the
grass is green and tall,
And I fain would think that this world of ours is
a good world after all.
The light of passion in dreamy eyes, and a page
of truth well read,
The glorious thrill in a heart grown cold of the
spirit I thought was dead,
A song that goes to a comrade’s heart, and a tear
of pride let fall—•
And my soul is strong ! and the world to me is a
grand world after all !
Let our enemies go by their old dull tracks, and
theirs be the fault or shame
(The man is bitter against the world who has only
himself to blame) ;
Let the darkest side of the past be dark, and only
the good recall;
For I must believe that the world, my dear, is a
♦ kind world after all.
It well may be that I saw too plain, and it may be
I was blind;
But I’ll keep my face to the dawning light, though
the devil may stand behind !
Though the devil may stand behind my back, I’ll
not see his shadow fall,
But read the signs in the morning stars of a good
world after all.
Rest, for your eyes are weary, girl—you have
driven the worst away—
The ghost of the man that I might have been is
gone from my heart to-day ;
We’ll live for life and the best it brings till our
twilight shadows fall;
My heart grows brave, and the world, my girl, is
a good world after all.
HENRY LAWSON.

POINTS
J.P.—Justice of the Purse.
Motto of Vic. Upper House : “It is never
too late to amend.”
Bundaberg, the centre of the Kanaka
trade, has returned an anti-slaver. But, all the
same, there’s not to be any local option in niggers.
An Irishman—Bums —and an Irishman —
O’Shanassy—were the first to introduce women
into G.P.O. employment in N.S. Wales and Vic-
toria respectively.
S.A. National Ass. crowd, in the pre-
liminary fighting now going on, much misses
Chaffey Baker (Kingston’s earliest enemy) who is
a master of election tactics. He is holidaying in
India.
Six four-wheeled wagons and one cart
laden with duty-free American leather, with the
stars and stripes gaily waving over all, paraded
Sydney streets the other day. There was no band.
But there should have been.
Last year the number of empty houses in
Melbourne decreased from 14,000 to 10,000, and
the city showed a good increase of population for
the first time since ’9l. Various other things also
suggest that the City of Dreadful Knights is
definitely on the up-grade.
It is all very well to cry out about the 17
N.S.W. Js.P. who are publicans. There were 793
publicans in the province last year, so that only
between 2 and 3 per cent, of the class have been
given opportunities to pervert the law; whereas
over 80 per cent, of the 116 spirit merchants of
N.S.W. are Js.P. —amid public silence.
South Australian Register :—“ In his
attempts to parry the attacks we have made upon
some of his measures the Premier (Kingston) has
been forced to adopt the desperate expedient of
quoting The Bulletin, a journal which noto-
riously subordinates truth to satire and ostenta-
tiously perverts facts to serve the purpose of the
moment.” When this paper made certain un-
pleasant comments recently on the Register, or
rather its weekly satellite, the Observer—the
same comments which Kingston quoted—it showed
exactly where and how that dismal paper had
interfered with the truth and ostentatiously
perverted facts.” The Register is hereby invited
to be equally explicit in return if it has anything
to go upon, instead of merely whirling itself in the
air, so to speak, without any evidence. What
particular “ perverted fact ” is it maundering
about ?
The ultimate reward ol
a Ministry, according to
Vic. Premier Turner:
“Well done, good and faith,
ful servants; make room
for your successors.”
One clause of the Vic.
Companies Bill which the
Council declared it would
die in the last ditch rather
than pass, was that prohi-
biting directors selling their
own property to their own
companies. This good old
swindle is too lucrative to
be sacrificed. The Melb.
director writes in his private
capacity to himself in his
official capacity offering his
own property for sale at not
more than six times its
value. Then in his official
capacity he decides that his
own property (in his pri-
vate capacity) should be
bought by the co. and he
officially pays the co’s
money for it to himself,
and reports to the co. that
he has successfully com-
pleted the purchase of his
own property from himself
at his own valuation, and
congratulates the co. on
the excellent bargain he
has made with himself on
its behalf. Sometimes a very
scrupulous director has
angry interviews with him-
self over the matter, and
beats down the price a
little, and only completes
the purchase on his own
solemn assurance to himself
that the bargain is a good
one —but generally the nego-
tiation goes off quietly. The
proposal that this touchin':
swindle should be abolished
simply flabbergasted the 48
Rich Landlords, who all
have property to get rid of.
N.S.W. Public-school teachers, in the
upper flight only, were, subsequently to ’94,
allowed <£loo a year for house-rent. Some time
after, when rents had fallen heavily, the authori-
ties reduced this allowance by 15 per cent., making
it ASS. This the teachers considered just, and they
murmured not. But the other day they got
notice from the Public Service Board that the
AB5 rent-allowance will, in future, be subject to
the 4 per cent, superannuation deduction—which
is also just, but the teachers don’t see it this time.
Charles L. Garland, who declines to stand
for Cowra (N.S.W.), was looked upon by Parkes,
Garrett, and other destiny-makers seven or eight
years ago as assured for a portfolio. But the teeth
of the Single-tax entered his flesh, and he day by
day developed such political contradictions that
his sponsors were glad when he cleared out.
There is likely to be frietion between some
Melb. suburban benches and the clerks of courts.
Without exception these officials denounced
bitterly the honorary-justice system when called
as witnesses before the Lormer Board. By the
way, in wiping out his brother J’sP. Lormer has
wiped out himself by reason of his record in
adjudicating as a rabid cold-tea man on licensing
cases.
Police magistrate Keogh, giving evidence
before the Lormer enquiry board, stated that
bench - packing in Victoria doesn’t obtain to
any great extent in the country districts. It
isn’t bench-packing that town prosecutors of
country defendants complain about. The in-
variableness of local sympathy with the local man
is what disgusts them, no matter how limited the
number of beaks sitting on the case.
How the good old boom days have
vanished ! A railway is proposed from Tamworth
to Manilla (N.S.W.) ; most of the land required
is being given gratis and a fund is being subscribed
locally to buy the remainder. And only five years
ago the Government would have bought the re-
quired land at three times its value, after fighting
a dozen expensive law-suits, and indignation
meetings would have been held because the locai
owners didn’t get six times the value instead of
only three times. By-and-by the time will arrive
when the district that wants a railway must not
only give the land gratis but put up half the cost
of construction as well, and will be expected to
feel thankful that it gets its line even then.
The Reid (N.S.W.) Government proposes
next session to introduce a law-reform bill, a bill
for the exclusion of colored races from N.S.W.,
and a scheme whereby, in case of a deadlock
between the two Houses, the matter at issue shall
be settled by a referendum. Also, light railways
are to be started and an elaborate system of water
conservation set going. As nothing is said about
levying any new taxation to provide a public
works fund to pay for the light railways and the
water-conservation, it is presumed that another
borrowed money orgy is to be started, and the
unnatural temporary swelling or financial car-
buncle that ensues will be called, as usual, “ a
he dhy development of the resources of the pro-
vim: or some such flap-doodle.
That mildly-republican platitudinarian,
“Gundagai ” Purves, has set the Jingoes howling
in Melbourne by hinting in a Federation yabber
that Australia will surely cast off the Imperial
connection in a million years or so. The Argus
feels hurt, and philosophises through the medium
of the usual useful “ Constant Reader ” :
All this reminds me of honest old Dr. Johnson, who,
being at the house of an eminent theoretical republican
lady, asked her why, if she was such a republican, she
didn’t ask the footman to sit down to table with her.
Which shows that “ honest old Dr. Johnson ” was
an ass at intervals, but quite an intellectual and
brilliant ass compared with the blithering “neddy ”

A QUART OF MOSQUITOES.
"Talk about muskeeters, you’ve just got to swing your pint-pot round and I’m darned if you
don’t catch a quart."
“ As the meek beasts in the garden came
flocking for Adam to name them, men for a title
to-day crawl to the feet of a king.”—Col. John
Hay.
In the little Bill on married women’s pro-
perty, which Postmaster-Gen. Duffy induced Vic.
Assembly to pass last month without discussion on
his assurance that there was “nothing in it,” it
turns out that there is a provision trenching
seriously on married women’s rights under “re-
straints on anticipation.” These have hitherto
been held sacred, but now a Judge can make an
order for the payment of lawyers’ costs out of
such property. No wonder the lawyers of the
House nodded approval when Duffy was bluffing
the rest. Was the “Unlearned Parliament,”
somewhere in the backblocks of English history,
so very unwise when it altogether disqualified
lawyers as members ?
Can a Prime Minister dismiss a colleague
who won’t resign ? Of course, it is the Governor
who does the formal and visible sacking, but can
he do it constitutionally ? Can a Governor dismiss
a Prime Minister, who doesn’t voluntarily resign ?
Again, when a Premier resigns and the whole Cabi-
net goes, willy nilly, and “ constitoosh’nly ” has
the Excellency to send only for the man recom-
mended by the resigning adviser? When then-
Premier Griffith (Q.) wanted “Mines” Hodgkinson
to resign some years ago, Hodgkinson “saw him
bio wed first.” When MTlwraith enacted the
“ Kitts’s Boots ” episode in the same province he
resigned (tailing his colleagues after him), and
refused to advise the Governor about anything, and
his Ex. caved in. Some lawyers hold that Eorrest
could not constitutionally “ sack ” Venn, but
should have resigned holus bolus and formed a new
Cabinet.
Premier Reid, at Hillgrove, in a tone of
enthusiasm : ‘ ‘ Despite the fact that large cargoes
of Californian wheat and flour had arrived, prices
were higher to-day in N.S. Wales than for many
years past.” Reid is a Freetrader because, as he
has said in Sydney over and over again, Freetrade
makes food cheap. And the same Reid proudly
boasts to the farmers of New England that Free-
trade hasn’t made food cheap—that it is dearer
than it ever was under Protection. Reid objur-
gated Protection through three years of dreadful
eloquence because (he alleged) it made food dear—
and now he is glad all over because Protection has
been done away with and food is dearer still.
Wdl the wobblesome Premier please define him-
self ? Does he claim that Freetrade has sent up
i.ood prices ? If so, is he pleased because food has
gone up ? If so, does he still claim that Freetrade
sends prices down ? Also, does he object to Pro-
tection on the ground (as previously alleged) that
it meant dear food or (as now alleged) that it
meant cheaper food than Freetrade has supnlied
up to date ?
Queensland telegram: “Thursday Island.—
The Government steamer Albatross arrived from
the south to-day, with lepers ” The time is
com in o - up North when they will simply put the
unclean in the manifest, along with the other
cargo and the Government Statistician will men-
tion him among the exports. Even now, they
take him quite as a matter of course. No one is
worried, or surprised, or much interested ; it is
merely mentioned that a vessel dropped in casually
at a wayside port “ with lepers.
In their retrenching operations, the N.S.
Wales Public Service commissioners are making
no distinction of persons that is visible to the
naked eye. The fact that a man who has been
getting paid for doing nothing is a member’s
brother doesn’t save him from official extinction.
The other day a N.Q. miner fell down a
70-foot shaft into six feet of water, with the only
result that he was immediately sobered up and
bawled vigorously for a rope and assistance. “No
harm done ? ” cheerfully chipped in a chum as
they hauled the warrior to the surface. “No
harm ! ” ejaculated the rescued one indignantly ;
“ why—d it, man — I’m sober / It’s spoilt my
drunk ! ”
The ennobbled Besant is grateful for
the royal favor, and in his latest novel, “ The
Master Craftsman,” now running in the Age,
handles the House of Lords with touching rever-
ence. His “radical” hero would not tumble
down the House of the anointed Lord.s, but would
strengthen it and increase its powers, andthrough-
out there is manifested a deep veneration for “ our
old nobility ” and all aristocratic institutions.
Drought has some strange effects. Cattle
are driven to eat anything, and maybe seen brow-
sing upon paper, sticks, and every variety of
rubbish. A cow will make short work of any clothes
hung up to dry in an accessible place. A well-
known Western man tells how he saw a horse eat-
ing something by a fence, and, on coming up,
discovered the body of a sheep half finished. It
is surmised the horse began by eating the burrs
on the sheep’s wool, and from that proceeded to
eat flesh and all.
An American scribe in the Fortnightly
throws a flood of light on Uncle Sam’s zeal in the
cause of Venezuela. Some time ago the Vene-
zuelan Govt, made a grant of 15,000,000 acres of
land, rich in rosewood, mahogany, gold, and
minerals, to certain citizens of the U. States. It
turns out now that most of this land is situated ia
the territory claimed by the British. The Vene-
zuelans are chuckling to themselves on the smart-
ness of the manceuvre by which they dragged the
Yankees into the quarrel.
There is a fine irony about Nature out-
back. In Western N.S.W. the tracks 011 which
yesterday men died terrible deaths from thirst are
to-day surging streams of yellow water. Parched
sheep and cattle, which have hardly stopped bleat-
mg pitifully for a drink, gurgle in the agonies of
drowning. Careworn pastoralists, whose runs
were, a few days ago, bare paddocks with empty
tanks lined with dead stock, are now miserably
watching roaring bodies of water wash away ex-
pensive dams and tear down miles of fencing. It
would be humorous were it not so pitiful.
At Hughenden, the other day, a poor
gm, the slave of half the township, was confined
by the side of a creek, and perished there all alone
Tl ? g ° nj h • • • At Thursday Island, one
An mat, a hopeless invalid, wished to leave the
ospital to proceed to Siam. Ten pounds were
required for the purpose. Ah Mat donated three—
au his worldly possessions—to the institution ;on
this the Govt, grant was M (two to one), and the
committee gave the remaining pound. Thus skil-
tuhy was the required amount raised ; the Govt.
ea ; and an incubus was gently removed to
another land.

Deputation on Public Mokals. —At 11 a.m-
on Friday week, Colonial Sec. Brunker has con"
sented to receive a deputation from the
council of the churches in the interests of public
morals. The deputation, which will repre-
sent all the Protestant churches and will be
introduced by the Primate, will bring under the
notice of the Col. Secretary the desirability of
some amendment of the laws respecting gambling
lotteries, licensing of dancing-rooms, and the age
of consent.
A SENIOR CONSTABLE’S EVIDENCE.
“Police Stud Depot, Dandenong, 24th Feb.,
1896. The Manager, National Wool Company,
Melbourne. Dear Sir, —I have much pleasure
in testifying to the utility of your rabbit-
poison, TOXA. Being in charge of the Police
Stud Depot, I was instructed by Inspector
Beckwith to procure some half-dozen tins of
TOXA. The use of one tin sufficed to show its
usefulness and simplicity. Fortunately, the
pests are not so numerous in the police pad-
pocks, but in the adjoining paddock no fewer
than 900 rabbits were found dead in the
furrows from the use of two tins of TOXA.
Words cannot express its value.—l am, &c.
(signed) A. J. Folkiner, senior-constable.”
Mr. A W STEEL,

Patent Street Directories .
AN INGENIOUS CONTRIVANCE.
Those whose business takes them in the vicinity
of Pitt-street must have had their attention drawn
to the number of bright vermilion-colored cases
erected in prominent places indicating the locality
of the business people in that busy thoroughfare.
These cases, known as the “Electric City Street
Directory,” are bmng introduced to Sydney for
the first time by Messrs. Percy and Dearsley, of
f
*
/
H. DEARSLEY.
P.'O. Chambers, Pitt-st., whose photographs are
printed herewith.
This invention commenced its career in N. Z., and
the patentees were not long in discovering that
they had struck an idea which filled a long-felt
want, and this fact was borne out by the strong
support tendered them by the business people
of the various centres, over 11,000 subscribers
being obtained in less than twelve months.
The erection of these directories terminates, to
a very great extent, the trouble and annoyance
visitors and the public generally have had to put
up with in the past when trying to locate any
address required in the vicinity.
Tiie indexes are of the very best workmanship,
having a British plate-glass front, which is set
into rubber to prevent any dust or moisture
getting into the case, and thus the names are kept
free from obliteration and are always distinct,
and, being of a uniform pattern and official color,
at once attract the attention of passers-by and
those who may wish to consult them ; the plates
on which the names are painted are metal, and
can be transferred from one case to another should
a subscriber change his or her address.
The Mayors in all cities and towns where this
* t
G. PERCY.
patent has been introduced, have favored this enter-
prising firm with eloquent testimonials as to the
benefits the public have derived from these
directories.
In addition to the Street Directory, Messrs.
Percy and Dearsley do a large business as adver-
tising contractors ; their branch offices in all parts
of the colonies give them special facilities for ex-
peditious and thorough work.
The Proprietors should have a long period of
success before them ; a success few people will
grudge to the enterprise which may so fairly claim
to have supplied a long-felt want. *

About the only circumstance that prevents
the present Government from sinking into insig-
nificance and oblivion is the large advertisements
one occasionally bumps up against, announcing
that owing to the repeal of the duties Mr. Ragtag
will hold a Freetrade Sale, and give his goods
away almost free of charge (for their full value in
coin of the realm). First comes the Freetrade
sale, then the clearing sale, after which the bill
of sale brings matters to a climax. A notable
exception to the above, is the estab-
lishment known as the Misfit Parlor, where
Mr. Nathan Herman has introduced the latest
novelties. Thousands of people are being
attracted daily to this Mammoth Warehouse,
which has been turned into a retail emporium,
and they usually leave it rejoicing at the genuine-
ness of the magnificent bargains they receive,
only to return next day for more. Mr. Her-
man has a pile of letters (the postmarks on
which are convincing proofs of their genuineness),
advising the arrival of bales of orders to country
customers, and expressive of the satisfactory
manner in which the orders were attended to.
It appears Mr. Herman personally superintends
this branch of the business, and he is exceptionally
particular that all orders shall be faithfully ful-
filled, and nothing shoddy sent to his country
clients. A reference to his advertisement in
another part of this paper will amply repay
the trouble, as the great bargains there enumerated
are certainly unheard of. His £2 bale is a marvel
of the nineteenth century ; his £1 dress bale, and
his wonderful household parcel for £1 are eye-
openers ; and the whole lot, combined with other
bargains too numerous to mention, fill a list which
is verily astounding. The goods are all marked
in plain figures, and Mr. Herman invites anyone
to compare his prices. Intending customers
should therefore call early.*

A Sydney bookie remarked the other day that
the names of people who owed him, and the little
amounts they owed, “would shock a parson.” Betting
on the nod would go under if the tote came along. The
metallician would be practically eradicated—if people
wanted to gamble they’d have to pay cash over the
counter as in buying postage-stamps.
* * * *
One cannot commend the practice of metropoli-
tan clubs in taking bookies’ evidence at inquiries, but a
remedy doesn’t suggest itself as matters stand. An owner,
asked to prove that he backed his prad when it should
have won, but didn’t, just fetches in Ikey Mo. If Mo has
a “ faked ” book or, worse still, an interest in the horse,
the proceedings are farcical.
* * * «
The story goes that a policeman lately gave a
man ss. on the quiet to back a horse on the tote. The
individual joyfully drank the cash. X 47 followed and
saw, and later in the evening ran his friend in for drunken-
ness.
* » * *
It is not unusual for Melb. pavement-bookies to
be seated in chairs on the sidewalk. They enter up their
bets seated, but the weary copper seeth them not.
* * * *
George Martin, son of the late N.S.W. Chief
Justice, after rowing so well last year at. Oxford and
Henley, failed to get a seat in the ’Varsity crew this year,
as he shaped so badly in the trials.
* * * *
Significant line in a bookie’s half-column advb. :
“Merchandise of every description bought for cash."
• * * *
Last Saturday morning threatening clouds made
the N.S.W. League stand about with a dismal air, but the
afternoon saw on the ground 15,000 spectators and the
Governor.
* * * *
Martin, who rides a Humber machine, on
reaching the winning-post shoots bolt-upright to catch
the judge’s eye. He deserved every one of his grand wins
last Saturday, and more than every penny of his total of
£lOO, or just thereabouts, in prize-money. Parsons’s
prize-money last Saturday was £11; Megson’s total is
£26 ; T. P. Johnson stood second with £3B.
♦ » * *
Yankee Martin, the present Australian cycling
god, who has the luck to be an iron-jawed man of enor-
mous stamina, showed himself more consistent than most
gods by starting in five events and coming in first four
times, and second once. He beat Parsons twice—in the
Sydney Cup (1 mile) and Invitation International Scratch
(5 miles). The pair, sitting on their machines, shook hands
directly after the finish of the Sydney Cup.
* * • »
Overheard at Saturday’s cycling : “ Keep right
thar until I say hit her up, and then go like a funny feel-
ing through a church-choir. Keep right along; they
might as well try to snowball in h—l as catch you.”
• « • •
The second and third-class races were funereally
dull after the scintillations of the Martin star. Ex-sculler
Matterson’s win (with 180yds. start) in the 3-miles Open
Handicap (first prize, £2O) made the spectators more tired
than himself. Such is the effect of seeing Martin come all
the way from scratch and down everybody.
♦ * * »
The Tandem Handicap supplied more Martin,
who, with Harris behind, put in the last lap at rocket gait.
Lewis and Porta rode a splendid second, Megson and
Payne a good third. As for the others—they were prac-
tically dead.
* * * *
Parsons meets Martin this Saturday in their
private races. Parsons does not seem to have too much
show of winning. Taken down by Martin in the five
miles, and beaten in his heat of the one-mile by Payne,
the ten miles is probably his only chance.
* * * •
At East Melb. bicycle sports, on Saturday, the
erstwhile Austral winner, A. A. Middleton, was in great
form, winning the two-mile handicap from 20 yards in
great style, after making his own pace on the outside of
the bunch all the way. Arthur Turner, who started from
the same mark and persistently hung on to Middleton’s
wobbling wheel, was second. Middleton rides as un-
steadily as ever, but gets over the ground all the same.
* * * *
Parsons’ luck when after the bullion is not
particularly bright just now, but as a record-wrecker he
continues to get there. The other day, at the St. Kilda
ground, he reeled off a lap, 530yds., in 31secs.
• * • •
On account of the alleged excessive profits
charged by Melb. cycle firms, 40 residents of Richmond
(Melb.) despatched £3OO to a leading English cycling firm,
and received in return 43 machines, including pump, bell,
lamp, &c., at an average cost of £7 each.
* * * *
A South Melb. “ rational ” cycliste, who pos-
sesses the unique distinction (among wheelwomen)
of prettiness, turned her accomplishment and her charms
to some account last Saturday, when she took a turn at
pacing one of the competitors in a local road-race. As
was a foregone conclusion, the lucky youth won “ hands
up.” The man who couldn’t travel with a well-developed
pair of feminine calves a few feet in front to lure him on
might as well throw up the game at once.
J. V. Shorland (M.L.) recently set out to break
a 150-mile road record. A feminine Hercules, in bloomers,
suddenly appeared on the scene, and offered to pace him
throughout. She did it admirably, too, and—as per pre-
ceding moral—the record was broken.
A cycling Melb. penlady enthusing over a
female costume for the bike : “The divided skirt falls
evenly over a diamond frame, and to all appearances the
rider is in a skirt while the jacket hides from view the
least pleasing portion of a lady’s figure on the saddle.”
Least pleasing portion ” is distinctly good—but
polemical. Tub Bulletin will be pleased to hear dis-
cussion on this subject.
* * * *
The Brisbane 22-foot crack, Ettie, is likely to
show the Sydney boats her heels over their own course
next Nov. The Ettie, built and owned by an amateur
pure and simple, Mr. “ Billy ” Wain, of the Brisbane Port
Otnce, has beaten all the local and Sydney cracks on
Brisbane waters, and is, perhaps, the fastest boat of her
inches afloat. That an amateur-built boat should outsail
the best productions of the best professional builders is a
thing no fellow can understand.
* * * *
The Australian Eleven bungle is like the Dean
case—there seems no end to it. Ten to one when the
boat reaches Colombo a cable will come through that
Graham denies everything, or that Giffen has confessed.
* * * *
A Harry-cot : the Australian Eleven hash.
* * * »
Journalist: “Thank heaven the Australian
Eleven has gone ’Ome at last. I was sicker of them than
of anything I can recollect. Here’s a prophesy : this team
will lose matches, wrangle and generally play-up more
than any of its predecessors. They mayn’t drink as much
in the aggregate, but there are more elements of ruction
and strife.
* * * *
It is not likely that the appeal of secretary
Manning, of E. Melb. C.C., for funds to send Harry ’Ome
will be very successful. Melbourne people have been
dosed ad nauseam with Harry’s grievances, and it was
generally understood that the £l6O solatium had settled
matters. If not, what was it for ?
* * * *
The Bulletin’s year-old forecast : “ Unless a
great reversal of form takes place next season, five new
men at least will be in the team— viz., Eady, Albert Trott,
Iredale, Darling, and Clem. Hill.”
* * * *
Mick Dooley and Peter Felix—Peter Jackson’s
double in everything but science—are to meet in Mel-
bourne 30th March for the best of 15 rounds. Taking a
line from Felix’s go with Ball in Sydney, Dooley should
win in a couple of rounds.
* » * *
The Bweep business is booming at Johannesburg,
one for £lOO,OOO being fixed for April, with a special of
£50,000 to be drawn same day.
* * * *
Griffiths, of Hobart, intends adding two more
dreary hours to Bax’s 28-hour club-swinging record. If
he succeeds, Burrows, of Cairo, may follow with 81 hours,
and Bax will then get wound up and set himself going for
a week. As an en thusiastic sport club-swinging is about
on a par with trap ping mud-larks.
* « * *
if two men stop to speak in King-street, Sydney,
they are “moved on.” Hundreds block not alone the
footpath, but the roadway in the busiest part of George-
street, to watch the cricket scores, and the police make no
sign. Why ?
* » * *
Belfast Cycling Journal says that when Par-
sons beat Zim. “ladies embraced him and wept, mil-
lionaires wrote him out cheques, theatrical managers
implored him to accept private boxes.” Facts: The
“ladies” sent Mr. P. a 2d. compass; the millionaires
(where are they ?) didn’t know he was racing ; theatrical
managers wouldn’t implore a cyclist because he is a rival
leg "'how.
* * * *
Roseingrave, winner of the long jump at Sydney
Irish sports, with an Australian record of 22ft. a
few months ago covered 23ft. sin. in Ireland. He also
made a record of over 50ft. in running hop-step-and-jump,
and has won the Irish championship high jump with a
moderate sft. 7in.
* * * *
Mr. M‘Sweeney has backed both his horses,
VVahine and Te Whiti, for the Doncaster Handicap.
Blessed is the owner who thinks he can win a big race
with either of two horses, and backs ’em both with equal
enthusiasm, saith Ikey Mo.
“ Amykos ” educational competition, par-
ticulars of which appear in the advertising columns
of this paper, closes on 30th June. There are 720
prizes m all, varying from <£lo down to 2s. 6d.,
and the total value is £435.

Invalids
AND
The Aged.
About the finest Polynesians in Queens-
land are the Gilbert Islanders employed on the
Burnett—-physically handsome men of more than
ordinary intelligence. They receive letters regu-
larly from the Gilberts in parcels of forty or fifty,
and answer the same—some of them in quite a
dashing commercial hand.
Holy Reuben Keirl—a Scripture name
again ! —Melbourne’s leading non-sassiety swindler,
was known in his youth as a shining light of Sun-
day-school and started life as coachman to a
Ballarat Chinese doctor who, when he died, left
all his property (some <£1500) to his faithful jehu.
With this money the godly Keirl began jobbing
on the local ’Change, and with his undoubted
business ability did well for some time. Growing
ambitious, he moved to Melbourne in ’B6, and was
soon much boomed by the local press for his
alleged “saving of a young girl from ruin.”
Keirl’s account was that one evening he noticed
a man and woman entering a coffee-palace “ in a
suspicious manner,” and at once accosted the
couple. The base deceiver shot off down a cor-
ridor, and the “ victim,” on being taken to the
hospital, was found to have been drugged. When
this smellful Y.M.C.A. sensation began to wane,
Keirl bounded once more into public view by
appearing as the most important witness at the
sensational inquest of Laura Swain, the girl
drowned at the Victoria Bridge, Studley Park,
and deposed that, a few weeks prior to the
tragedy, she had confided to him her intention of
committing suicide. In addition to being a wit-
ness in the case, Keirl also took a hand in the
post-mortem conducted by Dr. Neild, and when
the latter functionary stated that, though 2000
cadavers had come under his official notice, he had
never seen so perfectly-formed a woman, Keirl
just put in a word of endorsement, which was
carefully quoted in the reports. At this time
Reuben stood on the same plane in public estima-
tion as the most gilded and holy saints of the then
roaring boom. According to indications, very few
months would pass before Keirl was running Mel-
bourne, and, when he got some capital together
took a hand in publishing the Sporting Wire, a
journal which started off by tipping five winners
out of every possible six, and keeping the average
up, the whole country arose and called him
blessed, while the bookmakers were on the point
of voting him an annuity to go away somewhere.
When the Wire died, allegedly of an attack of
Keirl’s “financing,” he visited Adelaide and
swept over. the Holy City like a simoon. Por a
few months Keirl ran the place, borrowing
money in all directions, became a prominent
social Gimletist, and joined every pious organisa-
tion within a radius of 20 miles. Then, as a
climax, he became engaged to a local heiress, but
before the nuptials could be celebrated certain
information from Melbourne caused the lady’s
parents to consider hard. While they were thus
ruminating Keirl returned to Melbourne, and put
Dr. Deeper, of Trinity College, on to the deadest
of all dead birds in the shape of land companies,
the worthy cleric enthusing to the tune of <£3oo.
Then the awakening came. The alleged co.
was found to be non-existent and Leeper’s
money a minus quantity. It seemed ridiculous
to suspect such a thing, but the guilt was
unmistakeably fixed on Keirl, and that plausible
person got four years’ hard. In ’O3 Keirl was
released from Pentridge, and from that time until
his recent arrest he has been living principally
upon the capital of a wealthy German farmer at
Preston (Vic.), whom he apparently hypnotised
into a complete state of obedience. But the game
could not last for ever ; even the false-pretenc6
racket has its limits, and now Keirl stands sen-
tenced to three years for false pretences, two
years for larceny as a bailee, and a few odd
months for other minor swindles. Keirl is dis-
tinctly unlucky ; had he managed to restrict him-
self to land-companies or banks he might now be a
wealthy and representative citizen living in the
odour of sanctity. His suave address and his
business tact would have carried the man through
any speculating community in the world with a
good balance on the right side had he but kept clear
of the criminal law, for his unblushing effrontery
would have been bound to land him some gigantic
coup sooner or later. Just after his first discharge
from gaol Keirl entered a Coburg tram-car attired
m spats, bell-topper, yellow gloves, and all the
paraphernalia of aggressive respectability. On
taking his seat he noticed Colonel Bull, Governor
of the Pentridge Prison, with four ladies, seated
opposite. Instantly raising his hat, Keirl coolly
remarked “Ah, excuse me, Colonel, I did not
notice you at first.” Bull glared, but made no
response. But Keirl was nothing daunted ; pull-
ing a handkerchief from his pocket, he fanned
himself gently, murmuring “ These hot winds are
very trying, are they not ? ” and sank gracefully
into a restful position in the corner.

HIS Company is being formed to acquire five 10-acre Gold-mining Leases held by the Newcastle
_ and Stockton Gold-mining Syndicate. Three blocks of 10 acres each are located on the famous
Surface Hill, and occupy the crown of the hill, overlooking the Turon River near Wattle Flat.
The other two blocks containing 20 acres are on the Devil s Knob, or Wattle 1 lat Hill, about
li miles distant. . , , , „ , .
The crown of Surface Hill, which is over 1000 ft. above the level of the Turon River, may be
described as a network of leaders, and veins carrying rich gold. And, what is of still more importance,
is that the material between the veins also carries payable gold. ~ . . ,
Intersecting these veins are innumerable leaders carrying gold, and in the stone adjoining the
veins are quantities of decomposed pyrites. , , , ,
The lode runs almost north and south through the Coy s 3 blocks, and has been proved to carry
payable gold to a width of 600 feet, and throughout the whole length of the Coy s leases.
As an illustration of the richness of the leaders, a small quantity of 4£ tons of stone taken from
the northern 10 acre block returned, from a local Battery, after first having all the free gold washed
from it, lOoz. lldwt. A bag of the tailings was also sent to the Mines Department for assay, and the
return shows 20ozs. 9dwts. of gold, and lOozs. 7dwts. of silver per ton, which was not saved at the
local Battery. From this it will be seen that the veins and leaders are very rich indeed.
It is the intention of the Coy. to quarry the whole hill, and put everything through the battery.
It is estimated that the material delivered at the Crushing Plant, alongside the river, will not cost
more than Is. per ton, and that 7s. 6d. per ton, including the above will be the total cost of dealing
with the stone, or in other words, 2dwt. of gold per ton will cover all expenses, leaving everything over
and above that for division among the shareholders. - , , . .
Active operations are being carried on on the Surface Hill blocks, and already numerous veins of
rich gold-bearing quartz, through which the gold is plainly visible, have been crossed m the open
cutting, almost every day disclosing new ones. The two 10-acre blocks on the Devil s Knob, which
have been referred to, are on the same line of lode as the “ Queenslander ” which property is a very
'wtahaU for, the Company will be declared formed and registered.
No more need be said, except to refer intending shareholders to the accompanying report of Mr
Pleitner, of Adelaide, S.A., who was recommended to the promoters as being one of the best Gold
Mining Experts obtainable in the colonies.
Particular attention is directed to the fact that the vendors have such faith in the bona fides of the
property that they are asking no cash consideration whatever, taking full payment in shares ; so that
the whole of the subscribed capital, less expenses of flotation, will be available for the purchase and
erection of the best gold-saving machinery.
ROUGH ESTIMATE.
To show the enormous quantity of crushing stuff in sight to the present level:—
According to estimates there are two million seven hundred thousand (2,700,000) tons in sight.
One hundred (100) head of stampers would crush, roughly speaking, 60,000 tons per annum ; this
will give, at very low estimate, work for over thirty (30) years. W. PLEITNER, M.E.

Book-buyers can get rare bargains at Angus
and Robertson’s cheap sale, now proceeding at

Mr. James Kidman, the well-known
Sydney grocer and many other things besides, has
again scooped almost all the Government tenders
for 1896. The secret of this firm’s 56 years con-
tinuous success is that it is its own importer,
wholesale merchant, middleman, and wherever
possible its own grower as well. Mr. Kidman is
his own baker, having a large bakery in the city.
He has also a large butchery, and all the meat
supplied to customers is killed there. He has a
tea-plantation in Ceylon and another in China,
and all the teas supplied are of his own growing.
Kidman’s is a very extensive affair nowadays,
with three stables, 23 carts constantly employed
in the delivery of goods, 54 horses, 15 bakers and
a foreman, four or five men constantly employed
in the bottling department, an office staff of 17,
and 102 miscellaneous employes. It is re-
putedly the cheapest establishment in Aus-
tralia, which is evidenced by the fact that
Mr. K. is purveyor of all eatables and
drinkables to the military forces at Sydney, Wol-
longong, and Newcastle ; N.S.S. Sobraon ; Hos-
pital for Insane, Gladesville; Hospital for Insane,
Callan Park ; Hospital for Insane, Parramatta;
Hospital for Insane, Rydalmere; Hospital for
Insane, Newcastle; Hospital for Insane, Recep-
tion House, Darlinghurst; Darlinghurst Gaol;
Biloela Gaol ; Parramatta Gaol; Shaftesbury Re-
formatory, South Head; the Coast Hospital, Little
Bay; the Leper Lazaret, Little Bay ; Industrial
School for Girls, Parramatta ; Benevolent Asylum,
George-street, Parramatta ; Benevolent Asylum,
Macquarie-street, Parramatta ; Benevolent Asy-
lum, Newington, Parramatta ; Benevolent Asy-
lum, Rookwood, Parramatta ; Benevolent Asylum,
Liverpool, Parramatta ; the Quarantine Station,
North Head ; and all Government institutions not
otherwise provided for. #
Dear Bulletin, —I know nothing abort
snakes swallowing snakes, but I to-day saw a
game-rooster swallowing a black snake about 18in.
long. The fowls of a neighbor were kicking up a
row, and on investigation the rooster was seen
with the snake caught by the neck strutting
proudly around. After several strenuous efforts
it got a fair start and gobbled it down. That was
eight hours ago, and the rooster has gone to its
perch as usual.—A. D. Mittagong, March 9.

A Martyr to Indigestion
PERMANENTLY CURED.
Mr. F. Wokfold, Taree, Manning River,
says : —“ Some years ago I suffered severely from
an ulcerated stomach, and ever since I have
been a martyr to Indigestion. I was led to try
BOOT’S INDIGESTION CURE, although I
had little faith in the possibility of medicine
affording me any relief. After taking a few
doses I was so free from pain that I put away
the remainder of the bottle. After several weeks
I had a slight return of my old complaint, but a
few doses of the ‘ CURE ’ soon drove it away
permanently. That one bottle I consider would
be cheap at a pound note. ”

In’62, writes “ Lycidas,” the flies on the
Barcoo were very bad. Hot meat was out of the
question, as it would get so thickly covered with
flies as to be uneatable. Sometimes even you had
to get inside your mosquito-curtain to eat your
salt beef, and ditto if you would read or write.
At night they would be so thick as to perfectly
blacken the grass outside the camp, and if you
went outside the tent they would rise up with
such a roar that you would be unable to hear the
horse-bells 100 yards away. The horses had a
terrible time —the flies settled on the corners of
their eyes and made sores as big as half-crowns.
Some of the men dressed their horses in calico to
protect them, and I remember one old fellow who
rode up to our camp at sundown with his horse
dressed in a comfortable suit of calico. He looked
a most curious object, and, as the sun was behind
him, you could see the flies, looking like half-a-
dozen swarms of bees, around him and his horse.
I never knew what a plague of ordinary flies was
before, but it is infinitely worse than one of mos-
quitos or sandflies. They have never been so bad
since.
Dr. Hope’s Liver and Blood Clarifying
Compound is recommended as a cure for every
disease which has its origin in a disordered condi-
tion of the blood. Long ago a philosopher, who
was far ahead of his era, reported that “ the blood
is the life,” and his statement has stood inspection
to this day. The diseases which begin with an
unwholesome condition of the blood are legion, and
a remedy that will repair that vital fluid covers a
vast extent of ground in the materia medica.*

from you were of great benefit to me and others.

your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.

your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special

Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline

of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.

plaint. May you long be spared under God to

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
ot the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block m Collms-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
‘Ten Tears’ Practice in Australia,” by
surgeon Iredell, of Melbourne, is a bright, chatty
booklet that may persuade here and there a
reader to take life a little more rationally. Mr.
Iredell, who has no fads to urge, throws in some
wise comments on the particular follies of Eat
Man. “ With increasing age and diminishing
powers a corresponding decrease in the quantity of
food must be taken in order to preserve health,” is
a precept handed down by Luigi Cornaro, a
Venetian born five and a-half centuries ago.
“ But,” says the Melbourne practitioner, “after in-
stilling this into a 16st. man, and obtaining a re-
duction of 2st. in weight, I had the satisfaction of
hearing that I was ruining his constitution, and it
had been necessary for him to take a tonic and
increased nourishment. Not only does the
patient himself become anxious as he loses
superfluous flesh, but his friends buzz round him
and tell him he is not looking up to the mark, and
object to his shadow ever growing less !! ” The
danger of passing from a busy state to otium cum
dignitate, when vitality begins to fail, is neatly
put: ‘‘ He suddenly takes the weight off the safety-
valve. He removes the cork from the bottle of
life. The internal pressure was already lowering ;
now rapidly it diminishes, escapes, and his
exhaustion takes no long time before it is com-
plete. Carefully, and with great judgment, should
such a man have lessened his work to suit his
altering fires, and no man is to be pitied less than
the man who is said to die in harness. ”
