The most sordid and miserable tale of the
famous Jameson raid is that concerning the
flight of the Outlanders when the first trouble
began. Even before thenews reached Johannesburg
concerning the advance of the wild Caledonian
warrior from Buluwayo, a panic arose, and the
maddest skedaddle of modem times set in. Inside
of three days 20,000 people left for Natal or the
Cape.
Carriage compartments to hold 10 people had in them
from 17 to 20. On one train, two children were suffocated
by the crush. Their dead bodies were passed out of the
train at Bloemfontein. The smell from the overcrowding
was so bad that people could not stay in the station.
Several births took place in the crowded trains. One com-
partment that ought to hold eight had 17 in it when a
child was born. For the first few days people seemed to
go mad, and not to care what they did so long as they
got away from the town. An eye-witness said it was
agonising to witness the rush to obtain milk at the Bloem-
fontein buffet, as there was a great number of children of
such a tender age that they could partake of no other food
but milk —and there was none to be had. The hotels were
crowded to suffocation, and 30 women used one apartment
as a dressing-room.
A whole lot of elaborate reasons have been given
for the failure of the Johannesburgers to rise—
they were “ deceived ; ” they didn’t know Jameson
was coming ; they were told that he had been cap-
tured, and would be executed if they rose, so they
lay low from unselfish motives; they lay low because
the representative of their beloved Queen told
them to, &c., &c. But this new tale seems to sug-
gest another possibility—that they kept quiet from
unmitigated scare and cold perspiration. Ap-
parently, in these days, every soul who could get
into a train, even by sitting on the head of a dying
fellow creature, got in and bolted for his life, and,
in all probability, many others would have gone too
if they could have hung on to the buffer of the last
carriage and run behind. Not being able to get
away, they stayed behind and surrendered the City
of Dreadful Funk at the first time of asking.
There is a moral in the story. Almost any kind
of brawny white man will fight to the bitter end
for his home or his bark-hut and his country.
But in a mixed community, where the nigger re-
places the Aryan pick-and-shovel man, and the
white community is mostly Jews and stock-
brokers and speculators and “first robbers”
and shopkeepers, and there is only a stock ex-
change and a lot of British syndicates to fight for,
nobody really wants to fight.
Laureate Austin’s is one way of rhyming
about Jameson’s freebooting raid. Here is
another :
Jameson’s men into Krugersdorp’s
Glen rode clanking with swords on thighs ;
Jameson’s men left many a corpse
Staring at Heaven with glassy eyes.
“Oom Paul ” was but “a fat old man” ;
“ Jim” was going to “sour his ale.”
“ Oom Paul ” laughs as he drains his can ;
“Jim ” is now on his way to—gaol.
Or, at any rate, deserves to be. The soul of old
Lobengula must have shaken the rafters of Hades
with laughter when he heard from the latest
arrivals there how his enemies had eaten dirt in
their turn.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THREE CASTLES” and “ C-A.ZPST'.A.IIsrV 5
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“ Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. {Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

Dear Bulletin,-The adhesion of many Aus-
tralian papers to the doctrine that money-lenders should
be allowed a free hand in fixing interest rates in their trans-
actions, and that Govt, interference in their business is
impolitic, rashly experimental, and almost certain to lead
to failure, is best answered by a few facts which these
papers ought to be acquainted with, but seemingly are
not. Almost every State of the U.S.-if not absolutely
every one-regulates the usurer’s interest, and some of
Jw* h 'u V f- d ? n s so . for I,iar D' years. The result shows
that such limitation is perfectly feasible, is not a rash or
novel experiment, and doesn’t necessarily lead to failure
In two States of the Union the law has fixed the maxi’
mum interest at 5 per cent. ; in 29 at 6 per cent, ; in 10 at
7 per cenb. ; in six at 8 per cent. ; in two at 10 per
cent., and m one at 12 per cent. The penalties for
exceeding the legal rate amount in some instances to a
total loss of the legally-allowed interest, and in others to
the loss of both principal and interest. In Columbia the
payer of usurious interest can recover it by legal process
within twelve months, in Delaware the usurer forfeits a
sum equal to the amount lent, in Florida he is liable to
pay double the amount he has charged over the legal rate
and in Arkansas negotiable paper tainted with usury is
void. In New Hampshire the usurer forfeits three times
the excess interest charged, while in South Dakota he not
only forfeits the interest, but is punishable by fine of
500dol. with six months’ imprisonment. To the terrible
vaticinations with which the daily press endeavor to scare
Australian Parliaments from dealing with the cormorant
usurer these living facts are the best reply.—Z.
Editor Bulletin, —Re usury. The other day I
offered a Melb. money-lender a deposit receipt, worth
on Change 10s. in £, as security for a loan of £4 for
a month. All he wanted was a bill at 30 days for £5.
Simply 300 per cent, per annum on security on which
any bank would give an immediate overdraft of £6O at
6 per cent, per annum.—P.P.
* * * *
The lack of decent securities is playing havoc
with Melb. usurers. The high priest of the industry,
considering it cheaper to take a holiday than to lend his
good red gold under existing circumstances, has gone to
Europe. In consequence, the jackal bill-shavers have
commenced to dispute for the boilers on the wharves.
* * * .
Eor some time past the Sydney dailies have not
been publishing the values of the estates of deceased
persons, and this is the second occasion during the last
12 years in which such information has been withheld.
Now, why has matter of such peculiar interest been sup-
pressed? N.S.W. people are often informed by their own
newspapers of the sums left by wealthy men in the other
provinces, and even of the amounts left by persons belong-
ing to N.S.W. who had property elsewhere ; but as to how
much has been accumulated by deceased N.S. Welshmen
within the mother province, the Sydney daily press, for
some occult reason, maintains silence.
* * * *
Some time ago a N.S.W. man died, leaving all,
or nearly all, of his property to the R.C. Church. His kin
threatened legal fight, alleging undue influence, but the
.*« squared, the Church agreeing to accept
£lO,OOO m full settlement. This £lO,OOO was raised by
, , heirs-at-law on mortgage upon the property (then
valued at £38,000). The other day, default having been
made in payment of interest, the property was put up for
auction by the mortgagees, and £9OOO was the highest bid.
™rne is not the only place where property has
* * * *
S.A. producers find that, through the State
export Dept they get good prices for their produce
without being bled white by the middleman. Thus the
democratic party, already strong in the metropolis, gains
muscle in the country. Recently the Produce Dept,
realised in London 11s. 2d. per head for a shipment of
lambs for which only 4s. 6d. was offered in Adelaide.

Latest Westralian telegraph dodge. Message
tendered at 4 p.m. “Send this immediately.” Clerk
(either friend or tipped) dates it 9 a.m. same day, so that
it takes precedence of all messages dated after that time.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — You say that a Bendigo man
holds that gold and quartz grow through the generating
influence of certain mineral-water constituents. I’m an
old miner of nearly 40 years’ experience. About quartz
growing, I cannot say ; but I know that gold grows, and
that spontaneously, without the application of mineral
water or anything else. Some 12 or 13 years ago I got a
lot of very handsome specimens from a lode near King-
lake. I carefully chipped off every particle of stone
showing visible gold, and threw the barren refuse into a
shed, lour years ago, I was making up a box of speci-
mens to send to my brother in London, and I rooted out
the stones I had thrown away years before. *On washing
them, I was astonished and delighted te see that they
were specked all over with gold. I take it that this is
proof positive that gold grows.— W. E. Harcourt. Moonee
Ponds, Vic.
Nehemiah Bartley, an old Brisbane (Q.) identity,
who died not very long ago, was sure that “ gold grows.”
He used to treasure a specimen which he had put in a
drawer when there was no trace of gold showing. Coming
across it several years later, he found thin streaks of gold
visible. He noted the circumstance as curious, put the
specimen back, and forgot about the matter till some
years after, when the streaks had “grown” right across
the face of the stone.
But gold doesn’t grow when carried in a man’s
pocket. Can that be because it has been smelted ?

Vic. Supreme Court has ruled that although no
written or verbal contract may exist between seller and
buyer in re discount, if the purchase-money be paid within
the time up to which discounts are allowed by trade,
custom legalises the action of tHe buyer in deducting the
rebate.

A Melb. property - owner : “I have empty
houses in various agents’ hands, each of which has his
special crowd of barrackers. Every week one of the latter
comes to me and tells me to take my property out of the
other agents’ hands because they are d rogues. Every
to-let board I’ve had up I’ve been advised to take down
because no one will trust the agent owning it. lam now
looking for the biggest rogue and most-abused agent I
can find, for it will mean he is the smartest at letting. He
shall have my business, and I’ll take care he won’t cheat
me.”

The story of the money-changers being hunted
from the temple was lately turned t’otherside up by the
services of the Anglican Church being held in the Cool-
gardie den of thieves.
Victoria is developing a class of fruit"
pickers as distinct as the English hop-pickers'
They are a strange jumble of humanity. Lean>
sallow-faced youths, snowy - headed old men,
middle-aged wrecks, and even a few small boys
not yet in long trousers may be seen among them.
They all carry swags ; they all curse one another’s
eyes freely; and, on the whole, they use worse
language than the ordinary wallaby-track swaggy.
When on tramp they always send the smallest
“ kids ” in their ‘ ‘ push ” up to the houses for food.
They know that a woman who will refuse to feed a
man will often soften to a youngster.
One of Premier Kingston’s latest enemies
is old John Warren, M.L., a Tory squatter, who
has been on the stump in the interests of the
National Thistle. Warren is the legislator who
once asked a question in Parliament re the cost of
the Happy Valley waterworks, and when the
Govt, supplied him with the figures, John calcu-
lated that the country was going to the dogs to
the extent of .£lOO,OOO a year through this scheme.
Apparently he worried over the matter, and
couldn’t sleep for indignation at this latest iniquity
of Kingston’s, and he therefore asked a question
in the Council about five yards long. This ques-
tion explained the whole matter. Warren had
made a mistake of £lOO,OOO in his addition, and
when they led him away quietly to a cool spot
and explained that the deficiency was John’s
and John’s only, he collapsed. Eor months
after, when he had to do any calculations, he used
to have them audited by three reliable accountants
“ for greater accuracy. ” But that mistake in old
John’s addition made him Kingston’s enemy for
life.

Letter received by a firm of ironmongers
from a farmer: —“What for do you send me
a man to repare my reaper and binder that
knows nothin about it. the cros peece he put
on broke the furst time i use the mashine and the
seat bar snapt and dam neer killed me if i had i
would have sude your ferm for damages, the Man
told me the mettal he put in was unbrakeable but
it is brittler nor glass, now i have to employ men
cuting the crop with scithes. come and take your
rotten old mashine awa and refund me the iO
pound deposit i paid or i will go to law. do you
think ime going to get tore to peeces and evry
bone in my body broke with your old ramshakle
hurdy-gerdy and pay your man 4 pounds every
time he comes up for repares, if you are wiling to
send your man at once and ficks it proper i will
pay you balans but i want it fixt so it wont brake
again for i can never lern how to ficks a mistery
like that, if you dont do this ile see the lot of you
in hell before ile pay you the balans an su you for
the deposit as well, you may think my langage
ungentelmany but if youd fallen in the mashine
and hadnt a sound spot on your body and tore all
your close in to the bargan i reckon youd be pritty
mad too and had to pay 10 pounds for cuting th ■
crop after all not to speke of the men’s tucker. ”
Barber
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Barber was the sourest man everybody had ever
met. He was a long, thin, lugubrious wretch,
with hollow cheeks, hollow eyes, and a hollow
chest, and an appearance of being the victim of
merciless dyspepsia. But Barber was in excellent
health, and had never had occasion to spend a
copper on “doctors’ stuff ”in all his life. Barber
was fortunate : three parts of his land was the
best in the district, it was bisected by a creek, and
the spring in his well never wholly failed even in
time of fiercest drought. He was a flourishing
settler, and things went well with him, and yet he
always affected to be more miserable than the
legendary “orphan bandicoot on a burnt ridge,”
and wailed eternally to his friends and family
about his awful luck. He preached his belief that
Heaven and Gehenna were leagued against him,
and when anything unfortunate happened,
drought, fire, or flood, it was “just my luck ! ” and
Barber’s utterly woebegone appearance was quite
commensurate with his dreadful fate as the butt
of angels and devils.
One day the settler was running a furrow across
what he called his back paddock. The ground
was hard, and necessitated a good team. Presently
the share struck an immovable object, and a minor
catastrophe followed. Barber had a fall, and sus-
tained superficial injuries to the jaw, and the team
got mixed up with the plough, and broke things
in ' he excitement of the moment.
Three hours later it was known to everyone
within a radius of ten miles that Barber had struck
a rich reef in his back paddock.
Inquiring friends found Barber mounting guard
over his reef and a barrow-load of dazzling speci-
mens.
“ How about your luck now, old party ? ” some-
body asked.
“Dunno, dunno,” replied Barber, miserably.
“Look at that plough o’ mine—mined, simply
ruined! ”

Quitp.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have sold tea on a large and small scale around
Geelong and Mt. Brown. I have originated a
peculiar brand of dysyiepsia from Murray Bridge
to the Towers. My blends are unequalled. And
I know my business. An ordinary man may sell
tea, if he has a decent moustache, a pair of legs,
and can talk lies over the fence. It happens,
however, that in appearance I am but once re-
moved from the ape.
Years ago a man discovered me on the Circular
Quay. He gave me some advice and 10s., because
I was uglier than anything he had ever seen. “ Go
into the country,” he said, “ and sell something.
Tell fortunes, you ugly little beggar.” This man
put bread in my way when he taught me the trick
of fortune-telling.
* * * *
At the end of one hot January day, I dragged
myself along the sun-split road that follows the
Darling into Wilcannia. A woman’s voice crooned
from a tidy little cottage ; there was a smell of
new bread and pastry. I hurried past. Suddenly
her voice filled the air, and the household swarmed
to the gate. “ Here’s Quilp, quick; come and see
Quilp ! ” I turned in the middle of the road and
put on a Cheshire smile. God ! how those kids
howled when I limped off like Richard the Third !
* * * *
There’s a piece of country on the Darling that I
bar at present. I’m living down something I said
there a year ago.
It was getting late when I crawled into
Puncher’s Crossing, with the horse going on three
legs. Several “ cookies ” passed me on the way,
but they had always pulled up for a chat and a
kindly word. Into their ears went my secret, the
secret that fled to every homestead within ten
miles before sundown. Monsieur Pompadour,
the magician, is en route —the magician who
pierces the heart’s depths by glancing at your
little fat hand. Fin Maloney owned the pub.,
store, and newspaper at Puncher’s; and after
being sniffed at by hostile dogs, into Fin’s pub. I
hobbled.
* * * • *
Eight o’clock ! How the time passed. There
were noises in the parlor ; heavy breathings and
suppressed mirth. Good ! I threw myself into a
chair on the verandah. A mop of yellow hair
peered at me from the half-closed door. I main-
tained a commendable silence. Eve spoke.
“ Would you like to come inside ? ”
Business is business, so I went in. There were
fifteen girls there and one big man. He offered
me some tobacco, which was taken as men take
salt in the East.
(To-morrow I would ‘ ‘ plant ” a few hundred-
weight of tea on the strength of this performance.)
“ My gel here heard as you were pretty good at
readin’ fortunes. She’s worried at me till I had
to bring her over. These are my gel’s friends,
mister. ” I bowed. Lord ! how pretty are some
of my countrywomen: peeping brown faces, all
too quickly sullied by the wicked shadows of men !
* » » »
Fortune-telling out-back requires brains. Each
one must have something different, and there are
some awful combinations in 15. In the majority
of cases, you may kill husband the first with per-
fect safety, providing that the void created is
filled. They are touchy anent the old dying rela-
tive, and the. old, old pile. I married the first one
right awai' to 6ft. of gum. “ Would I describe
him ? ” Certainly. He never wore a waistcoat;
summer or winter ; he could sit any horse south
of the line. Didn’t go in for shearing now, but
when he did he was a “ringer.” On Saturday
nights it was his custom to fight about three men.
Tight pants—and big feet. There was a great
clapping of hands, a lot of pushing and laughter.
Everyone recognised him immediately ; the name,
“Jim Saunders,” was whispered through the
room. The girl fled to the verandah to hide her
flaring, happy face. All the others made similar
journeys when the dark, tall gentleman turned
up. (It is always a safe card to keep out short
men : they don’t live up there. Every man is
longer than Adam.)
* * * *
Now, there remained one who had sat quiet
throughout, although she was frequently whis-
pered to. She was partially hidden by a screen of
heavy cloth, but I knew that hers was the sweetest
face in the room. In the midst of the babble I
caught her hand gently—small, white, baby
fingers that did not shrink from mine as others
had. Her face became filled with an intense
eagerness at the beginning. Into her future I
threw those bright phantasms that crowd the
minds of children. A tiny castle was built near
the sea, towering amongst rock, fern, and moun-
tain heather. Then a deadly silence crept over all.
For one brief minute I knelt at her feet. It was
not for me to touch that wondrous hand with my
hideous, twisted lips : let the gabbling tongue put
one iota of pleasure into her fancy, and I would
be happy. And yet I had made a mistake ; it was
in every face. I stood up, still holding her hand.
And she was crying softly, her head down in the
cushions. I limped to the door, a fraud, a failure.
What I had said to that blind child let me not
recall.
ALBA DORION.
Victoria now possesses 1004 registered
medical practitioners, or 44 more than a year ago.
Ordinary household kerosene is an excel-
lent antiseptic for wounds and ulcers. It produces
quick healing, is economical, non-poisonous, and
not so evil-smelling as most antiseptics.
Rev. Zillmann, lecturing in Melb. on the
Coming Revolution :
When the candle of popular education gets in and the
powder-barrel of popular suffrage burns down to the
powder, there will be an explosion which will send
thrones, despots and aristocracies to their individual
nothingness.
Strange words to fall from reverend lips !
It is remarked that the ’Murkan jingo
newspapers, whilst endeavouring to stir up an-
tipathy to Britain over the Venezuela matter;
didn’t appeal to the anti-Saxon sentiment of the
local Irish population. And it is inferred there-
from that the Irish must have waived their ancient
grievance for the moment, like a tempestuous wife
who rows with her husband in the street until an
unwise bystander calls him a brute. “ The Wear-
ing of the Green,” with its “ Ould Ireland never
will get back the blood she has shed,” is a fine
ditty, but very misleading. For every half-pint
of Celtic gore shed in the Home Rule cause, a
hogshead has been freely sacrificed for the benefit
of the dirthy Saxon. The Irish regiments fight
not for their native isle but for “ Great Britain.”
And Fenianism, when it outbreaks upon the ould
sod, is suppressed by English and Scotch regi-
ments.
More voices from Rue Australia wail in a
London paper:—
The New Arcadia, which was to give the signal for a
social revolution to wondering mankind, has dwindled
down to a little cluster of shabby, half-savage, and more
than half-starved settlements in Paraguay, their chief
characteristics being a painful want of clothes and a
perpetual ebullition of quarrels. New Australia still
counts 250 members, but only 85 of these are men, and
the classification of occupations is very curious, including
as it does “one dancing master” and “one stump orator
(professional).” The original community has thrown off
about half a dozen distinct secessions, and is now broken
up into five separate settlements, at an average distance of
about two miles apart from each other. The standard of
living in these communities is sinking steadily in comfort;
the communities themselves, as a matter of fact, are laps-
ing from civilisation into barbarism. Butter is unknown,
matches are forgotten, sugar has become a mere
memory, flour is a medical comfort, a mutton chop is
a lost ideal. For months not only the Pilgrim Fathers
but their wives and children have been soapless. For two
weeks the community has lived on beans; and the
very beans, Mr. Lane confesses with a sigh, were
bought on credit. Money, of course, has disappeared,
and with it have vanished hats, shoes, candles, &,c. The
children are growing up without education, aud the
adults have pretty well dispensed with religion. The
atmosphere of gross Materialism is most deplorable in its
effects. Knowing the inner workings of our several Little
Peddlingtons, I repeat that Communism pure and simple
is imaginary rot. Co-operation without capital in a mixed
crowd, and on the lines we are following, is an utter mis-
take. We are drifting to speedy destruction. Com-
munism destroys industry, for the Communists them-
selves won’t work. The idle man under a Communistic
system is to be paid as well as the industrious man ; the
fool is to be as much honored as the sage. How this
system lowers the productive power of a community is
conclusively shown by New Australia. It consisted of
picked men ; it started with an average capital of £6O per
head ; it is set on fertile soil, and under genial skies. It
is, in brief, “a free body of associated workers,” with
free land, and no “ encumbrances ” in the way of
capitalists or parasites. But the average production
of each worker is less than £lO per head per annum !
There is absolutely no regard for common property. No
one will take the responsibility of checking the children
when destroying the fruit trees. Tools and implements
are lost, mislaid, or destroyed in the most disgraceful
manner. At the Las Ovejas settlement last year the
melon beds were trampled on by adults and children as
if invaded by a herd of wild elephants, and thousands of
melons were broken open—to see if they w-ere ripe—and
not one was allowed to come to maturity. It is nobody’s
business to look after public property. The elective board
of management changes its personality, on an average,
every six weeks. Everybody is jealous of everybody else.
The settlers quarrel furiously over the distribution of
a few grains of sugar or a few handfuls of beans. Any
windy stump orator who happens to come along can turn
them around his thumb. They will reverse their votes
on any subject within a week. No democratic community
can live where the units do not form and exercise their
own opinions. Two months ago 65 to 41 decided on
altering the constitution to single-tax lines. Last week
■ 83 to 23 voted against any change of rules on those lines.
Melbourne people are now quite en rapiport
with the doings of the honorary magistracy.
Evangelist Yatman’s story of the Mel-
bourne pietist who got <£2oo to meet a bill, by
simple prayer, has set a lot of ex-boomers on their
knees again. Previously, when they wanted =£2oo
or more, they simply preyed, and they got it.
The Bulletin even has faith in the preyer.
The financial ruin of the Cubans in the last
revolt threw nearly all the property in the island
into the hands of native-born Spaniards, and, as
the latter have now to bear the whole burden
of taxation, they are beginning to hope for the
success of the insurgents. “ Thus doth the whirli-
gig of Time bring his revenges ! ”

Ca ira ! It shall go !
Fatman has too long held sway—
Heartless human beast of prey !
Fate brings round the Judgment Day,
Day of wrath and woe.
Earth is full of groans and sighs,
Wailings of distress,
Curses, too ; his ears and eyes
Fatman closes ; crassly wise
Thinks they are but empty cries
In the wilderness.
Whom the gods would slay they drive
Mad, of old was said.
Fatman’s mad and doomed to dive
Deep, to where his victims strive,
Struggling hard for bread.
Yet all in vain
Will be the gain,
Ev’n if his blood be shed.
Other Fatmen will arrive—
Drones within the human hive—
And the race will keep alive
Till the devil’s dead.
The Last of the Bohemians.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
A tall, shambling, loosely-built man with long,
thick, wavy hair down over the collar of his coat,
which fits him anyhow.
His mane - that’s the word—after being torn
into a “ parting ” on the right side, is swept across
the head down over the left brow and behind the
long, narrow ears.
Bright brown eves in a worn face wearing a
long, inverted-M-shaped beard. The hair is very
dark—without artifice—considering he is well over
eighty, and by many suspected of being a hundred.
He usually sits in the dignified grandeur of a
solemn silence in London’s one genuine Bohemian
Club.
A strikingly picturesque figui'e, and an enor-
mously interesting personality. Keen and quick
as a machine-needle in repartee, and gifted with
an excessively droll and tropically dry humor.
He is said to be one of the cleverest raconteurs
in London ; and his delivery of Marcus Clarke’s
mock-heroic “Bill Jinks” would convulse a stack
of church elders.
No one knows where he lives, or how.
A sporting member made a wager he would
track the hoary hare to his “ form.”
The H.H. left the club well into the early hours
of a summer’s morning, and the S.M. followed.
Hour by hour he was led in a slow, sauntering,
“ home-was-never-like-this ” manner, up street,
through square and circus, and down alley ; along
puzzling passage and tortuous tunnel; out of the
city of Clubland to the suburbs of Snoring Sleep,
and back again ; he helped the moon to bed at
St. John’s Wood, and the sun to rise at the Isle of
Dogs. At last, when the stars sank back in the sky,
wearied with watching the monotonous meander-
ings, when the pursuer’s knees were kissing like
castanets, and he was too exhausted to be any
longer cautious in the chase, his queer quarry
turned suddenly, fresh as the lark’s voice above
them that greeted the dawn : “ Ah ! old fellow,
how are you; out for an early stroll?” And
while the stupefied S.M. stammered incoherently,
lie added, “I’m just off to Margate for a bath I
Good morning.”
London, Jan., ’96.

Enclosed please find 7s. 6d. in stamps, for which please forward by
return of post one of your Watches and Chains, as I want it for one of
my boys. Trusting you will favor by return of post,
I remain, yours faithfully, W. H. COCHRANE.

‘‘Mr. Nathan Herman.
‘‘Sir, —I received my bale, and I was very much surprised at the
contents. I am sure I could not get the same materials up here for
double the money. Allowing you to make any use you like with this
note, I remain, respectfully yours, ROSE HOPKINS.”
_ West Hillgrove, January 24, 1895.
NATHAN HERMAN, Esq.
Dear Sir—l am just in receipt of your favor of 22nd instant, and
the Watch, for which accept my best thanks. I think I will be able to
give you an order for one or two more shortly. My own is still keeping
splendid time. By all means publish my letter. I think the general
public should know what good value you are giving for the money. I
am well-known here and in Armidale and Melbourne.—Believe me,
yours sincerely, JOHN M‘BEAN, Senr.
Salisbury Villa, Peel and Piper Streets, Bathurst, Nov. 13, 1895.
Dear Sir, —Yours of the 11th inst. to hand, and am much pleased
in replying to it. For two years I have been purchasing teas from you,
and they have given me and all visitors the utmost satisfaction and are
superior to tea that before I had given 2s. a lb. for in Bathurst, and
you are quite at liberty to make use of this as a testimonial. —Yours
truly, SAMUEL STOW.
NATHAM HERMAN, Esq., 9 Wynyard-street, Sydney.
These proofs of what can be got at the MISFIT PARLOR are
positively genuine, as some of the know-alls may shake their heads
and perhaps doubt it. For the information of those who perhaps may
not be aware of the fact, I assert that no newspaper will publish any
letter without the Original Copy, and I am willing to hand over J 2500
to local charities if anyone can refute the veracity of such testimonials.
